After I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is the last of the science of medicine with its completion, and I have reached the end of it, from its expositions and its explanations, it seemed to me that I should complete it for you with this treatise which is a part of the operation, that is, surgery with the hand.
Because manual operation has been neglected in our region and in our time, completely deprived: until perhaps its science perished, and its trace was cut off: and there remained of it only small descriptions in the books of the ancients, which hands have changed: and error and hesitation happened to them, until its intentions were closed, and its force was distanced: It seemed to me that I should revive it with the arrangement of this Treatise in it according to the way of position, and explanation, and abbreviation: and that I should come with the forms of the instruments of the Cautery, and the other instruments of the work: as it is done from the addition of explanation, and from the speeding up of what is necessary.
The reason why a good craftsman of it with his hand is not found in our time is: because the art of medicine is long, and its author must be exercised in it before that in the science of anatomy, which Galen narrated, until he knows the aids of the members, and their forms, and their conjunction, and separation, and has knowledge of the bones, and nerves, and muscles, and their number, and their egress, and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their exit.
And because of that Hippocrates said that name with name is much, and with work is little, and especially in the art of the hand.
We indeed already said about that in the introduction of this book.
Because he who is not knowing what we said about anatomy, is not emptied, but indeed falls into error, by which men are killed: just as I saw many of those who are formed in this science, and boast about it without knowledge, and without experience.
That is, because I saw a foolish physician cut a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he cut certain arteries in the neck: wherefore the woman's blood flowed until she fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who presumed to extract a stone from a man, who had advanced in age, and the stone was large. Therefore he arrived at it, and extracted it with a piece of the bladder. Wherefore the man died on the third day.
And I indeed had already been called to extract it: and I saw from the size of the stone, and the disposition of the sick man, that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a certain Duke of our land on account of medicine: and it happened to a black eunuch, who was with him, a fracture in his leg near the heel: and the physician hastened with his ignorance, and bound the fracture above the wound with cushions and splints with a strong binding, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
Then he completed him according to his desires. Afterwards he dismissed him for several days, and ordered him not to loosen the ligature: and it remained thus until his leg and his foot became suppurated, and it came to destruction.
Therefore I was called to him: and I hastened to loosen the ligature: and calm followed, and he escaped from his pains.
Nevertheless, the corruption was already confirmed in the limb: and I could not restrain it.
Therefore the corruption ceased to spread in the limb, until it perished.
And I saw another physician, who perforated a cancerous abscess: and it ulcerated afterwards, until its malignancy was magnified with its master.
That is, because when a cancer is pure from melancholic humor, then it is not necessary to reach it with iron at all, unless it is in a limb that can tolerate having the whole of it cut off.
And because of that, O my son, it is necessary for you to know that manual operation is divided into two divisions: namely, operation with which is associated safety: and operation with which there is danger in many situations.
And I have already pointed out in every place in this book where an operation comes which involves terror and fear.
Therefore it is necessary for you to avoid that, and to leave it alone, so that fools may not find a way to speak and to bring dishonor.
Therefore take measures with care and caution, and treat your patients with ease and firmness, and use the better way leading by Godâ€™s grace to salvation and laudable success: and leave terrible illnesses of difficult healing: and remove your minds from that which makes you fear, lest hesitation enter into your faith and your well-being.
For it is more conducive to your glory, and more sublime in this world, and ultimately to your rewards.
For Galen already said in some of his admonitions. Do not treat a bad illness: lest you be called bad physicians.
Now however I have divided this treatise according to three chapters.
Chapter one.
Concerning cautery with fire, and cautery with a sharp medicine, chaptered and ordered from head to foot, and the forms of instruments and tools of cautery: and everything which is necessary in the operation.
Chapter two.
Concerning incision and perforation, phlebotomy and cupping, and wounds: and the extraction of arrows, and similar things: and the whole chaptered, ordered with forms of instruments.
Chapter three.
Concerning restoration, and dislocation, and the cure of torsion, and similar things: chaptered, ordered from head to foot, and with forms of instruments.
But before I recall the operation with it, it is necessary that I speak of the quality of its benefits and harms: and in which complexion it should be exhibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse concerning the quality of the benefit of cautery, and its harm, is a lengthy and subtle discourse, a science, and a hidden secret.
Many wise men have also spoken on it, and they disagreed: and for me, indeed, a little from their discourses suffices, fearing prolixity.
Therefore I say that cautery universally benefits every complexion which is with matter, and without matter: except for two complexions, which are the hot complexion without matter, and the dry complexion without matter.
However, in the hot and dry complexion with matter they disagreed. For some have said that cautery is beneficial in it. And others said the contrary of that, namely that cautery is not suitable in the illness which is from heat and dryness: because the nature of fire is heat: and it is unsuitable that a hot and dry illness be healed with something hot and dry.
And he who says the contrary said: that through cautery help is given in every hot and dry illness which happens in the bodies of men: because when you compare the body of man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I indeed say through his speech: because experiment has already revealed that to me several times.
Nevertheless it is not fitting that he come to that, except he who is already proven and practiced in the chapter of cautery by final exercise, and has known the diversity of the complexions of men, and the disposition of illnesses in themselves, and their causes, and their accidents, and the duration of time of them.
From the other complexions however there is no fear over you: and especially in cold and moist illnesses. For already all physicians have agreed upon them: and they did not disagree in helping with cautery in them.
And know, sons, that from the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its goodness over cautery with burning medicine is: because fire is simple, whose action does not arrive except to the member which is cauterized: and it does not harm another member joined with it, except with little harm.
Truly the action of burning medicine arrives at that which is distant from the members: and perhaps it causes to happen in the member an illness of difficult healing, and perhaps it kills.
Fire however, because of its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, does not do that, unless it overflows.
Now indeed that has become known to us through experiment, because of the length of service and study with the art, and standing upon the truth of things: and because of that I excuse myself from length of speech.
And if it were not that it is not permitted to this my book, I would bring to you a hidden secret in fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies of its expulsion, by which it expels illnesses with a philosophical demonstrative speech, which would be sharpened by your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree in the time in which it is fitting to perform cautery, and they place the better time in the spring.
And I say that cautery is fitting at every time: therefore, because harm happening because of time is submerged in the help which is drawn with cautery. And especially if cautery is because of pains from necessary, strong, urgent pains, which do not tolerate slowness: because it is feared from them that a greater harm may happen than a small harm entering because of time.
And let not that fall into your minds, O sons, which only the common people and foolish physicians do not fear, namely that that illness which is healed by cautery does not always return: and they place that as a consequence, and the matter is not as they think: because cautery is nothing but a medicine in place of that which alters the complexion, and dries the humidities which are the causes wherefore pains happen.
Nevertheless, cautery surpasses medicine by the speed of its healthfulness, and the strength of its operation, and the vehemence of its dominion: and it is possible that the illness may return at some hour of time according to the complexion of the infirm, and the location of his illness, and its strength: and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in it: and that which comes to him in its acquisition from foods, and similar things from causes, unless the illness in which cautery is administered happens.
An illness subtle in a member of few superfluities and humidities, such as cautery of a tooth because of pain, and things similar to it, for it is possible that that pain may not return in it. But that happens in few cases.
But truly the talk of the common people again, that cautery is the last of medicine, is right talk: not to that which they intend in it: because they believe that there is no cure which compares with medicine, nor with anything else after the setting of cautery.
The matter, however, is contrary to that, and the intention that cautery is the last of medicine is not, except that when we administer methods of cure in some illnesses, and those medicines do not help: then we administer cautery at the end of the matter, and it helps: then thence it falls out that cauterium is the last of medicine, not according to the intention which the common people and many foolish physicians understand.
The ancients, however, said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron: and they did not say that except because of the evenness of gold, and the excellence of its substance: and they said that the place of cautery does not produce pus. That is not true absolutely: because I have now proved it, and found that it does not do that in some bodies without others.
And cautery with it is good, and better than with iron, as they said. However, when you heat a cautery in the fire made of gold, it is not clear to you when it is heated to the amount you want, because of the redness of the gold: and when it approaches coldness, and if you add more heating to it, it melts in the fire, and turns into sheets.
Therefore the craftsman falls from it into difficulty: and because of that, cautery with iron is quicker and closer to us, in straightforwardness of operation.
And I indeed have now arranged this Chapter on Cautery, according to chapters: and I have arranged them from head to foot: so that they may ease one seeking what he wants from it.
Chapter II.
This cauterization contributes to the control of moisture and coldness over the brain: which two are causes of headache, and a multitude of the flow of humors from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and a multitude of sleep, and toothache, and sore throat: and universally every illness which happens from coldness, such as paralysis, and headache, epilepsy, and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The method of cauterization is that you should instruct the patient: that he be evacuated first with a cleansing purgative medicine for the head for three nights, or four, according to what the strength of the patient, and his age, and his habit require.
Then instruct him to shave his head with a razor. Then make him sit between your hands squarely, so that he has now placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the root of your palm on the root of his nose between his eyes. Wherever therefore your middle finger reaches, mark that place with ink.
Then heat the olive cautery; its form is this.
Then place it on the place marked with ink, descending, so that your hand is gradually tightened, and you will turn it. Then quickly lift your hand and look at the place.
If you see now revealed from the bone the amount of the head of alkiel, or the amount of zeuherbi, then lift your hand. And if not, repeat with your hand the same iron, or with another if it cools, until you see the bones which I told you about.
Then take some salt and dissolve it in water, and soak the cautery in it, and place it on the place: and leave it for three days.
Then place on it the cautery soaked in butter, and leave it on the place until the fire eschar is destroyed. Then treat it with tetrapharmacon ointment until it is healed.
Now they have said that the more a wound remains open, the more pus it emits: wherefore it is better and more helpful.
And some of them said that the skin should be cauterized to the bone: and the cautery should be held until something of the thickness of bone is burnt.
Then scrape away what has been burnt of the bone, then treat it.
And others say that it is necessary to reach the ultimate with the cautery, until it makes a strong impression on the bone, so that the bone falls, which is done like the form of kirate: or the instrument which women put in the infusion of small writing: and they think that vapors of the head are exhaled from this place, and the wound is left open for a long time.
Then treat it until it is consolidated.
But to me these two types of cautery do not seem at all suitable except in certain men according to the way of fear.
And to leave it, in my opinion, is better with its safety.
For the head is weakened by the solution of its natural continuity: as we have testified in the other members: and especially when the head of the weak person is naturally weak: and indeed the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it: therefore work with it safely.
Chapter III.
When chronic pain occurs in the whole head: and it is prolonged in the patient, and he uses bleedings, and cuppings, and head purges, and oils, and cataplasms. And especially if it has already been cauterized with one cautery, which we have described, and none of those things benefit him: then consider. For if the patient's head is of strong natural constitution, and is not weak, and finds intense cold: then cauterize with another cautery a little below that one. Then cauterize it above every horn of his head with a cautery, until the skin is pierced, and a quantity of bone, which we have described, is uncovered. And cauterize it with one cautery in the occiput of his head, in the place which is called the vessel: and it is the place where a cupping glass is placed: and raise your instrument in this, and do not uncover the bone. For the patient finds because of that a vehement pain, and different from the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head.
And I shall call this cauterization in its place: and it is necessary that it be a cauterization, by which the horns of the head are cauterized, and its occiput with a more delicate cautery, than that by which the middle of the head is cauterized.
Chapter IV.
When pain and swelling occur in a part of the head, or the pain extends to the eye, and the head is evacuated with purifying medicines, and the remaining treatment which I described in the divisions of illnesses is administered, and it does not help at all: then cauterization is indicated in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine: or with iron.
Now, cauterization with a sharp burning medicine is as follows: take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut off its ends from both sides. Then incise the place of pain at the time with a wide phlebotomy knife, until a space is made under the skin, into which the clove is placed.
Then insert it under the skin, until it is hidden: then bind over it with a suitable compress, and leave it for fifteen hours, then unbind it, and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days: afterwards, apply a cloth soaked in butter to it, until the place produces pus: then treat it with ointment until it heals.
And if you wish, do it with certain burning medicines, which I wrote about in the twelfth treatise, On Burning Medicines.
Cauterization with iron, however, is done in this way. A fire cautery, whose form is this.
And it is called a nail because the shape of its head is like the shape of a nail, in which there is a certain curve, and in the middle of it there is a small protrusion.
Then place it on the spot and hold your hand; and you slowly revolve the iron quickly; and let the amount that is burned be of the thickness of the skin, as much as half of it. And raise your hand, so that the artery which is below is not burned, and a flow of blood does not occur.
Then soak a piece of cloth in salt water, and place it on the spot, and leave it for three days; then place a piece of cloth with butter; afterwards treat with ointment until it heals.
And if you wish, cauterize this hemicrania with the second knife-like extremity of the cautery. And beware of cutting the artery in this hemicrania, not properly an old one.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in an old hemicrania.
When you treat a hemicrania, with what we said from the preceding treatment, and what we said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment does not help: and you see from the illness that what we said from the first cautery with medicine, or with cautery with fire, is not sufficient for it, then it is necessary that you heat the knife-like cautery until it becomes white after you mark on the place of pain with a line, whose length is half a finger, or about that: and put down your hand at once, and press it until you cut the artery, and reach to the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to beware of the continuation of the jaw, which moves in mastication, lest the side, or the nerve which moves it, be burned, whence a spasm may result.
And be watchful and cautious about the flow of blood from the artery which has been cut. For in its excision there is danger, and especially to him who is ignorant of what he does, and is not studious, nor experienced: and he leaves the operation with that in the first place.
And I will come with a recollection of the regime of the flow of blood happening from an artery according to its manner, and in its place in this book.
But if you see from the sickness that this cautery is not sufficient for him, and you see the body of the sick person tolerating cautery in the middle of the head, as we have narrated, treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we mentioned in the chapter on the extraction of the artery with a cautery having two knives. For cauterization is better than this and more beneficial.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in earache.
When pain occurs in the ear from cold, and it is treated with resolvents and the other treatment which we mentioned in the division, and the pain is not removed, then cauterize with fire: and it is that which is called a point, whose form is this.
Then make points with it after its ignition around the whole ear, as it is rolled around; or around each, if the pain is in both at the same time. And move the cautery away from the root of the ear a little, after you have marked the places with a hot iron: and let the quantity of cautery be ten points in each ear, and about that.
Then treat the places until they are healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in torticollis.
Chapter VII.
Torticollis which is cured with cauterization is only from the types which happen from phlegm, according to what we said in the divisions of illnesses.
And avoid cauterization of the type which happens from dryness and spasm of the nerve.
When you treat this type of torticollis with bleedings, and purgatives, and gargles, and your treatment does not help, then it is necessary that you cauterize the afflicted person with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, and a second a little lower than its temple, and a third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part. Because softening does not occur except in the part which appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize it with a cautery directly at the end of the upper ear, a little below the horn of the head: and another in the temple; whose length should be according to the length of a thumb. And place your hand with the cautery until half the thickness of the skin is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a kind of knife, whose form has been described above: however, it is not a little thinner than that, as you see: and it is necessary that there be a little superfluous thickness in it.
Then treat the place with what preceded until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in old apoplexy.
When apoplexy becomes chronic, and you treat it with what we have said, and your treatment does not help, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries on each horn of his head, and a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said, and a cautery in the occiput of the head, according to what preceded: and the method of cauterization should be according to what preceded.
Also, cauterization with a cautery over the stomach is done; and this is more extreme.
Then let him be treated with what preceded.
Chapter IX.
It is necessary that in the beginning large doses of hiera and pills purifying the brain be given to the sick person.
Then shave the patient's entire head, and apply to the back of his head the mustard plaster described in the treatise on plasters; and apply it several times.
For this is the method of cauterization: and do it according to the same order which I there described.
Therefore if he is healed by it, it is good. If not, then make for him three cauterizations in the posterior part of his head, which should descend from the upper part of the head to the lower part of the eye; and place between each cauterization the width of a finger.
Then treat the cauterization with what preceded.
But if you wish an addition, and the patient is able to tolerate it, then make for him a cauterization in the middle. But if you wish an addition, then cauterize it above the two horns.
Then treat it until it is healed, and let the cauterization be olive-shaped according to the form which was mentioned before.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in paralysis, and softening.
Chapter X.
It is necessary that you proceed in the cleansing of the head with syringes, and with that which we have said.
Then shave the head of the patient; afterwards cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery upon each horn of the head, and with a cautery upon the back of it, and with three upon the vertebrae of the neck.
But if you need in the illness a softening of the body with more of those things, and the patient is tolerant, and the illness is severe: then cauterize him with four cauteries upon the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery, until a great deal of his skin is burned, and raise your hand. Then treat him until he is healed: and thus cauterize with olive oil.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized, except one whose epilepsy is from phlegm.
Therefore it is necessary that you first cleanse his brain with leeches and with the other treatment that we have mentioned in the division: when the patient is large, or is tolerant in receiving medicines.
But if he is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then a gargle and masticatories are administered, cleansing the brain, before that for many days with improvement of diet.
Then shave his head, afterwards cauterize it with one cautery in the middle of the head, according to what preceded in the manner: and with another cautery in the occiput, and above each horn of his head with a cautery. But if the patient is strong and is tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I mentioned in the one having paralysis, and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back: and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the manner which preceded.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small cautery, according to this method.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in melancholia.
When the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize him with the cauteries which we mentioned in the case of one having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is an excess inclined to melancholia, and the body of the patient is moist, then give him something to drink which will cleanse his brain, according to what preceded in the division; then shave the head of the patient, and afterwards make a properly made bandage of linen, like a circle.
Then place it in the middle of the head; and the patient sitting squarely should be held on all sides.
Then present the liniment. And it is more worthy from old sheep butter. And heat it over a fire with a moderate measured heat, which a finger can tolerate when put into it. Then empty it in the middle of his head in a circle, and leave it until it cools.
Do this for the patient once every week, with the rest of his good regimen, until he is healed.
Or if you wish, cauterize it making very small points of it, except that you hold your hand with the cautery: rather, let it be by smelling.
For this kind of cautery moistens the brain with a quality.
Then place on it a pledget soaked in butter, or chicken fat.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a descending flow to the eye.
Chapter XIII.
When the beginning of water in the eye is declared to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and purge the patient with that which cleanses his head.
And keep him from all dampness, and make him sit in a bath, fasting for several days.
Then order him to shave his head, and cauterize him in the middle of his head. Then cauterize him on the two temples with two cauteries, if the beginning of the descent of water is in both eyes simultaneously; or from one side, if the beginning of water is in one eye: and cut off with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin: and let the cauteries be long in the breadth of the temples: and beware of the flow of blood.
If you see anything of it, then cut it off immediately with whatever medicine is possible: and I will come with wisdom, which is in the extraction of arteries, and the cutting off of them, and caution from the flow of blood.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck, under the two bones, with two cauteries ultimately produced.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in old tears.
When the tears of the eyes are old, persistent, and are upon the veins and arteries which are in the exterior of the head manifestly, it is verified that this is from cold, phlegmatic superfluities: then cauterize with the same cautery that I narrated in the beginning of the descending water, with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries upon the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck under the two bones. And if it needs addition, then cauterize it on every side cautiously of the eye, above the end of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the fetor of the nose.
When you treat it with what we said in the division, and the medication does not help: then hasten, and give the patient  infirmocochias to drink for three nights; then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. Then cauterize it with a nail-shaped cautery, with two cauteries, above the two eyebrows under the hair a little.
And beware of the artery, lest you cut it.
And this is the shape of the nail-shaped cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the softening of the eyelid.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or moisture, then cauterize the eyelid with one cautery, with this lunar cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize it above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in each part; and let them be distant from the two temples; and let the length of each cautery be according to the length of the eyebrow; and do not move your hand with the cautery, but according to the quantity which burns a third of the eyelid.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the eyelid when the hairs are inverted, pricking the eye.
Cauterization in it is according to two methods: either cauterization with fire, or cauterization with a burning medicine.
In cauterization with fire, however, instruct the patient beforehand to remove his hairs, if he is one of those who clean them, until they are lengthened and made even. But if they prick him at their root, then bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow.
When therefore they grow and are even, then place the patient's head in your bosom; then mark above the eyelid of his eyes with a cautery, a mark which is according to the shape of a myrtle leaf; and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
Then place a pledget soaked in egg white or in psyllium mucilage over the eye.
Then heat the cautery, whose shape is this.
Then cauterize over the shape which you marked gradually in many times, until the surface of the skin, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, is entirely burned properly.
And the sign of the health of your work is: that if you see the eyelid of the eye now contract, and the hairs rise up at the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days.
Then place a pledget with butter on it, until the eschar is removed. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if some of the hairs return after a while, and the eyelid softens, then repeat the cauterization on that place, as you did at first.
But if the hairs are below, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is level so that the hairs do not prick the eye.
It is that you should instruct the patient to let the hairs grow until they are long and even. Make from paper or cloth the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Then take of known soap and of unslaked lime, one ounce of each or thereabouts; then rub them together well and quickly, so that it does not grow cold. Then spread some of it on the paper which you made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place it on one or both eyelids. And place under the eye a piece of linen soaked in egg white; and let the patient's head be in your lap.
And place your index finger on the medicine, and press it gently. And you should move it whenever the patient feels the medicine biting; for its bite is found to be like fire.
While therefore the biting which he feels persists, then remove the medicine and move it with your finger. When therefore the biting ceases, then remove the medicine, and wash the eye with water.
And consider then, if you see the eyelid raised, as it is raised by burning or by incision, it is well: and if not, then apply more of the medicine to the place where the medicine does not take hold and does not blacken, until your work is even, and the eye is contracted.
Then place a cotton compress with butter on it until the burnt skin is removed. Then treat it with diaphnincon ointment, or another, until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you during the operation to observe the utmost care, lest anything of the medicine fall into the eye.
But if the eyelid softens afterwards, and something from the hairs fights in the eye, then repeat the medicine on that place which softens from the eyelid itself, as you did at first. Then treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of a myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified in size and smallness. Therefore, let your consideration be according to that; and the way of rightness is not hidden from him who studies this art.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a lachrymal fistula.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and your treatment does not help, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this method.
Instruct the patient to place his head in your bosom, and let a servant hold his head between his hands in such a way that it is not moved or shaken.
Then place a cotton pledget soaked in egg white or in psyllium mucilage on his eye; then heat the cautery, whose shape is this.
Let it be concave, like the shape of a vulture's quill from one end, with which the cautery is made; and if you wish it to be penetrating to the other end, and if you wish it to be upward, try it thus.
However, this concave shape is better for your work.
Then press the fistula, if it is open, and draw out the pus from it, and dry it; and if it is not open, then open it, and draw out its pus.
Then place the cautery, made very hot, upon it.
And hold your hand with it a little near the cautery from the eye, toward the side of the nose, so that your hand does not err, or the sick person be disturbed; and place the cautery on the fat of the eye, and destroy it.
If therefore you reach with the beginning of your cautery as far as the bone, it will be well. And if not, then repeat the cautery once, or twice, if you need it; and leave it for three days.
Then apply a pledget with butter, and treat it with a drying ointment, until it is healed.
But if forty days have passed, and it is not healed, then apply a sharp corrosive medicine to it, until the bone is exposed; and scrape it according to what comes in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which certain early authorities mentioned. Apply to the place of the fistula, and incise it; then place in the incision a thin tube, that is, a thin cannula, of which this is the form. And pour into it the quantity of a drachm of molten lead, and hold your hand with good retention of the cannula, and let the patient not move at all, so that the molten lead does not run, or reach his eye.
And it is necessary that you place over the patient's eye a compress soaked in egg white, or in water.
For the lead burns the place of the fistula, and heals it with a marvelous healing.
If the fistula is healed with what we have said, by cautery and treatment, it is good. And if not, then it is necessary to administer to the opening of the nose, and to reduce the fistula to the nasal passage, according to that which will come to its own proper place.
Concerning the method of cauterizing fissures of the lips.
Very often fissures occur on the lip, which are called chaps, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, when you treat these fissures, with what we have said in the division of illnesses, and the treatment does not help, then cauterize with a small, knife-like cautery iron, according to this form.
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then quickly place the heated iron in the fissures themselves, until the cautery reaches the depth of the fissures.
Then treat it with cerate until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a fistula of the mouth.
When an abscess occurs in the root of the gum, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth: then it produces pus, and breaks; afterwards it becomes chronic, and pus runs, and a fistula forms: then you treat it, and the treatment is not effective, then it is necessary that you apply a cautery according to the size that it occupies in the fistula. Then insert the heated iron into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and its end; do this once or twice.
Then treat it afterwards with what we have said concerning treatment until it is cured.
If then the pus is cut away, and it heals, it will be good. And if not, then it is necessary to expose the place, and remove the corrupt bone according to what will come into its head.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the gums and softened teeth.
When the gum is softened because of humors, and the teeth are loosened, and you treat it with medicines, and it does not help: then place the patient's head in your lap.
Then heat a cautery, whose shape will come after these things, so that you place a cannula over the tooth: and quickly insert the heated cautery into it: and hold your hand a little while, until the patient feels the heat of the fire has already reached the root of the tooth.
And raise your hand; then repeat the cautery several times, as you wish.
Then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water, and hold it for one hour: and spit it out.
For the loosened tooth is made firm, and the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt humor is dried.
Concerning the method of cauterizing for toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or a worm in the tooth, and treatment with medicines is not helpful: then cauterization is indicated in two ways: either cauterization with butter, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with butter is done as follows: take cow's butter and melt it in an iron spoon or in a pan. Then take a tent and wrap it around the tip of a probe. Then dip it in the melted butter and quickly place it on the aching tooth, and hold it until it cools; then repeat this several times until the effect of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or a tent into cold butter, and place it on the aching tooth; and place a heated iron on top of it until the heat penetrates to the depth of the tooth's cavity.
Cauterization with fire is done by using a bronze or iron tube. And let there be some thickness to it so that the sharpness of the fire does not reach the patient's bone.
Then heat the cautery to the appropriate shape and place it on that tooth and hold your hand until the cautery cools.
Do this several times, for the pain will be removed either on that same day or the following day.
And it is necessary in the impression of that cautery that the patient fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour; then he should spit it out.
And this is the shape of the cautery.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, as is possible; and this is the shape of the cannula.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in strumae.
When strumae are from phlegm and from cold humors, and are not obedient to digestion through medicines, and you wish to digest them quickly: then a concave cautery of fire, whose shape is this.
Having two open ends, so that smoke may exit from the cautery at the other end: and place it, ignited, upon the abscess once, and a second time, if you need it, until it reaches the depth of the abscess.
But if the abscess is small, then place the cautery according to the size of the abscess.
Then leave it for three days, and place upon it lint infused in butter, until that which the fire burned is removed.
Then treat it with tooth unguent until the fistula is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
When humors overcome the lung canal, and especially when it is with coldness of complexion, then it is necessary that the patient be evacuated first with solutive medicines.
Then cauterize it in the uvula at the root of the throat in a depressed place: and beware lest you reach the throat with the cautery, nor the skin, except a little of it.
Then cauterize it with another cautery at the juncture of the neck in the last vertebra of it, finishing: and let the cautery be nail-shaped according to the manner which preceded.
Then treat it with what preceded, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in the illnesses of the lung and cough.
When cough and illness of the lung are from cold humors, and the infirm one does not have a fever, nor consumption, and the illness is old: then cauterize him with two cauteries above the clavicle in places depressed, soft, empty. And with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples: and let the cautery be of a tube according to the form which preceded.
And if you wish your cauterization to be by puncturing with a cautery, which is named Punctum, whose form preceded in the puncturing of earache: and let the points be from thirty points up to almost others.
Then treat him with what preceded, until he is healed.
And now let the cautery have three branches: according to this form. Therefore the cauterization is alleviated with it, when you do with it at one time three cauterizations.
Chapter XXVII.
When the head of the humerus is dislocated by reason of humors, and is not fixed in the hour of its return from its dislocation, until that is accustomed to return, then it is dislocated by a small movement, which happens as we have already testified, then it is necessary that you reduce the dislocation in the first instance. Then the patient is laid on his back, or on his healthy side. Then lift the skin which is in the inner armpit upwards with the fingers of your left hand: and if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then have a cautery with two prongs. Whose form is this.
Then cauterize the skin until it penetrates it to the other side, and the figure of the cauterization will be with four cauteries.
And sometimes the cauterization is done with a cautery having three prongs, wherefore the figure of the cauterization then is of six cauteries: and the prongs are according to the thinness of a probe.
And this is the form of a cautery having three prongs.
And sometimes one is added in this way, wherefore there are eight cauteries.
Then place crushed leeks with salt on the cautery. Then make the patient adhere to stillness, so that the limb is not moved for some time, until it is strengthened.
And if the dislocation is towards the upper parts, and this rarely happens, then cauterize it above the shoulder with a clavicle cautery, or with many cauteries by puncturing.
For the joint is strengthened, and the moisture is removed: and the patient is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the stomach.
When coldness and much moisture occur in the stomach, until they draw it away from its complexion, and catarrhs are multiplied in it, and the methods of treatment are continuous, and do not help: then it is necessary that the patient be laid on his back, and extend his legs and his hands. Then cauterize with three cauteries, a cautery under the breastbone the size of one finger with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauteries below on the two sides of one cautery, until the figure of the cauteries becomes triangular. And space them out so that they do not join together when they produce pus.
And let the depth of the cautery be two-thirds the thickness of the skin.
And let the figure of the cauteries be according to this form without addition: and if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form: and let the cautery with which this cautery is made be one according to the figure of a circle according to this form.
And the stomach is cauterized by puncturing it for him who is frightened by this cautery: and it is, that you mark on the stomach a point according to the quantity which you wish with a cautery: then cauterize it with a pointed cautery: then treat it with the treatment which preceded until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing a cold liver.
When pain occurs in the liver from coldness and dampness, or from gross flatulence, until superfluous matter departs from its natural complexion, and the sick man is treated with what we have said, and it does not benefit him: then it is necessary that the sick man be laid on his back, and you mark with a cautery three cauteries according to this figure, and this same quantity on the lower liver from the hypochondria, where the elbow of a man reaches: and let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger: and let the cautery be according to the length of a straight body: and do not press your hand with the cautery very much.
And let the quantity of what is burned be from the thickness of the skin, a quantity of half of it not added: and let the sick man stand on his feet.
But if he is not standing, then let him be lying down, who now extends his legs and raises his arms.
This is the form of the cautery.
And it is indeed possible for you to cauterize these cauteries with a knife cautery, when you have skill and prudence in the art.
And be careful not to proceed excessively in cauterization, and burn the whole thickness of the skin, therefore burn the whole body, and reach the intestines: because the skin there is thin, therefore know that.
Concerning the method of cauterizing abscesses of the liver.
When an exit occurs, and you wish to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver, or in its tunic: then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the sick person experiences pain and heaviness without sharpness: and if it is in the tunic of the liver, there is intense sharp pain: and you see now, that its treatment has fatigued the physicians.
Therefore, the sick person should lie on his back. Then mark the location of the abscess with a cautery iron; then heat a cautery iron that resembles a ray. And this is its form. And cauterize with it one cautery until the whole skin is burned and it reaches the fat, until all the pus comes out: then treat it with the treatment for wounds, until it is healed.
And it is not necessary to use this kind of cautery, except by one whose study in the art of medicine is prolonged, and through whose hands these illnesses concur with experience many times: then indeed let him dare to perform this operation.
But truly, to leave it alone is better in my opinion.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in pleurisy.
The ancients said, cautery with the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy in this manner. And it is that you should take of the dried roots of birthwort, one longer root which is found among them, and let it be like the thickness of a finger. Then immerse it in oil and ignite it in the fire. Then cauterize with one cautery in that which is between the continuation of the clavicle with the neck, and with two small cauteries somewhat sloping downwards to the part which is under the chin.
And with two cauteries also in that which is between the fifth and sixth rib, somewhat sloping towards the back: and with another cautery in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. And with three cauteries from the back: one in that which is between the two shoulder blades; and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, lower than the cautery which is between the two shoulder blades.
And it is not necessary that you press your hand with the cautery, rather let it be evident on the skin by smelling.
And now some ancients have said that there are men who use a cautery of iron similar to a ray: and they ignite it, and internally they insert it into that which is between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess with it, and pus comes out: as we said in the abscess of the liver.
But in this perforation with a cautery there is fear that either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula occurs in the place, for which there is no healing.
Chapter XXXII. On the method of cauterizing the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen, with that which we have said of treatment in the division of illnesses, and your treatment does not help, then cautery in it is according to three methods, which are all correct.
One of which is, that you make three cauteries, or four, in a line according to the length of the spleen, according to the figure of the cauteries of the liver, the figure of which preceded.
And let there be between each cautery the quantity of the thickness of a finger, or a little more.
And let the method of cautery be, which we said in the cautery of the liver, equally.
And do not press your hand with the cautery: and let the patient be lying on his back.
And another method of cauterization is: that you ignite a cautery having two prongs, which we said in the chapter on the dislocation of the humerus: and raise the skin which is in the direction of the spleen, where the left elbow of the patient arrives: and let your raising of the skin be according to the breadth of the body, so that the cauteries fall according to the length of the body.
Then insert two very hot prongs until you penetrate the skin on the other side with them: then withdraw the cautery: therefore four cauteries are made.
And if you wish to cauterize with another cautery having three prongs.
Then take care of the cautery after you release it, that it emits pus for many days: for it is more beneficial than all the others which preceded it in the treatment.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in dropsy.
Cautery does not fall in dropsy, except for ascites properly.
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment which we have mentioned in the division, and the treatment does not help, then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries around the navel, and one cautery above the stomach, and another cautery above the liver, and a cautery above the spleen, and two cauteries on the back between the vertebrae, and one in the region of the chest, and another in the region of the stomach: and let the depth of the cautery be close to the thickness of the skin.
Then leave the cautery open, so that it emits pus for a long time.
And do not remove the patient from treatment after the cautery with him: for it is necessary that two intentions be combined for him: and healing will hasten to him with Godâ€™s help.
However, the shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized, should be nail-shaped: according to what preceded its shape: and that with which the back is cauterized, should be olive-shaped according to what preceded its shape.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the feet and legs.
When abscesses form in a dropsical patient, and are filled with a yellowish water: it is necessary that you cauterize on the dorsum of the foot in the hollow which is between the little finger and ring finger: and raise your hand with the cautery, and do not twist it: then raise your hand: and do not repeat it at all.
For the yellowish water will sweat out, and the cautery should be according to this shape.
Then make two cauteries on each of the two legs, and let the cautery be made with the knife-like end of the cautery; and let the cautery be made according to the length of the leg, one below the knee, and another lower than that, about the middle of the leg: and on each hip two cauteries: and leave the cautery open without treatment for a long time: so that some water sweats out from it: then treat it with the other things which we have spoken of.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in looseness of the bowels.
Chapter 35.
When there is looseness of the bowels from cold and humors, until it weakens the retentive and digestive powers which are in the stomach and intestines: and it is treated with methods of treatment, and is not healed: and you see the patient tolerating cauterization of exuberant virtue, then cauterize him with a large cautery over his stomach, as preceded in the cauterization of the stomach with a circular cautery, and four small cauteries around the navel, with a thin nail-like cautery: and a cautery over the spleen above the ribs, large, or two cauteries.
And if you see exuberant humors, and the patient tolerating it, then cauterize him with a cautery over the pubis, and a cautery over each flank: and perhaps you will add two small cauteries over the stomach near the large one.
For it is a beneficial treatment, whose help does not fail.
Concerning the method of cauterizing hemorrhoids.
Chapter XXXVI.
When in the anus there are many old hemorrhoids, or one, and they are from thick, cold humors, or corrupted humors, and they are treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not help: then cauterize with three cauteries over the lower vertebra of the back below the loins somewhat triangularly, and with a cautery below the navel about two fingers [width].
And if you think that his stomach is chilled, and his food is not digested, and you see his face breaking out in pustules, then cauterize him over the stomach with a large cautery, according to what preceded: and with a cautery over his liver, and with another over his spleen, with a nail-shaped cautery: and leave the cautery open for some time: then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter XXXVII.
When you cut away the althelul, then heat a cautery that resembles a ray. Then insert it, burning, into the excised althelul: and move your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which blood flows: do this once or twice.
If therefore the althelul are many, then cauterize each one with a cautery according to what we have related. Then treat them with what benefits them from ointments until they heal.
And if you cauterize it with a large cautery over the alcatin, it is more ultimate in aid.
Concerning the method of cauterizing a fistula in the anus.
Chapter XXXVIII.
When the patient does not submit to the incision and the operation that we have described in its place, and is frightened by it, perhaps he is cured with a cautery.
Therefore, when a fistula occurs in someone, and it becomes old, pus and corrupt humors flow from it. It is therefore more fitting that you should probe it with a thin probe. Then ascertain the extent of its depth with a probe.
Then heat a cautery, which is similar to a ray. Then insert the heated cautery into the fistula and the amount that the nail entered.
And repeat the cautery over it until all the corrupt matter is burned away, once, or twice, or three times, according to your needs.
And beware of burning a nerve, if there is one, or a large vein; or if the fistula goes to the body of the bladder, or to the body of the intestine, then beware of all these places.
And do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy place: and you think that it is not penetrating.
Then take care of the place until it is healed.
But if the place is sealed, and the materials are cut away from it, and it remains so for a long time: then know that it is now completely healed.
And if materials are not cut away from it, then know that it is penetrating, or in its depth there is a corrupt bone, or something like that from those: whose remembrance will come in its place.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the kidneys.
When pain occurs in the kidneys from cold or from gross flatulence, and the patient's strength diminishes because of it, then it is necessary to cauterize it above the almethen on the kidneys themselves, cauterizing over each kidney with a cautery iron, whose mention preceded.
And perhaps we cauterize it a second time in the almeidatu itself. Therefore three lines will result. Therefore the latter will be more helpful.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder from cold and moisture, until the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it in the lower part of the pubis above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubis begin, and with a cautery on the right side of the pubis, and another on the left: and let the length of that lateral cauterization be according to the size of the thumb's knuckle; and cauterize it with a cautery in the lower part of the back, or with two cauteries, if you need it: and let it be according to the preceding cautery,  a nail-shaped cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the womb.
When cold and moisture affect the womb, and it is prevented by that from impregnation, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and she experiences pain at their arrival, then it is necessary that it be cauterized with three cauteries around the navel, as we said in the cauterization of the bladder, and with a cautery above the coccyx in the lower back, or with two cauteries: and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the anus in dislocation.
When mucous humors are poured out to the socket of the hip, and become the cause of its exit from its place: and the sign of this is that the leg is lengthened over the other, when they are compared to each other: and the place of the dislocation is found empty in it.
Therefore it is necessary that you cauterize the patient above the socket of his hip with a cautery similar to a circle: after you mark with a branding iron around the socket, as it is revolved: and that the socket itself fall in the middle of the circle.
But if this cautery is not present to you, then cauterize it with three cauteries, with a large olive-shaped cautery: and let the depth of the cautery be according to the quantity of the thickness of all the skin.
Then take care of it, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in sciatica.
When pain occurs in the hip joint, and its cause is cold and dampness: and the patient is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help, and it becomes chronic: then it is necessary that you resolve the patient from gross humors with fetid pills, or with artificial pills, and the like. Then cauterize him.
And the method of cauterization in it is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with fire is according to many methods.
One of which is, that you cauterize above the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations, according to this form. And make them a good, or bad depth. And let the space that is between each cauterization be the quantity of the thickness of a finger: and let the cauterization be olive-shaped.
Sometimes however the cauterization is done in the middle above the head of the hip joint itself. Therefore there are four cauterizations.
And if you wish to cauterize with a circle, whose form preceded in the cauterization of the dislocation of the hip, with one: so that it is a circle containing the whole hip.
And burn the thickness of the whole skin.
This is the method of cauterization in sciatica, when the pain is adhering to the hip itself, and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
But if it extends to the thigh and leg, then cauterize it with two cauteries above the thigh, above the place to which the sick person points there, in which there is pain, and with your cautery above the ankle four fingers towards the outer part a little: and let the cautery be knife-shaped.
And let the depth of the cauterization be according to the depth of the thickness of the skin.
But if the sick person indicates that the pain extends almost to the toes, then cauterize it there where he indicates with a pointed cautery, three, or four, or more, if he needs it.
And if he indicates the pain to be below the knee towards the leg, then cauterize it there with one knife-shaped cautery.
And beware in all your cauterization, that you do not go so far with the cautery as to burn a nerve, or a large artery, and thereby draw upon the sick person great harm, or contraction.
And I indeed have already seen one and another, of those who cauterized above the ankle, and went too far in the cauterization, and the leg swelled, until the swelling reached the foot, and it was entirely perforated, and the whole foot was corrupted: then there followed a looseness of the bowels, and death after that.
But if the pain is in two parts at once, cauterize each according to this method.
And certain wise men have said, in the cauterization of the hip, whose cautery is of this method. Make a similar iron cautery, whose diameter is half a palm: and let it be at its orifice according to the thickness of a date-stone, or somewhat less: and within that cautery let there be a third cautery.
And let the space which is between each cautery be the size of a thumb-joint: and let the cauteries be open on two sides: and let their height be about a joint, or two joints: and make for them a handle of iron decently fitted to the cauteries.
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows and throws off sparks: then let it be placed upon the hip-joint, the patient lying on his sound side. Therefore cauterize him with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave him for three days. Afterwards apply plasters with butter: and leave the wound open for many days. Then treat him with ointment until he is healed.
Albucasis says, â€œMy son, we have used this type of cautery little, because of the terror of its appearance: and because we have found few who would endure it.â€
However, it is one of the good cauteries for him who endures it, and finds with it its place.
The cautery, however, with burning medicines is that you make two samia having a perforated ring, into which descends the hinge of its gate of bronze or iron, the length of the wall; of which in their height are two knots, or as it were the same.
And let the space which is between both be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, and let them be open below and above; and let that which contains them be together, according to this form.
Then place it over the hip socket, the patient lying on the healthy side. And press your hand very much upon the hip socket. Then pour between the two circles of warm water, which has been heated a little with fire, and hold it for the quantity of an hour; and let the patient endure its biting and its burning, for he will find the biting like fire, until the biting ceases.
Then raise your hand with the instrument, and wipe away the warm water from the whole hip with fresh water: then leave it for three days.
And put butter on it, until the blackness of the burn is removed: and leave it for several days, and pus will flow from it. For it is the ultimate aid.
Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the pain extends to the hip or leg: make him stand in what contains water, as you did to the hip equally.
And this is the method of hot water: and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, one part of each; grind both, and put them in a new pot, the lower part of which is perforated with one small hole, according to the size of the radius that will enter it; and place under the bottom of the pot another glass pot; and pour over the alkali and lime enough fresh water to submerge both by one finger; after which you compress them with your hand with good pressure; and leave the pot until the sharp water descends to the lower part of the glass pot.
Then collect all that water, and pour it over more lime; and filter until it is renewed.
For then it will be very strong and sharp, which is used in many medical operations, and in cauterizing other members. Because it produces the operation of fire itself.
And among medicines are those with which ankles are cauterized, also such as tesisie, and cashew honey, and lime with soap, so that both are mixed.
And Galen indeed, in narrating, related the cure of a certain man from antiquity, by which he was cured of hip pain and sciatica; and he magnified it to be very much so. And he said that he did not need another cure, and that he was healed in one time, until he related that he perhaps entered the bath carried, and came out of it already healed.
And it is so that it is taken from fresh seitaragi; and if fresh is not found, let dry, recent [seitaragi] be taken; and let it be ground much with some fat, and let it be placed upon the hip, where there is pain, or leg, or in the thigh; and let it be bound, and let it remain the amount of three hours, or for the amount that the infirm person feels a lessening of the burning. Then introduce him into the bath.
Therefore, when his body becomes moist, introduce him into the bath. For the pain recedes, and it is healed.
And know that it is not necessary that someone use a cautery with these medicines, except after evacuation of the body.
And Dioscorides also said that the dung of goats, when the sciatic vein is cauterized with it, benefits it: and the cautery is made in this way. Let wool be taken, and let it be soaked in old oil, and let it be placed on the deep place which is between the thumb of the foot and the ankle, and is closer to the ankle.
Then take dry goat dung, and burn it in the fire until it becomes charcoal.
Then put it on the wool, and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat it. Therefore do not cease doing this, until sensation reaches the middle up to the ankle, and the pain ceases.
And this method of cautery is called the Vaccine cautery.
Concerning the cauterization of back pain.
Sometimes pain occurs in the back from many causes, either from a fall, or from a blow, or from superfluous evacuation, and similar things, from the infusion of cold and moist material: and indeed cautery is not appropriate except in this type alone, which occurs from the infusion of cold material.
Therefore, after evacuating the patient with fetid pills and the like, three lines should be made on the back where the pain is, according to the width of the patient's almeidati, after marking with encaustum five cauteries in each line, or more according to the amount that comes from the patient's tolerance and strength: and the cauterization should be done with a punctate cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries, with a medium nail cautery, according to this form.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in incipient gibbosity.
This illness often happens to small infants.
And the sign of its beginning in children is that a constriction occurs in their breathing upon elevation and movement; and you find in the last vertebra of the back and its knots that it has already begun to protrude above the other vertebrae.
Therefore, when you see this and wish to treat it, then cauterize it with a cautery that is circular, according to this form.
And let the cautery take on every part of the vertebra equally; and if you wish to cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery, two or three lines, and let the points be close together.
Then treat the place until it is healed with what we have said.
And beware lest you apply the cautery to a hump that is caused by a spasm of the nerve.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in gout and the pains of the joints.
When the pains of the joints are from cold humors which are poured out into any limbs of the body, then when pains happen in the feet, it is customary for physicians to call it gout properly.
Therefore, when gout of a cold cause is treated with methods of cure, and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is so that he cauterizes it after evacuation around the joint of the feet, with many cauteries; and let the cautery be olive-shaped.
And if you need to puncture on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. But if the pains ascend to the knees, or the remaining joints, and this happens many times, then cauterize it above each knee with three cauteries, or four on every side, with this same Clavil cautery.
If, however, more are necessary to you with this cautery, then cauterize it. And do not submerge your hand with the cautery, rather let it be about the thickness of the skin only.
But if the pains ascend to the knees and the back: then administer what we said of the cautery in its chapter.
But if the pains are in the hands only, then puncture around the joints, as two lines are revolved.
But if pain remains in the fingers, then puncture them, above every nodule a puncture, and upon the comb of the hand.
But if the pains ascend after days to the elbows, or to the shoulders, then cauterize both from every part.
And do not deprive the infirm from a good regimen, from the reception of medicines. For if a good regimen is made, and phlegm is evacuated, it will be healed with the help of God with this cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in ruptures.
When a rupture happens in the groin, and the intestine or bladder descends to the testicle, and it is beginning nearby: then it is necessary that you instruct the infirm to omit eating on his day. And that he use lenitives, so that stool descends from his belly.
Then let him sit between your hands on his back: and instruct him to hold his breath, until the bladder or intestine emerges or appears.
Then reduce it with your fingers, and mark with a cautery under the rupture on the pubic bone, with a mark whose end is similar to half a circle towards the upper parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, whose form is this, until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or bladder to its cavity. Then let the assistant place his hand on the place, so that the intestine does not come out: and you have now opened between the legs of the patient, and placed a cushion under him, and another servant over his legs, and another over his chest who holds the hands.
Then place the cautery on the mark, your hand with the cautery standing straight. And hold it until you reach the bone with it; or repeat with it another time, if you have not reached the bone with it in the first attempt.
And carefully observe the exit of the intestine during your cauterization, lest you burn it, and there results from it for the patient either death or great harm.
And know that when you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not effective.
And it is necessary that the cautery for infants be thin, according to their size.
Then treat with the other ointments until it is healed.
And let the patient lie on his back for forty days, until the wound is sealed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment for the length of time of his healing, which will soothe his belly: so that the intestine does not come out during straining and defecation.
Then when he wishes to get up after forty days, then apply a properly made bandage, and let him keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be lessened from labor, and from fullness of food and drink, and from loud voices.
For when he uses this regime, thus he is healed with a whole healing.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures with incision in its chapter.
But when ruptures occur in the rest of the belly, and are beginning, and you wish that they not increase, then cauterize its rupture with a round cautery according to its size: and let that which is burned be then as a third of it. Then treat him with what we have said: and it will not increase.
And now some of the ancients have said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, according to this form.
After it is marked with a hot iron, and a line is placed which is across the width on the upper side of the rupture, and another line below. And place your hand in the middle with a nail-shaped cautery.
But the first cautery is easier and better.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in pains due to a fall or blow.
Chapter XLVIII.
When pain occurs in some member from a fall or blow, and that pain persists for a long time, and treatment with medicines does not help it, then it is necessary that it be cauterized with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery of every member be according to its size, and its suitability, and its weakness, and its strength, and the location of the twisting, and the pain from it.
If therefore it heals from the first cauterization, it is good. If not, then repeat the cauterization on it.
Because it is customary for these pains to move from one member to that which is near it. Therefore it is necessary that you pursue them with cautery, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in leprosy.
Lepers, however, are sometimes helped by cautery with the greatest benefit. And especially the type of leprosy which arises from the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy: therefore when you wish to cauterize it, consider.
If therefore the leprosy is incipient, and you have treated it with what we have said in the division, and it does not subside, nor remain stable: and you fear for the patient, that corruption might seize upon his whole complexion: then cauterize him upon the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, known, and a second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, and two upon the two temples, and one behind upon the nape of the neck.
And lastly apply your hand with the cautery lightly, until it imprints a small mark on the skin, and the scabs are loosened from it: so that the exhalation of gross vapors from it may be alleviated, and cauterize also with the cautery upon the spleen itself, according to what has preceded.
But if the leprosy is already spread over the patient, and appears manifestly: then it is necessary that you cauterize with those cauteries which we have mentioned on the head, and cauterize upon the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries upon the two jaws, and with two cauteries upon the vertebrae of the neck, and six upon the vertebrae of the back, and one large one upon the haunch near the coccyx and one above it, in the same place: and two upon the hips: namely upon each hip one, and two upon the knees: one upon each knee, and two upon the shoulders, and two upon the elbows, and two upon the soft parts of his chest: and cauterize him upon every joint, of the joints of his fingers and toes: and cauterize upon each heel of his feet, and the palms of his hands.
And beware of the nerves which are upon the back of the heel, lest you burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery upon the pubic bone, and another upon the stomach, and another upon the liver.
And know that the more you add cauterization, the more helpful and beneficial it is.
And know that the patient does not find pain in cauterization, as healthy people do; because his body is already numb.
And it is necessary that the cauteries be of size and smallness according to the limbs and joints, according to what preceded in the kinds of instruments.
Then treat the cauterization with herb flour with honey, and the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in numbness.
When a limb is numb, and is treated with medicines, and oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then cauterize that numb limb with cauteries, according to what the size of the limb, or its smallness, deserves.
And let your cauterization penetrate slightly into the thickness of the skin. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
And sometimes cauterization is done for a certain numbness which happens in the hand and foot, in the vertebrae of the back near the exit of the nerves which move that limb: and the numbness is removed.
Truly, let no one presume upon this except he who is wise in the anatomy of the limbs and the exit of the nerves moving the body.
Chapter 51.
When the albaras becomes chronic and nothing from the arts of medicine benefits it, then cauterize it superficially with a deep cautery, according to the amount of the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness is removed and its color is changed.
Then treat it with the flour of the herb, and lentils with rose oil, and leaves of arnoglossa, and pigeon's blood, and swallow's blood: of each let there be one part; let all these be mixed, and spread upon a cloth, and applied to the place until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing cancer.
When cancer is beginning, and you wish to stop it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it is rolled around.
The wise, however, have said that it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery in the middle.
But to me this does not seem good, because I fear that it will ulcerate; and I have already seen this happen several times.
And it is better that it be cauterized around it with a circle, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Adubelati.
When someone has an adubelati, and it is slow in maturing, either because of an excess causing it, or because of the patient's age, when he is elderly and has little blood, or because of the time, and one wishes to hasten the maturation of the adubelati, then cauterize around it with many small cauteries, puncturing. Then leave it. For it itself will hasten with maturation.
But if you wish its perforation with a cautery, then a fire cautery, of which this is the form.
And place it in the middle of the adubelati, until it penetrates the skin: and let the cautery be from that which follows the diseased area, so that the flow of pus may be relieved.
Then treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a corroding ulcer.
Chapter 54.
A corroding ulcer is nothing but a corruption in a limb which moves about and corrodes it: just as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore if you see a corroding ulcer in a place which can tolerate cauterization with fire, then have many fire cauteries, small and large nails, according to what is suitable for the place in which the corroding ulcer is.
Then cauterize it on every side until you eradicate the whole corruption, and nothing of it remains at all.
Then leave it for three days, and place on the cauterized areas ground sulfur with oil, until all the eschars and all the corruption are eradicated.
Then treat it with ointments which generate flesh.
Therefore if you see that after three days flesh grows with a healthy growth, in which there is no corruption, it is good.
And if not: then repeat the cautery on that which remains of the corrupted areas.
And a corrosive is sometimes medicated with a sharp medicine. For it itself arises in the place of the cautery.
However, cautery with fire is of quicker help: and indeed I have already spoken of its cauterization with a sharp medicine in the division: therefore receive it there when you need it.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Clavi and Warts.
Chapter 55.
Often this ailment happens in the lower parts of the feet: and it is a coarse, filtered thing which harms the foot.
And indeed cautery in them is according to two methods: cautery with fire, and cautery with hot water.
As for that with fire, make a concave iron cautery like a vulture's feather: which should be made of iron, according to the size which contains the clavus on every side: and let it have thin edges.
Then place it, burning hot, upon the clavus. Then rotate the hand with the cautery around the clavus, until the cautery reaches the depth of the clavus: and leave it for three days, until it begins to emit pus. Then apply a plaster to it with wild mallows, crushed with salt: and leave the plaster on it at night. For it will be eradicated from its roots.
Then treat the place with a flesh-generating ointment, until it is healed.
