But if they are not inverted, and frequently occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet: then apply a cautery to them with warm water: and it is so that you take a small tube of bronze, or iron, or of a vulture's feather: and place it on the clavus, or wart. Then pour a small quantity of warm water into the tube: hold your hand, and revolve it with a slight compression of your hand, so that the edge of the tube is pressed into the root of the clavus, and the warm water finds a way to the depth, to the root of the clavus: and let the patient wait a little for the biting of the warm water for one hour.
Then dismiss him, for the clavus will be eradicated with its roots.
Do this with them one after another, until you have come to all those that are in the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Tremor.
When tremor happens to someone from cold in a nerve, or from a quartan fever, or something else: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries, or five, on the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra a cautery, and on his chest a cautery, and on his stomach a cautery.
For tremor rests with olive cautery, and the healing of the cold illness hastens.
Concerning the method of cauterizing albathir.
Chapter 57.
Sometimes foul albathir, that is, pustules, which are made from gross corrupt materials, are expelled in the body.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary that they be cauterized above every head of the pustule with a subtle cautery with myrtle wood, the end of which is lit with fire. Or with the root of long birthwort. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner: and they do not increase; and the superfluity making them is broken, and they heal from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Often there occurs a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut in a wound, which happens externally, or in the incision of an abscess, or cauterization of a limb, and similar things: and it is difficult to stop it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then hasten with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger upon it: and press it hard, until the blood is stopped under your finger, and nothing comes out of it.
Then put into the fire small olive oil cauteries, and many more large ones: and blow upon them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place in which the artery is broken, and place the cautery upon the vein, after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
But if it spurts out when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery, from the cauteries which are prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease doing this with one after another, until the blood is stopped.
And beware lest you burn the nerve which is there, and another harm befall the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, except with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the bleeding is stopped. Or it is tied with a strong ligature. Or let medicines of a styptic property be placed upon it, and let it be bound with a suitable compress.
But he who manages its excision with a ligature, and constriction with a cloth, or the application of cauterizing agents, and the like, does not completely help it, except rarely.
But if this should happen to someone, and a physician or medicine is not at hand, then let him begin by placing the index finger over the opening of the vein, as we have described, and constricting it tightly until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And let the finger not be removed from above it, constantly applying very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened, and cut off.
And meanwhile let him consider what it needs of cautery or medicine.
Concerning incision, perforation, and phlebotomy in wounds and similar things.
We have already said in the first chapter, every illness in which cautery with fire and burning medicine is suitable, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I placed chapters there from the head to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same way: so that the seeker may be relieved concerning what he seeks.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you learn, O sons, that in this chapter there is something terrifying, which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, caution is necessary in it more vehemently, because in the operation which is in this chapter, very often evacuation with blood occurs, with which life stands in the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of an exit, or treatment of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision upon a stone, and similar things: from all of which trembling and fear are associated, and death occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that which is hesitant fall upon you, for sometimes there fall to you in this art types of men with types of infirmities. For among them is one who is angered by his illness, and death is light to him: because of the vehemence of what he finds from his illness and the length of its malice. And with illness there is fear that signifies death.
And among them is one who gives his wealth, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything of it except after certain knowledge, which verifies for you that to which praiseworthy success has come.
And use in the treatment of all your infirm, prognosis and indication of that to which health is suitably directed.
For in that is help for you in acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His understanding, and may He not take away from you direction and inspiration: for that is in His hand.
And I indeed have arranged this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck to the feet: that the thing which you wish from it, and which you need, may be lightened for you.
Concerning the treatment of water which gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this infirmity often happens to children at birth, or when the midwife constricts the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it also happens from a hidden cause which is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this sickness in others than in children: and to everyone of them whom I saw, death hastened; therefore it seemed to me to omit an operation for it.
And indeed I saw one of them, a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day, until he could not sit upright because of the size of his head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture is either collected between the skin and the bone, or collected under the bone upon the dura mater.
And the operation in that case, if the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head, one in width: and let the length of the incision be about two fingerbreadths, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the shape of a spatula.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then make two incisions, according to this shape.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side: and the water is depressed when you compress it with your hand towards the interior, and it is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with three incisions, in this manner.
And after the incision remove all the moisture. Then bind the incisions with cloths and powders. Then swab it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then unbind the ligature, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equal tightness. And feed the patient with all dry food of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is that you consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes it is greater in the occiput, or in the sinciput, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear to you: and incise it as much as is possible for you.
And observe that you do not cut an artery, and that a flow of blood occurs, and that the patient dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of the arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs happen to someone in the eyes, or in the chest, and it becomes chronic, and the treatment of medicines does not help: then the last cure in that is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to incise them, the patient must shave his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then the patient should bind his neck with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein pulsates. And the locations of both are the depressed places which are behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in certain men.
Then mark with a cautery on each: and incise each with a sharp spatula on both sides, the incision reaching to the bone: and let this be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, introduce the spatula under the artery, and incise it upwards with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers.
For when a vein is incised, blood flows out with a pulsating flow, which leaps inwards with a frequent leap.
But if the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear a space of the size of three fingers. Then mark with a cautery, and cut down to the bone.
And that which it is necessary to let out of blood, is six ounces and perhaps you let out of it more, or less. All that according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the sick person and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if something of the sifac remains at the bone, then incise it, that a hot abscess may not happen: then bind the wound with linen cloths.
Then treat it with ointment, until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic hemicrania, or acute catarrh because of acrid humors and heat in the temples, or chronic headache, and similar things, and he is treated with methods of medical treatment, and it does not help: then we have experienced in these sicknesses the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have recounted.
And the method of operating in their extraction is that the sick person should shave the hairs which are in the temples, and should stretch the artery apparent in the temple. For it is shown to you by its pulse, and is rarely hidden except in a few men, or by the intensity of cold.
But if it is hidden from you, then the sick man should bind his head with any cloth of his; then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with warm water, until it appears to you in manifest appearance. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little while ago. Then flay the skin with it easily, until you reach the artery. Then throw a hook into it, and draw it upwards, until you draw it out of the skin, and make it emerge from the tunics which are beneath it on every side.
But if the artery is small, then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and so that a flow of blood does not occur. For if it is not cut and if it is not severed, the blood does not completely ascend.
Then evacuate blood from it, six ounces down to three.
But if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with double, strong thread, of silk or of alhod cords, so that putrefaction does not hasten to it before the healing of the wound, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then cut the excess which is between the two ligatures. Do this at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery as far as the bone, with a knife-cautery, until its ends are seared, and it will suffice for the same work.
And it is a better procedure, unless the patient has a fever or is of a heated complexion. Because cautery is among those things which help to end humidities. Therefore it is a better aid.
And it is necessary after the extraction of arteries that the place be filled with old lint, and that properly made pledgets be placed upon it.
And after the loosening it should be treated with dry medicines which cause flesh to grow, and with linen cloths, until it is healed.
But if meanwhile, while you are operating, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hasten to cut it off with a cautery, or to fill the place with lint; and squeeze your hand until the blood is cut off. But if none of these things is present to you, then place your finger on the place until the blood clots. And bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness ceases, and it is constricted as much as is necessary.
And of what is lighter and easier in the extraction of an artery, it is to cauterize a vein with this cautery having two knives: and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to an almond. However, they are of much less sharpness than two knives. Because if they are sharp, like two knives, cold hastens to both: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is held in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this therefore the work is better than every work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space that is between the two knives be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with a cautery: and place both very hot, until they arrive at the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between its extremities is lengthened. For they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the care of sharp, constant lachrymation from the eyes.
When there are constant tears, and their flow is from the veins which are on the outside of the skull, and no medicinal treatment helps, and you see the patient's face becoming red, and he feels a crawling on his forehead, like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their hairs are already corroded by the corrosion, and his eyelids are heated by the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients used this operation in their treatment, and they called it treatment with the knife.
And it is so that you should order the patient to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equidistant incisions on the forehead along the length of the forehead; and let the length of the incision be about two fingers, and one incision be equidistant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the muscles, and the third on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries which are on the two sides: and lengthen your hand from the continuation of the two jawbones, and let the space of each incision be the amount of three joined fingers: and let you have a prepared piece of sponge, or many dried cloths, with which you will wipe away the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, whose form follows now. From the incision which is from the side of the temples, up to the second incision: and scrape with it all the skin which is between the two incisions, with the membrane which is above the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp knife, smooth on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh connected with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision be continued to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, cleanse the places of the clots of congealed blood.
Then place in each fissure a pledget of old cotton, and place over them pledgets soaked in wine and oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the ligature, and administer abundant irrigation with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment and other remedies with which wounds are treated, until it heals.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs affecting the eyes from within the head.
When someone experiences acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs, and you see their eyes weakened, small, and their vision already impaired; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is a sharp, painful pain deep in the head, accompanied by sneezing: then understand from these symptoms that those substances and catarrhs do not come except from many veins.
And the best treatment for them is this. And it is that you instruct the patient to shave his forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or slightly to one side, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision reach the bone: and move your hand away from the templesâ€™ muscles, which move during chewing, until the bone is exposed: and you will wipe away all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with old linen or linen threads. Then bind it above with pledgets; and soak the pledgets with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you unbind it, and you see that the hot abscess is now diminishing: then it is necessary that you rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen which causes flesh to grow: such as if you take two parts of wheat flour, and four parts of resin: and prepare a plaster from them, and apply it for the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Whatever falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something similar to stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as a chickpea and beans; or it is a fluid substance, such as water and vinegar, and things similar to them; or some animal.
When therefore a stone, or a kind of stone from among those which are not added to, nor grow in the ear, falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if therefore you see the stone, then instill into the ear something of violet oil or sesame oil. Then manage its extraction, and make him sneeze with sneezing powder, and block the nostrils at the coming of the sneezing, after you place around the ear pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards.
For very often it comes out with this treatment.
But if it does not come out, then manage its extraction with forceps, that is, with fine hooks: whose form is this.
If therefore it comes out with forceps, it is good.
And if not, then manage its extraction with a small, thin, blunt hook of few windings.
If therefore it does not come out by that, then make a cannula of bronze, and insert the end of the cannula into the hole of the ear very far, and stop what is around the cannula with wax softened with oil, so that there is no way for the wind except the cannula: then extract it with your strong suction.
For very often it comes out with what we have narrated. But if it does not come out in this way: then take a little of helkalimbat, or of birdlime, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught: and put it on the end of a probe after you have neatly wrapped cotton around it: then insert it into the hole of the ear easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have narrated, then hasten to incise, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of incision is that the phlebotomist first bleeds the patient in the cephalic vein: and extract blood from him according to the measure of his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its fat in a depressed place: and let the incision be in the shape of a crescent, until you reach the stone: then remove it with whatever is possible for you from the instruments.
Then sew up the incision quickly, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains which grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
But if it does not yield to exit, then take a thin, delicate spatula, according to this form.
And administer with it the excision of that kind of grain which falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think that that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you put it into many pieces: then extract them with hooks, or with a thin probe, or with suction, as we have said, for its extraction is alleviated.
In the case of water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with the head held upright; and he should then fill his ear with a little old lint; and he should lie on that ear in which the water is. If therefore it exits through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, about the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and warm them slightly with fire, and let the patient insert one of them into the opening of his ear: and let him stand on one foot on that side, and strike with another stone upon the stone which is in the ear. Therefore let him not cease doing that with stone and stone, until all the water comes out.
And then the water comes out so that you take one of a pipe cleaner or of a rush, and insert one end of it into the ear: and light the other end with fire, until the greater part of it is burned. Then repeat another rush, and do this many times, until all the water comes out: and draw it out with a tube as was previously described for the stone.
But in the extraction of an animal entering it, if it is of small body, like a flea and the like, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which is apparent to the sense: then manage its extraction with forceps and hooks: and its matter is easier than all things which are held in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something of worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
But if nothing appears there, then take a cannula, of which this is the form, narrow below, wide above: and introduce the thin end into the ear according to the amount that the patient tolerates it: then suck with it with strong suction. Do this many times, until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then stop up the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the case of the stone.
But if they do not come out with what we have said: then administer distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the killing of worms: and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring out, by which you pour oils and medicines into the ear, be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, with a small hole, wide above.
Therefore let that which pushes what is in the concavity of the cannula be made decently of bronze. And if you wish, take a probe and wrap cotton around its end neatly; then pour oil or juice or whatever you wish of these medicines into the cannula; and it shall be in the ear; then insert the probe with the cotton on top and press it with your hand with moderate pressure, until the oil is driven into the concavity of the hearing, and the patient feels it inside; and that which is poured into the ear shall already have been warmed a little with fire; and beware lest that which is distilled into it be very cold, for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the treatment of blockage happening in the ear.
Sometimes certain infants emerge from the wombs of their mothers imperforate.
And it has also happened to some men that there is a blockage in the passage of the ear from a wound, or from flesh grown there.
And this blockage sometimes occurs in the depth of the opening of the ear, which sight does not reach; and sometimes it happens near the exterior of the opening, and sight reaches it.
Therefore, that which happens in the depth of the ear, and which sight does not reach, is in most instances difficult of healing.
And in that which sight reaches, it is necessary that you put the ear of the sick person in the sun, and look into it: then if you see the apparent blockage, open it with a thin spatula, of which form is that which follows, let its extremity in which there is a certain small width, some part of which is sharp: and the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides: so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the blockage is from flesh grown in the passage of the ear: then hold it with a thin hook, and cut it with strong ease, until all the flesh is removed.
But if the blockage is in the depth of the ear: then take a thin, smooth rod, and heat it slightly with fire: then put it into the passage of the ear.
If therefore you feel the blockage with that which the sick person finds with difficulty of hearing, try its opening with ease: and beware, lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then put in the ear a small piece of linen according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for a few days, until you are sure of the healing of the wound.
Or take linen, and dip it in rose water, and sprinkle on it crushed verdigris, and use it.
But if you see the ear already festering: then it is necessary that you remove the linen, and replace it with another linen, which you will wrap in cerate which is made with rose oil, until the warm abscess subsides: then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs: then submerge a sponge, or cloths in cold water, and place it over the ear: and use the remaining treatment, which stops the flow of blood, previously mentioned in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of the sty of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
Those styes which occur on the eyelids, are sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary to grasp them with forceps; or with a hook, and to incise them with a spatula from their roots. Then apply pounded vitriol to the places.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after incision. For often they return when something of their roots remains. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of the hailstone-like growths of the eyelid.
Chapter IX.
Very often something occurs in the eyelids which is similar to hail in its strength and hardness, and because of this it is called hailstone: and it is an aggregation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you should then consider if it is a hailstone growing in it, which appears from the eyelid, and moves to every part, and its matter is easy.
Therefore, make a fissure across it. Then scrape it from every side easily until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
But if its excision is not prepared for you except after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the patient in some way.
But if the fissure is large, then join it with a suture and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come from it to you. For the ointment restores it and causes it to grow flesh.
And if the hailstone slopes towards the interior of the ear towards the inner surface, then invert the eyelid and suspend the hailstone with a hook; except that you need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is lengthened by incision, that does no harm in any respect.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the hailstone with salt water: and treat the place with that which causes it to grow flesh, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the axirnath of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is fat which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid: and indeed it happens most to boys: and it weighs down their eyes, and catarrhs happen to them in the mornings, and they cannot look at the sunlight, because tears rush to them, and because of that you see them lying down on their faces always, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids are moist under the eyebrows, which swelling has already covered: and indeed the prominence of the axirnath is apparent to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel the axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person puts his head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare a pledget in it, and make from that pledget a circle according to the quantity that contains the axirnath on every side. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers on every side, so that the axirnath is gathered together in the middle of the circle.
Then make a cut in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, making incisions in width, and let not the incision be larger than that which is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you incise the whole skin, until you arrive at the axirnath, for in many dispositions the axirnath proceeds from the incision according to the form of a piece of white fat.
Therefore extract it with the cloth which you have infiltrated between your fingers to the exterior: and you turn your hand to the right and left, until it is dispersed: and beware, lest you add to the incision, so that you do not arrive with the lancet to the eye, and injure it.
But if the axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you should add a little to the incision easily, until the axirnath comes forth. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then submerge cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the place, and bind it with powders.
And some men grind salt, and put it in the hollow of the incision, so that what remains of that moisture may be dissolved.
Then care for it until it heals.
But if it happens in a place of a warm abscess, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
Concerning the ways of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye is softened, whence it happens that it is wrinkled as it ought not to be.
Chapter XI.
When hairs grow on the eyelids in addition to the natural hairs, and grow against them: then they harm the eye, and cause various diseases, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and leucoma, until it becomes the cause of the destruction of the eye.
And Ataxmir of the eye is indeed cured according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what was stated in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and suture. Or by tubes, according to what I shall say.
It is necessary that you put the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, in which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let that be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid, in which the hairs grow: and draw the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a radius.
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with a spatula, going from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then remove the thread, and place a small pad of cotton or cloth under the eyelid: then mark above the eyelid with a cautery, which should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: because this varies in people.
For there are some who require that a large amount be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount that is softened in the eyelid.
Then with a spatula incise upon the two lines which you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct, and let the incision be one near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a ray.
Then insert a hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then flay it completely. Then unite with a suture the two lips, with needle and fine woolen thread: and wipe away the blood, and make adhere that which superabounds from the threads upon the two eyebrows, with some sticking substances, if you wish to do that: and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture and threads for three or four days; afterwards treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture, and treat it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is raised during the closing of the wound and its uniting: but the future will be better.
The ancients taught this method of treatment, Ataxmir.
Nevertheless, there is concern over the patient, and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is also in Ataxmir: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a figure which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three hooks separated, or joined together, according to this form which follows, then cut away what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with evenness of incision.
But if it is not possible for you to hold a hook, and it is not equal: then take a needle in which there is thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger lachrymal, and a second thread near the smaller lachrymal: and gather together between your fingers the threads with evenness.
Then raise your hand with an even raising: and raise the skin over which the cutting has been marked, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and take care of it, until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with cerate, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid also, that its hairs are turned.
Therefore it is necessary that you use in it what we said in the incision: but suture in both is more skillful. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with canes.
And it is, that you turn back the eyelid, and cut the fissure which is within, according to what I have related. Then skillfully make two canes, or two thin pieces of wood: whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width is less than the width of a spathula: and you have already incised at the extremities of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully apply each to that which superfluously protrudes from the eyelid, and bind the two canes on both sides with a strong binding, and leave it for several days. For the constricted skin will die, and will blacken, and will rot, until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it is slow, then incise it with scissors. Then treat it until it is healed.
When therefore it has consolidated, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two tubes, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the elevation of hairs pricking in the eye with a needle.
The operation in this is, that you take a fine needle, and insert into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then bring together its ends, and knot each with a very fine knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place in Alinxotati another fine thread under it shortly, and knot its ends if you wish.
Then let the patient place his head in your bosom, and be near the sun so that the operation may be clear: because it is a fine, or small, operation.
Then insert the needle with the Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you pull it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up above the Alinxotati: and insert that hair into the Alinxotati: if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair comes out with the Alinxotati into the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if the Alinxotati is pulled, and the hair does not come out with it: you will pull the Alinxotati downwards, with the thread which you have placed in it, until the Alinxotati comes out from the upper hole, and you see it with the sight of the eye: then indeed you will pull the Alinxotati and the thread: and pull out both, and close the eye: and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
But if the hair is short, then either associate with it a long hair from the natural hairs, and elevate it with it: or leave it, until it lengthens after a space, and then elevate it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental, therefore, it is made from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation in it is that you should split that consolidation, and separate its lips, and place between them a pledget of linen, and bind it until it is healed.
And it is not necessary that you should use in its cure things which dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: indeed use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek: and embrocate with water in which has been boiled mallow, linseed: and diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil: and wrap with it a pledget, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your cure is that you strive with all your skill that it may not consolidate according to the form which it had at first.
And this operation which we have spoken of is nothing but a certain rectification of the Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not entirely return to what it was.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is that which is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And accidental occurs from a wound, or fissure, or cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operating on it is that you take a needle in which there is double thread, and you insert it into the flesh, and you make it penetrate from the left lachrymal to the right lachrymal, until the thread arrives at both extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and incise it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it ought, it is good.
And if not, take a radius, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures in the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is made from them: until when they are joined their figure becomes this figure, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts: sew it with linen thread.
Then treat them with what we have spoken of, emollient medicines and linen cloths, until they are healed.
And if an abscess occurs from a fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, even as has preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a linen cloth, as we have said.
And the sum of the discussion of the treatment of an abscess, when it is above, or below, is, that the operation should be performed, according to what is prepared in the form of an abscess.
For it is of many diversities in form: and a skillful surgeon prepares a method with whatever way is possible for him, until he restores the form to the natural form, or near it, and the patient himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in many preparations.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many men, so that the upper eyelid is consolidated with the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural functions.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the end of a probe under the eyelid, and lift it upwards: or extend it with a hook: then incise the adhesion with a thin spatula, which is not with the sharpness of cutting spatulas, but rather somewhat blunt: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and injures the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be as if you are scraping away that adhesion easily, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the whole adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, wash it with water: or distill into it from a sieve made, from ziniar dissolved with water.
Then separate the eyelid and eye with a linen cloth: and place on the eye wool infused with egg white: and after the third day use a sieve, and with solidifying agents, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the eminence of flesh of the tear ducts.
Chapter XVI.
Know that a nail is according to two methods. Or it is sinewy: and it is similar to hard, thin fat. Or it is not sinewy, similar to congealed white moisture: which when iron touches it, or you try to grasp it with a hook, is cut off, and the hook does not fix in it.
And both types do not begin except from the larger tear duct, until it gradually goes, until it covers the sight, and prevents light and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operating on its incision is: that the sick person puts his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of little curvature, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hair of a horse, or ox, or cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread, and extend it upwards: and flay with the hair the side of the nail which follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair to its end.
Then incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that a constant flow of tears may not result from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone; or with thread, as we have said. Then scrape it with a smooth spatula, of which this is the form.
And beware lest the spatula touch the corneal tunic, and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye some crushed salt, or a poultice made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with what is necessary, until it is healed.
But if the nail is not fleshy, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor can a hook be fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicines, and is healed as I have described in the division.
But if you wish to incise it, then open the eye of the patient, and take a thin, smooth spatula, of weak sharpness, according to this form. And shave the nail from above carefully with subtlety. But if you see that it is thinned and broken, and the shaving impresses itself upon it, then instill from your hour into the eye a solution made from ziniar, or from red copperas, or crushed salt: and close the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation on it until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye contrary to this: for then leave it, and treat the abscess until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation on it carefully until it is healed.
However, if the prominence of the lacrimal flesh causes great harm to the eye, suspend it with a hook, and incise a part of it: and do not extend the incision, lest the flow of tears be affected.
Then instill into the tear duct red wine, or something made from verdigris, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic, and the flesh growing in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes a red, composite flesh grows in the eyes of some people, either superimposed until it covers the pupil, or approaches, or constricts it over the eyelids, and perhaps inverts the eyelids outwards: and it resembles a wild rose.
And the operation in it is also that the infirm person puts his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh with hooks suitable for it: and hold it with one forceps, or instrument.
Then incise the flesh first, until all of it is finished with a scalpel. And beware of the eye, so that you do not injure it during the operation: and let your incision, with which you incise it, be either with a spatula, which we have described in the incision of the nail: or with small scissors, with which the asebel is collected, according to what its form will be after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is completed, and all the alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt: or distil into it red powder, and things similar to it from corrosive medicines: and place over the eye outside a cotton ball with egg white: so that it may be protected from a hot abscess.
But if some alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess: then leave the eye, and treat it with that which soothes the abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh which happens in the eye from this species.
However, it is necessary that you avoid treating any weak eye, from a natural or accidental cause, by operating with iron: and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that the member is able to tolerate that operation.
Concerning the removal of a cataract from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Cataracts are red veins which are woven over the eye, and prevent the vision from performing its function; and they weaken the eye with the passage of time.
Therefore it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which is the cataract is strong, and there is in it no illness whatsoever besides the cataract: then remove its cataract.
And it is [the case] that you instruct the patient to place his head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment; and let the hook be a fine double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks joined in one body, according to this form.
Then collect with fine scissors those veins delicately, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and they are resolved; and beware lest you injure the eye with the points of the spatula.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have finished, then instill into the eye green or red vitriol, so that by its sharpness it corrodes what remains of the sebum.
But if it is not possible for you to collect all of it in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the pain of the eye, and leave it for a few days, until its pain is settled and it is secure from a hot abscess; then repeat on it in the same manner, until it is healed.
And this is the shape of the scissors.
Concerning the reduction of the Arisatum to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call Arisatum a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a sharp burning medicine, according to what its account has preceded.
And if it is not healed, then there is no remedy for it: except that you should incise above the abscess at its origin, and draw out all the moisture which is in it and the pus, until the bone is uncovered.
When therefore the bone is uncovered, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called the head of the rough file.
Let it be made of untempered iron, and let its head be round like a chisel, which should be pointed with the point of a file, or with the point of an aliskilfer, with a fine point: and place it upon the corrupted part of the bone.
Then roll it between your fingers, and press your hand gently, until you know that that corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying and styptic medicines.
If therefore the place consolidates, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of pus is stopped, and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not fester, and no accident happens to it: then know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is nothing to do but to reduce the fistula to the nasal opening, according to this method.
And it is that you uncover the second bone with an iron or with a sharp instrument: and when the bone is uncovered, then take a drill of this form, let its iron end be triangular: and let its wooden part be a pyramid tapering to a point, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself: and let it be near the lacrimal duct: and move your hand a little away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the drill until it penetrates the bone, and the patient feels it: by this, that he finds wind coming out of it when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with drying styptic medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the place hardens: then the matter which was discharged externally, returns to the nose: therefore it is easier on the patient.
Concerning the reduction of a protrusion of the eye.
Chapter 20.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no harm or diminution occurs in vision: then it is necessary that you loosen the patient; then you bleed him; afterwards you apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the eye an ointment which is made from acacia, and aloe, and frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above with a plaster with many powders.
And the method of the powders is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth of them is doubled four times: make many of them: then place them one upon another, according to the quantity which the eye needs: then bind over them with a hard cloth with a strong binding.
And repeat the cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses, and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if the bandage is again loosened, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly: then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has returned to the tunic of the grape, it is good: and if not, then repeat the plaster, and the powders, and the binding, and the cupping glasses, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the grape tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the tunic of the grape, then it protrudes and comes out beyond the eyelids like a grape seed, and the man's appearance is disfigured because of it, and you wish to incise it: then the operation in it is, according to what I relate: and it is, that you insert a needle into the root of the grape from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle in which is a double thread from the lachrymal side, and make it penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then incise the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the grape around the needle; and tighten it very much.
Then withdraw the needle, and place on the eye wool infused in egg white; and leave it until the thread falls off with the grape.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the grape is constricted on every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds a large prominence.
Then it is perforated with a thin spatula, which reaches the depth of the eye, and the white humor flows out, and the eye descends, and immediately enters; then constrict it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnati.
Chapter XXII.
This sickness which is called Alcomnati is nothing but pus which gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is that you make the sick person sit on a prepared or straight seat.
Then take his head with your hands from both sides, and move it until you see the pus reach the lower parts of your eyes. Then it is fixed, and does not descend, and the light is resolved: and the sick person sees things as he saw: and if it does not descend lower, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula, and incise with it above the corneal membrane a little at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the circle, until the pus comes out.
When therefore it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then let it be treated with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water which descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already spoken of the types of water in the division, and what is suitable for perforation by declaring and explaining speech, then take that same thing according to the health.
Then therefore it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands squarely, opposite the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye, and bind it tightly: then the eyelid of his eye with your right hand, if the eye in which is the water is the left eye: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then take with your right hand an almond, if the eye is the left; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the end of the almond near the cornea by the thickness of a ray in the very apparent whiteness of the smaller lachrymal eye: then push the almond with force: and you revolve with it your hand, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you feel that the almond has now arrived at the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the almond's journey to the depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and that is the crown of the eye. For you will see them in the very pupil by the vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the corneal tunic.
Then place the almond up to the place where the water is. Then press it downwards one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the infirm person sees that upon which he opens his sight immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then remove it a second time, except that the almond comes out.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend: then extract the almond with ease: and you roll your hand gradually.
Then dissolve something of clear rock salt in water: and wash the eye inside with it. Then place outside the eye a whole pledget, or wool infused with rose oil and egg white: and bind with it the sound eye.
But we, and those who are of our time, place on it crushed cumin with egg white.
But if the almond does not obey you in entering the eye because of its hardness, because it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then it is necessary that you take a spatula, which is called Alberid, whose form is this.
Therefore pierce with it only the conjunctiva, and do not thrust your hand into the opening: and it is only to make a subtle way for the almond to its place.
Then insert the almond, as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, let a properly made bed be prepared for the patient, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room: and let him be forbidden all movements, and coughing: and give him food which soothes his nature: and let him not move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage remain in its place until the third day.
Then loosen it in that dark room, and test his sight: and make him see things. Then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that he do this at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision: rather, it is necessary that he avoid it; because water rises quickly through vehement gazing.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary to loosen the eye before the seventh [day]: and rectify it with that which soothes the abscess, until it is soothed: then restore its sight, and place on his face an oral [medication] by which his sight may be governed under it for a few days, he himself remaining in that dark room.
Then let him go out from the house gradually, and converse in his own houses.
And know that in the cautery the student is not excused from vision at times: for then let him dare to perform the operation.
And now indeed there has come to us a certain one of those who are from Alayrach, who said that there was made in Alayrach a perforated magdan, by which water is sucked.
Truly therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has made that: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of the kinds of magda, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand upon them.
Let them be made of bronze properly; and let their ends be triangulated with this same subtlety, and a sharp point.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the care of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes diverse fleshy growths are born in the nose, of which some resemble a scorpion with many legs; and of these there is cancerous flesh, stony, dark in color; and of these there is flesh that is soft, not dark in color.
Therefore, in that which of these fleshes is soft, not malignant, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make the infirm person sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open his nostrils, and throw a hook into that flesh; then draw it to the exterior, and incise what you have grasped of it with a thin, sharp spatula from one side, until you know that all the flesh has now been removed.
But if something remains of that whose excision is not possible, then shave it with one of the thin instruments easily, until nothing of it remains. But if the blood dominates you, or a hot abscess occurs, then oppose it as is necessary; or if it is of malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it until the blood is cut off, and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar and water, or wine, should be thrown into the nose after the incision. But if the nose is opened, and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that he is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that within it there is flesh grown in the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary to insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and place between each knot the amount of a finger, or less; and let the patient put one end of the thread into the nose with a probe, or with whatever is possible for him after he has made a similar azer; and let him pull his scent until it reaches the abscess, and comes out over his throat.
And indeed boys often do something similar to this in schools; and it is an easy thing for whoever wants to.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then take and remove the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread: and after wiping away the blood, put a pledget that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose; do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then finally put a lead tube in the nose for a few days, until it heals.
If, however, it needs treatment that dries, use that.
To make a head-purger, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or bronze: let it be similar to a small lamp, open like a shell: and its passage similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for making a head-purger should be full, open; to which there should be a handle at its end, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever you wish of juices and liquid things.
Chapter 25.
Often a polypus is born in the extremity of the nose, and it is magnified, and increased with the days, until its appearance becomes foul.
And because of that it is necessary that you cut it in the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate the whole of it. Then place on the location, either a cautery, or a burning medicine which stands in its place.
If therefore its incision is delayed, until it is magnified, then consider, if it is stony, hard, of a dark color, of little sensation: then do not touch it with iron: because it is a cancerous abscess.
For many times I have seen, he who incised such an abscess, and great harm happened from it to him who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not of a dark color, and you see that incision is possible in its entirety, then evacuate the diseased area, and incise it without fear, without dread: and treat the place with that which dries and constricts, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
Know that when a discontinuity occurs in one of these cartilages, the operation benefits little, except in certain men.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you ought to consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood; and then bring together the two lips of the wound with suture; then treat it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the discontinuity are already separated, and each part has become healthy: then it is necessary to scrape away all the part from its apparent skin until it bleeds. Then bring together the two lips with suture, and bind them, and powder over it dragon's blood and crushed olibanum: and place over the powders a piece of diachylon plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters: and leave it bound for two or three days. Then unbind it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads are cut off by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
The method of suture is to bring together the discontinuity, either with needles, as we have recounted in the suture of the belly: or bring together the discontinuity with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of knots from the lips.
Indeed, many people often have hard abscesses in their inner lips, similar to grains of barley: and some of them are smaller, and others larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the lip, and incise over each knot, and suspend it with a hook, and incise it on every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with powdered alum, until the bleeding stops.
Then let it be washed with vinegar, and let the place be treated with it, in which there is astringency, until the wounds heal.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of flesh grown over the gum.
Often flesh grows over the gum, which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore it is necessary that you suspend it with a hook; or hold it with forceps, and incise it at its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
Then place pounded egg white or other drying, styptic powders on the area.
But if that flesh returns after treatmentâ€”for it often returnsâ€”then incise it a second time and cauterize it; for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the treatment of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, foul crusts gather on the surfaces of the teeth, on the inner and outer parts, and between the gums; and sometimes they blacken, and become yellow and green, until corruption from them reaches the gums, and the teeth become foul through it.
Therefore you ought to make the patient sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth where you see crusts and things like sand, until nothing of them remains; and similarly do with the blackness, greenness, yellowness, and other things until they are clean.
If therefore that which is in them is removed from the beginning of the scraping, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second, and a third, until finally you achieve your purpose.
And know that a tooth needs scrapers of diverse shapes and many forms, according to what is prepared for your operation; because the scraper with which a tooth is scraped from below is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other shapes.
And these shapes of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you see.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the extraction of aching teeth.
It is necessary that you treat a tooth from its pain with all skill, and delay in its eradication. For there is nothing that can replace it when it is eradicated, since it is a sublime substance: until when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it is necessary, when the patient is compelled to eradicate it, that the aching tooth be steadied and made certain for you.
For often pain deceives the patient, and he thinks that it is in a sound tooth and eradicates it: then the pain does not depart, until the diseased tooth is eradicated.
For we have already seen this from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is made certain for you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is some virtue, until the gum is loosened on every side: then move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps on it with a good placement, and the patient's head between your knees: which you have now held well, so that it does not move: then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it on every side easily, and attempt its movement, as you did at first.
But if the tooth is perforated, or corroded: then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth: and bind it with the end of a thin rod, so that it is not broken at the time of your tightening on it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act exquisitely with scarification around the gum on every side abundantly.
And guard your strength, so that you do not break it, and part of it remains, and returns upon the patient from that malice which is greater than his first pain.
And beware lest you do what ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste, and boldness concerning its eradication, beyond what we have described.
For many times they cause to happen to men great infirmities, which are more difficult to break, and its roots all remain, or some of them; or that they extract it with some bones of the jaw: as we have many times testified to that.
Then let it be washed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
But if a flow of blood happens from the place (for it often happens), then grind something of zegi, and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if the zegi does not help you.
The shapes of the fine forceps with which the tooth is moved at first, should have long ends, a short handle, thick, so that they are not doubled when pressed with them upon the tooth: let them be like you see of thick handles, until when you squeeze with them, they do not injure themselves, nor double: of short ends, and let them be made of good iron, or of steel decently made, of straight ends: and at their ends let there be teeth, some of which enter into others, and grip with a decent, strong constriction.
And sometimes the ends are made like the shape of the mouth of a bird which is called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Concerning the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jaw bones.
When something remains from a broken tooth at its eradication, then it is necessary that you place a poultice with butter on the place for one or two days, until the place softens: then insert into it an elevator, or forceps; the ends of which resemble the mouth of a bird which is called a stork. Now this is the form of the forceps.
Let their ends be made like a file inside: or like *aliskilfegi*.
If therefore it does not yield to you to come out with these forceps, then it is necessary that you carefully expose the root, and uncover all the flesh, and insert the instrument which is called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be short in the extremity, somewhat thick: and let it not be soaked, lest it break.
If therefore the root comes out by means of that, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to help with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular at the end, in which there is a certain thickness.
The form of a triangular end, slender.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the matter of teeth.
Also let there be help with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, of this form, with a twisted triangular end, in which there is some thickness, so that it is not broken: and let it not be imbibed.
And know that the instruments for teeth are many: and similarly the remaining instruments are not counted, and a skilled craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself an instrument, according to what the operation and the illnesses themselves signify to him.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not mention an instrument, because of the diversity of their kinds.
But if a bone is broken from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or putrefies: then examine it in its place with that which is suitable to it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with a gest, whose form is this.
Let there be some gradual thickness in it, so that the bone may be tightened, or held with it, and not escape and go, until the bone comes out: and restore the place with medicines suitable to it.
But if there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then scrape it from its putrefaction, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Concerning the sawing of teeth prominent above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, the form is made foul thereby; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely, those who are for sale.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is grown after another tooth, and is not located, or its sawing or filing is possible, then eradicate it; and if it is joined with another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, of which this is the form.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of strong iron, very sharp at the tip.
And let your incision, by which you cut it, be in many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another of the teeth is not moved.
But if it is prominent and exposed to its filing, then file it with a file of iron, of which this is the form.
Let it be entirely of iron, and its handle of very small points, like a file with which needles are made; with which the tooth may be filed little by little in many days easily, so that the tooth is not moved and falls out; then smooth it, finally shave it with certain razors.
But if it is a tooth, from which a part is broken, and injures the tongue: then it is necessary that you file it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and it is leveled, and smoothed, and does not injure the tongue, nor impair speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Concerning the retention of teeth with gold threads.
When a concussion and shaking of the front teeth occurs from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats lest it fall out, and you have treated them with styptic medicines, and the treatment has not availed: then the method is that they be bound with a gold thread; for silver turns green and rots after days; but gold never changes.
And let the thread be medium in fineness and thickness according to the amount it should occupy between the teeth.
And the manner of binding is that you take the thread, and insert a double portion of it between two sound teeth; then weave with the two ends of the thread between the moved teeth, whether it be one or more, until you reach with the weaving to a sound tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving to the part from which you began; and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not at all removed; and let your constriction, with which you tighten the thread, be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not come loose.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the thread with scissors; and join both, and twist them together; and hide them between the sound tooth and the moved tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue; then let it remain so bound.
But if it loosens or is cut, bind them with another thread, and it will remain so for the whole time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of the binding of two sound teeth, and of two moved teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after the fall of both, and are retained as we have described, and remain; and this is done only by a skillful, prudent, and experienced craftsman.
And sometimes something from the bones of animals is fitted: and it is made into the form of a tooth, and is placed in the place, from which the tooth is strengthened and bound, as we have said: and it remains, and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue, is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound which has already healed.
And the operation in it is, that you open the mouth of the sick person at his head in your lap, and lift up his tongue: then incise that nervous ligament across, until the tongue is released from its restraint.
But if in it there is something of hardness and knotting, and it is from the healing of a wound: then throw into it a hook, and split it across, until you incise the ligament, and the knotting is released.
And beware, lest there be a fissure in the depth of the flesh, and you incise an artery there, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then let the place of the incision be washed with rosewater, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a linen pledget, which the sick person should hold throughout the night: so that it does not heal up again.
But if a flow of blood occurs, then place pounded alum on the place. If however the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a slow cautery, which is suitable for it; then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue which resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it is magnified so much that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in it is that you open the mouth of the sick person before the sun: and consider the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark or black color, hard, to which the sick person feels no sensation: then do not touch it, because it is cancer.
And if it inclines towards whiteness, in which there is moisture, then throw into it a hook, and incise it with a thin spatula, and free it from every part. But if blood overcomes you at the time of your operation, then place on it crushed alum until the bleeding stops: then return to your operation until you extract it with its entirety.
Then let it be washed with vinegar and salt: and treat it with the remaining appropriate treatment for it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes abscesses occur between the throat glands, that is, glands which resemble the glands which occur externally: and they are called the two tonsils: which when you treat with what we said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore the abscess is hard, dark in color, and has little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it matures. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break by itself.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is thin: then it must be incised.
And the procedure in it is that you consider before its operation, if the heat of its abscess has quieted completely, or is diminished by some lessening: then therefore have the infirm one sit before the sun, and let his head be in your lap: and open his mouth, and let a servant take [a tool] between his hands, and press his tongue down with an instrument, whose form is this.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which is thin like a knife.
When therefore you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is shown to you, and your vision falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can, except that you draw something of the tunics with it.
Then incise it with an instrument, whose form is this which follows. It is similar to scissors; however, let its ends be curved, so that the opening of each is directly opposite the other sharply: therefore take care that it is made of untempered iron, or imbued with alfuled.
But if this instrument is not present, then incise it with a spatula, whose form is this. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you incise one tonsil, incise the other according to this same kind of incision.
Then after the incision let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
But if a flow of blood happens to him, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and things similar to those from styptics, until the flow of blood is stopped: then take care of him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses are produced in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore incise them according to what I said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
