If however a hot abscess, or blood flow, or something else opposes you during the time of incision, or during the time of cautery, then it is necessary that you treat that which opposes you from it with the treatment which is suitable for it, according to what has been stated before.
But if the patient is timid, and does not await this operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I relate it: and it is.
When it is confirmed to you by what we have said about the signs, that the swelling is watery, then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a broad spatula; and place the perforation according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place a pledget over the place, and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water may run out; then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is opposition to the outflow of water at the time when you perforate it, and this is only because a white membrane opposes the opening of the wound and prevents the exit of the water, then it is necessary that you insert a feather into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of the dropsical is drawn out, the form of which has preceded; or add a little to the operation of the wound.
And know that the water returns and collects after six months, or thereabouts. When therefore it collects, then let it be perforated in the same way, and the sick person will pass his days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy fistulas.
Know that in the incision upon this fistula, there is fear leading to destruction in many conditions. Therefore, it seems to me that its dismissal is to be saved from it.
And I am recalling the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
Therefore I say that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are formed: and this happens from many causes, namely.
Either from an excess of something sharp or acrid, which is poured out to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it: and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation in it.
And sometimes it happens from a knotting and swelling of the arteries, as its mention has already preceded: or from a swelling of the veins.
Therefore, that which is from them from the swelling of the arteries, is known by the disposition of the abscess, when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary that you touch it completely.
That however which happens from the swelling of the veins, nothing is dispersed from it upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that a section is made upon this kind and upon the remaining flesh: and it is, that you cut the skin of the testicles. Then extend the egg upwards, and extract it with the white sifach: and free the suspensory ligament from the vessels, and ligate the vessels: and incise the suspensory ligament after you free it from every part of the parts of the egg.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those born [or, newly formed]  fleshes, then it is necessary that you draw out the egg, and incise it.
And if adhesion is between something of the siphaket, or in that which is between the vessels: then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion, and incise it with a round incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind: then it is necessary that you incise all of it, and extract the egg, as we said.
When therefore your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine: then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIIII.
Concerning the treatment of the ramic, which is with a vine.
A vine is a swelling enveloped in a certain wrapping, similar to a bunch of grapes, with softening of the testicles: and it makes movement and exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, there is fear, because of that which preceded its remembrance.
Truly it is necessary that we speak of the operation on it according to what the first ones did.
Therefore I say that it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat. Then push the suspensory of the testicles downwards. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, with the vessels which are near the penis: and let another assistant hold them, and extend them with strong extension.
Then incise with a broad, sharp spatula, a transverse section in the direction of the vessels, until the vessels are revealed.
Then flay from every part, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
Afterwards, insert a needle with double thread into them, and tie it in the first places where the veins meet: and tie it again in the last of them. Then cut them in the middle with a straight section according to the length of the body, and extract what is gathered from the foul, corrupt humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of the other wounds, in which the power to generate pus is present, so that the vessels which nourish one testicle fall away. For there is no harm through that.
But if a vein affects all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed with the vessels: lest the egg be deprived of nourishment, because of the incision, and waste away, or dry up, and there be no help with them.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal fistulas.
The outcome of this fistula is from a section which happens in the sifach extended over the abdomen in the groin region from the mirach of the belly.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one of the testicles. And this rupture is either from the sifach, or its extension.
And these two types happen from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or lifting a heavy thing, and similar things.
And a sign, when it is from the extension of the sifach. It is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and it is an equal abscess towards the deep; therefore, because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And its sign when it is from the section of the sifach, because it causes a vehement pain to happen from its beginning suddenly: and it is a diverse abscess, appearing under the nearby skin: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it exits with the intestine zirbum: and this is named intestinal Ramex, and zirbia: and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes dung runs into the intestine, and is retained there: and with it is the destruction of the infirm; because it causes a bad pain to happen, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the cure of the species of this illness with iron there is fear: wherefore it is necessary that the case be avoided in it.
And the method of operation is, that you order the infirm, that he reduce the intestine with his hand to the interior of his cavity, if it obeys return. Then he will be laid supine upon his neck between your hands: and raise his legs.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin to the upper parts, and cut the whole skin of the testicles lengthwise. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them: and let the incision be of such a size, that, if possible, the egg may come out of it.
Then flay the siphak, and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white siphak is uncovered, hard on every side: then introduce your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is under the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and absolutely loosen with it the adhesion which is in the posterior part of the egg.
Then move with the right hand to the interior the skin of the testicles: and with this extend the white siphak upwards, with the left hand: and raise the egg with the siphak to the part of the incision: and instruct the assistant to extend the egg upwards: and you loosen the adhesion which is behind with the solution completed.
And search with your fingers, so that there may be nothing there of the intestine involved in the hard white siphak. But if you find something of it, then push it down to the belly.
Then take a needle, in which is a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads: and introduce it near the last siphak, that is, of the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision: then cut the ends which project from the thread, until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some with a figure which is in the manner of a cross: and tie with them the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligature, until it cannot send anything to them from some of the vessels which nourish them: so that a hot abscess may not happen thence. And place another ligature again outside the first ligature, less than two fingers distant from it. And after these two ligatures leave from the siphak, which is under the skin of the testicles, a quantity the size of a finger: and cut away all the rest according to the roundness, and remove the testicle from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles an incision, from which blood and pus flows: as we have related in what has gone before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound: and apply the ligature which we have described. And sometimes the white siphak which we have mentioned is cauterized, after its incision, fearing the flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be moistened with warm water: and place on it that which we have mentioned, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the remaining treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Concerning a suction cup.
In this suction cup I have not seen anyone who dared to operate on it with iron.
And the first indeed said that it is done as we have narrated in the suction cup which is with a vine. That is, to tie the vessels after the incision upon it with ease below; then cut in the middle, and finally treat it with that which opens it, until the vessels fall away; and treat the wound, according to what we have said, until it heals.
Chapter 67.
Concerning the rupture which occurs in the groins.
When a rupture occurs in the groins, as we have said, and the place protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it descends, it is a little, and returns at all hours: truly if time is prolonged with it, a fissure is added in the sifach, until the intestines descend into the sifach.
And this happens from the extension of the sifach, which is in the groins, as we have said. That is, because the sifach is extended: then it softens, and the place suppurates, and protrudes.
And its cure is with cautery, as its account preceded.
And sometimes it is cured with iron, according to this method: and it is, that the sick man lies on his back between your hands. Then cut the place of the rupture of the abscesses and the protruding part, with a cut across, to the amount of three fingers.
Then constrict the alsifak and what are under the skin, until when the white sifach is uncovered, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a ray, and place it on the protruding part of the sifach, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew two protruding places on the end of the ray from the sifach, and connect one of them with the other with a suture; then withdraw the end of the ray.
And do not cut the alsifach completely, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, as I made you know in the treatment of intestinal fistula.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. Therefore, when you cut the threads, clean it, and restore the wound until it heals.
For the sisach is contracted, and no prominence is added to the rupture.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to healing.
Chapter LXVIII.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles is softened in some men, on account of which the flesh which is between them is softened, and its appearance is made foul.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is asked in its treatment, that he make the sick man lie on his back, and that he incise all the skin which is softened upon the natural skin.
Then he shall bring together the two lips with a suture: and if you wish, sew first the superfluity of the softened skin with three sutures, or four, and make the suture firm.
Then incise between the sutures.
Then treat with the treatment of the other wounds, until it is healed, and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law: and because of that it would be permitted to me not to mention it in this my book: and its mention is not necessary except for two reasons.
One of which is that it should be in the knowledge of the physician when he is questioned about it: and that he should know the treatment of him to whom it happens.
And the other reason is that we often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit through it, such as rams, he-goats, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore I say that castration is done in two ways. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and incision.
Now in that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is: that we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles are softened and relaxed and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear to the touch.
But in castration by fissure and incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament and cut above each testicle with one incision, until when the two testicles come forth: then incise them after you have skinned each one, and do not leave on them of the *sifak* anything except the fine *sifak* which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition: because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires coitus.
Then take care of the wound until it heals.
Chapter LXX.
Concerning the care of a hermaphrodite in two ways.
A hermaphrodite from men is according to two ways. One of them is because there appears in him that which follows the space, or in the skin of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a figure like the vulva of a woman, in which there is hair; and sometimes urine flows from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one kind, and it is the vulva of a woman above the pubis like very small male testicles, protruding outwardly: of which one is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of the three kinds, two from men, and one kind from women, is: because it is necessary that you cut away the added flesh, until the impression of them disappears. Then treat them with the treatment of other wounds.
In the second kind of men, however, from which urine comes out, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the frenulum, and of the protruding flesh in the vulvas of women.
The frenulum is perhaps added to the natural thing, until it is deformed and its appearance becomes foul; and sometimes it is magnified in some women so much that it is spread out like in men, and reaches even to coitus.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold the excess of the frenulum with the hand, or with a hook, and incise it: and do not go too far in the incision, especially in the depth of the root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
Flesh, moreover, which arises, is flesh which is born in the orifice of the womb, until it fills it; and perhaps it protrudes outwardly in the likeness of a tail; and on account of this some first call the ailment tailed. Therefore it is necessary that we incise it just as we incise the frenulum, and treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the imperforate vulva of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is, as it were, the imperforate vulva of a woman, or it is a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from a preceding illness; and indeed this happens either from added, naturally occurring flesh, or from a thin membrane, or a thick one; and it occurs either deep within the womb, or at its sides, or in its upper or lower part; and it prevents intercourse and conception and childbirth, and perhaps it prevents menstruation.
And its recognition is made by the midwife's examination, if the obstruction is apparently near.
But if it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is from a thin membrane, and is near the two labia: then hurry, and break it.
And it is [necessary] that you place little pillows on the two labia.
Then place the index fingers of your two hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then spread the two labia forcefully until the thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil, and place it on the area: and let the woman cohabit every day, so that the area does not heal in another way.
But if the membrane is thick, then it is necessary that you cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is of a fleshy nature, then suspend it with hooks and incise it: and have with you medicines which staunch the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead tube, so that the wound does not close quickly, keep it in place for several days: and administer a pledget of dry linen, then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
And sometimes the birth of other flesh occurs in the womb: therefore it is necessary that they be incised in this manner: provided it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess which is in the womb, should never be treated with iron.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red Bothor in the vulvas of women.
Hemorrhoids indeed are the opening of the orifices of the veins, until much blood flows from them constantly.
When therefore hemorrhoids become old, they become athelil: and I have already spoken in the division of their species, and their signs: and I will say here which of them receive treatment, and which do not.
I say therefore, that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses: then there is no treatment for it with iron.
But those of them which are in the orifice of the womb upon which the sense falls, are those which are cured.
It is necessary therefore that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the hemorrhoids with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them from their roots.
Then pulverize over them at the flow of blood from one of the incisive powders without biting: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar to them.
Then let him push her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
But if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it over her back.
If therefore blood is cut off with it, it is good: and if not, apply cupping glasses to the back and breasts, without scarification.
Then take wool and immerse it in the juice of tarthith, or the juice of arnoglossa, or the juice of the branches of alaulat, that is, aglanter, with Pontic wine, and apply it to the place.
When therefore the abscess is quiet, then treat the place with ointments until it is healed.
Albarth however is red, similar to the heads of alhaasse, and is of rough appearance.
Therefore it is necessary that you incise what appears of it, according to what I said in thelil equally: and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LXXIIII.
Concerning the perforation of the exit which happens in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and a stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and ragadiae, and thelil, and a hot abscess.
And indeed I have already described all these illnesses and their types, and their signs, and their treatments in the division: it is necessary that I describe in this treatise the hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is of the abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is done with iron.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is acute with its percussion, and inflammation, and fever: and you see it redden: if sensation falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation: and treat it with that which aids in maturation, until when the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat which surpasses two seats, and she will recline, or be laid on her back, and draw her feet to the lower part of her belly: and let her hips be spread, and let her place her arms under her knees, and bind each with a suitable ligature to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument by which the orifice of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix it with a probe to the depth of the woman's womb: so that that which enters from the instrument is no more than the depth of the womb, and harms the weak woman.
But if the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary that you place pledgets on the covering of the depth of the womb: so that the entrance of the whole instrument into the depth of the womb may be prevented: and it is necessary that he place the handle, which runs in the instrument, from the upper side: and that he hold the instrument, and that the attendant rotate the handle, until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and is touched by hand, and is smooth and soft: then it is necessary that you cut it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a pledget submerged in rose oil: or green oil, in which there is some stypticity: and place the pledget, and extract the section in the depth of the womb.
And place externally over the womb, and over the vulva, clean wool submerged in water in which mallows have been boiled.
Then let it be treated after two days with the ointment which is suitable for it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, so that it is clysterized with water and honey: then with water in which the root of the lily, or long birthwort, has been boiled: then it returns to the ointment.
But if the wound is hidden internally in the womb, then it is necessary that you abstain from its treatment with iron, nay, treat it with what we have said in the division.
Chapter 77.
The teaching of obstetricians, how live embryos, having emerged according to a non-natural form, are to be treated.
It is necessary for the obstetrician to know first the form of a natural birth.
Therefore, among its signs is that when you see a woman being compressed downwards, and she desires to smell the air, and that which is upon her of the birth is lightened, and the exit of the embryo is hastened: then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is head first, and the afterbirth with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
When therefore you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities with a good cleansing.
But that which is besides this birth, is unnatural, and not praiseworthy.
Because sometimes the fetus exits on its feet, and its body exits before its head, and its feet, and one of its hands, or one of its feet: or its head and its hands exit: or its belly exits: and perhaps it is turned over on its neck, and similar things from unpraiseworthy forms.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, skillful in all these things, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every form of them, and what its nature is, and is signified by it, and is relied upon.
When the fetus exits head first in its natural exit, and it becomes strenuous for the woman, and the birth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened: then make her sit on a seat, or let women hold her: and vaporize her vulva with a decoction of fenugreek, and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and let her break the secundine with it, and cut it with her nail, until that which is in it of moisture flows out: and press the woman's belly, until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman must be given a clyster of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then instruct her after the clyster, that she be compressed: and make her sneeze with conserve, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will exit immediately.
But if two hands of the fetus come out first, then it is necessary to gently replace them little by little. But if it does not obey on entry, then place the woman on a seat, and raise her feet upward; then shake the seat on the ground; and let the woman be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
Therefore if the hands do not come out, and the fetus dies: then incise them, and extract the remainder of the fetus: and bind its hand with a cloth, and draw it out evenly: for thus it will come out.
The exit of the embryo on its feet. When the fetus exits on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; then gradually turn the fetus; afterwards straighten it.
Then take one of its feet, and extend it gradually.
When therefore they come out as far as the hips, then instruct her to be compressed, and make her sneeze with snuff: for it will come out, if God wills.
And if it does not come out with what we have related, then gradually return the fetus until you place it according to its natural form. For then it will come out with an easy exit.
But if it is prevented with all that we have related: then take some marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum: and beat the whole in a mortar with a good beating: then anoint with it the vulva of the woman, and the lower part of her belly. Then make her sit in warm water until it reaches her hypochondria.
Therefore, when you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make her a pessary of myrrh: and apply it. When therefore the pessary has remained for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze: and compress her mouth and nose: and press the lower part of the belly with gentle pressure. For the fetus will come out immediately.
The exit of the fetus is over the knees and hands. When it comes out in this manner, then she will succeed in gradually inserting her hands. Then the woman will be laid on her neck, and her feet will hang downwards: and she will lie on the bed, and clasp her hands. Then press on her belly gradually. Then remove what is under her feet from the cushions, until she is prepared in the lower part. But if the fetus does not come out, then take its feet and move them with a strong movement. Then press what is above the hypochondria gradually, until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then let him insert his hands and equalize the fetus gradually: and instruct the woman to be compressed, until the fetus comes out.
The exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. Let his hand be used in the reduction. But if he cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
But if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed: and shake her legs with a strong shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you have prepared from mucilages.
But if labor takes her, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive to reduce the hand, and adjust the fetus according to its natural form: and make the woman sneeze, and instruct her to strain herself until it comes out.
The exit of the fetus is over its neck, with its hands outstretched, and its face is towards its mother's back. The midwife ought to hold the hand of the fetus, then gradually turn it, and adjust it easily.
Therefore, when it is adjusted, then place on her vulva that which we have described of oil and mucilages.
Then instruct her to move gradually. When therefore you have done that, then make her sit on a seat, and instruct her to lean to her right side. Then instruct her to be compressed; and make her sneeze with a sternutatory.
The exit of the prepared fetus on the side. When it descends to the orifice of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then tear the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it may not be retained, and die.
Then push the fetus inwards, until you place it as is necessary; and instruct the woman to compress herself several times: for it itself will exit with an easy exit.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born: and sometimes three, and four are born. However, that is unusual and rarely happens.
Five, however, is unnatural, and they do not fully live: and a single afterbirth contains them, however many there are: and they are separated by the sifach, distinguishing between them, connected at the umbilicus of each of them.
And sometimes they come out as a single fetus does, by a natural, easy exit; and they come out by a laudable exit, as we have narrated.
But if some of them are difficult at birth, and they arrive at what we have described from unnatural forms, then treat them as we have narrated: and let the midwife be skillful, and let her do what she does with skill and gentleness, and let her adapt to every form with what is prepared for her by skill leading to health.
Concerning that which is formed in the womb from embryos, and is aborted.
Sometimes one, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb: and it has been verified by me, that a woman aborting emitted seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
Which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore all this ought to be in the knowledge of midwives. For perhaps a similar thing will come to her: therefore let her oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I mentioned in the division concerning medication, and it does not come out, and you are forced to use an operation with the hand, then it is necessary that you consider.
Therefore if the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted with any of the accidents from which loss is feared for her: then it is necessary that the woman be laid supine upon the bed, upon her back, and her head be inclined lower, and her legs raised: and a servant should hold her from both sides, or she should be bound to the bed, so that her body is not drawn out during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with moist oils with mallow mucilage, and fenugreek, and linseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages: then let her introduce it into the womb with ease. Then let her seek with it the place in which to fix hooks on the fetus, and the suitable places for that.
Consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head: then fix hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its clavicle, or in places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is upon its feet, then in its pubis, and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand: and place its curve in that which is between the fingers of her left hand: and introduce it with her hand with ease, and fix the hook in some of those places which we have mentioned, until she arrives at the empty thing.
Then let her fix in opposition to it another hook, or a third, if she needs: so that the traction is equal, and does not incline to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let the extension not be according to straightness only, but rather let it be moved to every part, so that its exit is eased: just as is done with a tooth at its extraction.
And it is necessary that that which is between them be softened, so that the extension is easier. But if a part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some fingers with oil, and introduce them from the side, so that she may roll out with them that which is retained.
If therefore a part of the fetus comes out, then let the hooks be moved to another place which is higher: do this until the whole fetus comes out.
If therefore a hand comes out before other things, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until, when it comes out completely, you incise it either at the joint of the sole or at the elbow; and thus you must do in the other hand and in the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or a constriction happens to it in its exit, or water is collected in its head, then it is necessary that she insert a thorny spatula between the fingers, and split the head with it, and extract the water; or break it with the instrument which is called Almisdac, whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is after this.
Similarly, if the fetus is by nature large-headed, then it is necessary that you cut the algengematu; or extract it with the misdach, as we said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
But if the head comes out and is constricted at the clavicle, then let it be split until the moisture which is in the chest is poured out.
But if it is not entangled, nor yields to exit, then incise the head piecemeal, according to what is possible for you.
But if there is an abscess in the lower part of the belly, or dropsy is in it, then it is necessary that you perforate it until the moisture runs out of it.
If however the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the womb is light.
But if it is constricted at the belly or chest, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you have wrapped around your hand: and split the belly or chest until that which is in them runs out.
But if the remaining limbs are removed, and the head returns, then let the midwife insert her left hand. Therefore if the mouth of the womb is open, then let her insert her hand into the depth of the womb, and search with it for the head, and draw it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb; then let her insert into it one hook, or two hooks, and draw it with them.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, because of a hot abscess which has happened to it, then it is not necessary that labor should be undertaken on it; rather it is necessary then to administer the effusion of moist, oily things, and apply plasters, and to sit in waters which soften and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it may be straightened; then let that which we said concerning a living fetus be administered.
But if that is not possible, then let it be cut piecemeal, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave anything at all of the afterbirth inside.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, then administer what we said concerning hot abscesses which occur in the womb.
But if a flow of blood occurs, then let it descend into styptic waters, and administer the remaining cure.
And I indeed already saw a woman, in whose belly a fetus had died; then she was impregnated above it a second time, afterward another fetus also died. Therefore, after a long time an abscess happened to her in her navel, and it swelled until it opened: and it began to emit pus.
Therefore I was called to her cure, and I treated her for a long time: and the wound was not consolidated.
Therefore I placed upon it some plasters of strong attraction: and a bone came out from the place. Then several days passed for her, and another bone came out. Therefore I wondered at this, since there is no bone in the belly.
Therefore I thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
Therefore I investigated the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in better condition: and she now lived thus for a long time, and discharged a little pus from the place.
And I did not establish this extraneous matter here, except because there is knowledge in it, and help for that which a skillful physician administers with the hand in treatment.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the forms of instruments necessary in the extraction of a fetus.
The form of the pivot by which the matrix is opened.
Here below is the form of a constrictor, by which books are bound, which equally has two pivots at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this pivot ought to be more slender than the constrictor; and it should be made either of ebony or boxwood.
And let the width of each piece of wood be about two fingers; and the edge of each of the two is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are now fixed to both, whose length is half a palm more or less; and the width of each is about two fingers, or more or less.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the matrix, so that it opens with them when you turn the two pivots.
Another form similar to that one, more slender and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity let there be two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each addition is about a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore when you wish to open the matrix with it, then make the woman sit on the bed, with her legs hanging down, open what is between her legs. Then insert these two joined additions into the matrix: and you are holding the lower extremity of the instrument between her hips. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with scissors, equally according to the amount you wish of the opening of the mouth of the matrix, until the midwife does what she wants.
The form of another Vertigo which the ancients called the Impelling Form by which the fetus is impelled.
The form of the Almisdac, by which a large head is crushed: and it is similar to forceps, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is oblong, like forceps according to this form, as you see, which has teeth like saw teeth, with which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook having one point.
The form of a hook having two points.
The form of a spatula having two ends for cutting off the head.
The form of two broad spatulas also.
All these instruments are practiced, and the more their types are multiplied and prepared by the craftsman, the quicker he is at his work, and the more highly regarded among men, because of their quantity.
Therefore do not despise any of these instruments, but let them be prepared for you. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the extraction of the secundines.
When the secundines are retained after childbirth, then it is necessary that you instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it comes out by this, it is good.
If not: then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid; and put into it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as calamus, rue, and dill, and chamomile, and seseli, and cassia lignea, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them with water, and put them over the fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole in the lid of the pot, and the other end into the mouth of the womb; and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will come out easily.
But if it remains after this treatment, and does not come out: then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her insert it into the vulva, and search for the afterbirth with it. When therefore she has found it, then let her take it, and extend it gradually.
But if it is attached deep in the womb, then let her insert her hand, as we have described, until when she finds the afterbirth, and pull it out gradually according to the straightness, so that the womb does not fall during forceful traction: rather it is necessary that it be moved easily to the right and left sides: then let the amount of traction be increased. For then it obeys, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing, and with medication from a pot with herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have described, then beware lest you tire yourself in repeated extraction.
Truly it is necessary that what comes out to the mouth of the womb from it, be tied to the woman's hip. Then give her a clyster with tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will putrefy it after days, and it will dissolve, and it will come out.
However, when it putrefies, a bad odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and harms the weak woman. Therefore, suitable fumigations ought to be administered to it.
Some of the ancients, however, have already tested the fumigation of nasturtium and dried figs.
The form of the instrument by which a woman is fumigated for the retention of the secundines and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to an alembic, or of brass: and let the wide end be placed over the fire: and let the fumigation be placed upon the coals: let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed: and repeat another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus.
Many boys are born, and their anuses are imperforate, which a subtle membrane has already obstructed. Therefore, it is necessary that the midwife perforate that membrane with her finger: and if not, let her perforate it with a sharp spatula: and let her beware of the muscle, lest she touch it.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil upon it. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will become obstructed, then place a lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the boy wants to defecate.
And now there occurs an obstruction of the anus also from the healing of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore it is necessary that that consolidation be cut, then treated with what we have said of treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part, are a knotting, and a swelling which occur near the anus externally: or in the space of one part: and it is one fistula, or several.
When therefore that knotting becomes old, it is opened, and a watery white moisture, or thin pus, flows from it.
And sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore penetrating ones are known by that which comes out with the excretion and flatulence, when the sick person uses excretion: and perhaps worms come out of them.
And some of these fistulas, when they are penetrating, extend to the bladder, and to the urinary passage. And some of them penetrate to the hip joint and to the coccyx.
However, what distinguishes a fistula penetrating to the anus from one that does not is that you insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe into the fistula, made of air or iron, when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
But if you do not feel it completely, nor does anything like excretion, flatulence, or a worm, as I said, come forth from the opening, then know that it is not penetrating: therefore hasten to its cure.
And when a fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary meatus, then its indication is the exit of urine from it, and the prevention of its consolidation in this place with medicines.
If however it penetrates to the hip joint or to the hip bone, then its sign is that a probe reaches there, if there is no tortuosity in it to the bone, and the discovery of pain around the part, and that pus always comes forth from it, and that medication does not work in it, nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all; and their cure is labor and vanity for that foolish physician upon whom it is inflicted.
However, in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I relate to you, and I have already proved it: and it is so that the patient lies between your hands on his back, and you raise his legs above his hips, and his hips are inclined towards his belly. Then insert a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
But if the patient feels it around the anus, then you ought to insert your index finger into the anus.
Therefore if you feel the probe has already penetrated by itself, detected by your finger, beyond what you feel between your finger and the fistula itself or the flesh: then you know for certain that it is penetrating: therefore you do not labor over it, since there is no cure for it, as we have said.
However, they have said that in some men the flesh heals, and of the medication by which help is hoped for it, is to heat a fine cautery according to the size of the fistula, as preceded, and to insert it burning into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat this twice or thrice, until you know that all those added and filtered flesh-like tissues, which are similar to a bird's quill, have been cauterized.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, until those cauterized tissues come out; then treat it with ointments that generate and consolidate flesh.
If therefore it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it never heals with any other treatment.
And if a probe emerges and does not reach your finger which is in the anus, and there is a thick covering of flesh or fat between it and the probe, and you see a fistula in what follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed cutting with the probe which is in the fistula, until the incision reaches where the end of the probe reaches, and the probe is freed and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered tissues, which resemble the quill of a bird's feather, so that nothing of them remains.
But if blood overcomes you and prevents you from operating to cut those tissues, then it is better, and of quicker help, to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because cautery with fire brings together good conditions, the burning of those added tissues, and the cutting off of blood, and the drying of humors.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, or with crushed cabbage leaves, until the place discharges pus, and those burnt tissues come out in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which generate hard flesh: and these are medicines in which there are stypticity and drying.
For when they are restored, then it is healed: and you do not fear a recurrence.
But if a fistula has already reached to the depth of the anus, and is extended by the superfluity of the body: then insert your finger into the anus, and search with it.
If therefore you feel a probe, and between you and it is a covering of fat or flesh, and it is near penetration: then there is no operation in that, except according to the way of trust and hope.
That is, because one of three methods is administered to it: either to cauterize it, as we have said: or to excise it, until you obtain its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert a linen cloth, and treatment from nearby. And do not proceed far with the incision, lest you cut the muscle containing the anus, and there happens to the patient an involuntary discharge of excrement. Then treat it with what we have said.
For perhaps it will heal: or you will lengthen that covering, and cleanse it after you have cut it up to near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp extremity. Then cleanse those filtered flesh which are in the incision, as far as you can with exploration.
Then attempt the restoration of the whole wound with suture, and the upper opening where it consolidates: and a fistula remains open in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier on the patient.
And sometimes a fistula is sutured in this manner: and it is, when you insert a probe into the fistula: and it is in the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the skin and margin of the anus: then take a probe of perforated extremity, which is like an aleskef needle, that is, of those preparing hides, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wound from five threads, and around it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its depth.
If therefore it is penetrating into the margin of the anus internally nearby, then pull the thread out from that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and bring together the two ends: and tighten each, and leave it for one day, or two days: and the more the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well, until those flesh which are between the two ends of the thread are cut, and fall away: then treat the wound, until it consolidates, and heals.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating: then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not in your power to do this because of the muscle, so that you do not cut it.
Then do with it as we have said, until it is healed.
The shape of the spatulite with which the fistula is split, whose curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision: and the treatment of fissures.
There are hemorrhoids according to two methods. For they are either within the anus, similar to bladders of coals, or plums; or as if they themselves were grape seeds. And of them there are small ones and large ones: and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those which are outside the anus are, for the most part, few in moisture, from which flows a yellowish water, or a little blood with a constant flow: and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is that you instruct the patient to strain; and let it be pressed out until it leaves the anus, and the athelil appear to you. Therefore, also suspend it with hooks; or hold them with your nail. Then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks do not hold them, because of their moisture and softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a pledget of woolen fabric, and pull them out with your fingers. Then incise them, and sprinkle on them after incision certain hot powders, that they may stand in place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
But if the anus does not obey evacuation, then administer an enema to the patient in which there is a little bite, so that what is in the anus may be washed out with it, and it will obey evacuation quickly when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are external to the anus, then their matter is slight, easy: and it is so that you take them with your nail, or take them with a hook, and incise them. Then treat them with what we said, until they are healed.
And whoever shrinks from incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture according to this method. Take a wound thread, and insert it into a needle. Then draw the hemorrhoid upwards, and make it penetrate with the needle at its root from the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, in the lower part of the needle, and let them be crosswise: and bind the hemorrhoid with a strong ligature. Then knot the thread and remove the needle.
Do this with all the hemorrhoids, and leave one of them: nor should you cauterize it, that an excess of blood may flow from it.
Then place on the anus a cloth infused in rose oil, or cerate, and place it over the anus: and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave it until it falls off.
When therefore the hemorrhoids fall off, treat them with ointments and the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of fissures. For very often they occur from the dryness of the stool and the constriction of nature.
Therefore, when they become old and medicine is ineffective in them, then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your nail until they become moist and the upper layer which prevents their healing is removed from them. Then treat them until they heal as is necessary.
But if they do not heal, then scrape them again with greater force than before, until they bleed much and are excoriated; then treat them until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted almirmar, and of dry athelil, and of formica.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that almirmar is nothing but a round nodule, the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it occurs in the whole body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain from them occurs during walking.
Therefore it is necessary that what is around the nail be cut, and held with forceps, or with a hook, and eradicated from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, however, there is a small, rough, filtered, round prominence on the surface of the body. And they often occur in the hands of children.
And its treatment is that you extend the athelil and incise it: or tie it with a silk thread: or with hair, until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was stated with fire, or with a sharp medicine: and now inverted athelil, and non-inverted, are eradicated with the instrument which I shall describe.
An ant, moreover, is also a small, filtered, thick prominence on the surface of the body, going very deep: and when cold finds it, something like the bite of an ant is felt in it, and it occurs in the whole body: and it occurs most in the hands.
And its treatment is that you take a tube from a goose quill, or a strong vulture quill: and place that tube over the ant, until the circle of the tube contains some of it. Then roll your hand with the tube until you cut what is around the ant: and place it deep in the flesh, and eradicate the ant from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a tube of bronze, or iron, according to this method.
Let the top of the tube be of a height drawing a rolled-up deafness, so that its grip and its rolling on the finger may be eased.
And if you wish, cauterize the ant according to what was said before in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter LXXXIII.
Concerning the forms of instruments which are used for clysters in diseases of the anus, and for looseness of the bowels, and colic.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or of brass, or of cast or hammered bronze.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments, small and large, according to those in whom they are administered.
Therefore that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small: and of those whose anuses are weak or painful, so also their clysters.
A large clyster.
Its tube, that is, the upper head in which the bladder is tied, should be wide in this manner: and let it have a distinction so that the bladder may be tied as you see, and its lower extremity which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt, that is, not open, very smooth, tapering to a point: and on one of its sides let there be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And let the size of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker, and let the bladder in which the medicines are placed be of an animal's bladder: or of sheepskin, which should be according to the form of a small asofratus: whose root is one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes as it is rolled up, and let the space which is between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes strong thread of raw thread, which is made of ten threads, or thereabouts, and gather with it the bag like an asofratus. Therefore, when the bladder is placed in it, let it be tightened at the head of the clyster between two distinctions with a strong constriction. Then clysterize with it.
And this is the form of the bladder here depicted, and of the holes.
Moreover, the method of clysterizing with it is this: that you fill the bladder or vesicle with medicine, which is warmed according to the amount the infirm person can tolerate; and beware lest it be very hot, or very cold.
Then the infirm person is laid on his back, and he raises his legs upwards; and place under him an inthaan, that is, a leather on which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or the like. Then insert it into the anus gently. Then let the attendant take the bladder with both hands together, and squeeze it forcefully until he evacuates all that is in the clyster into the intestine.
Then withdraw the clyster, and let the infirm person raise his legs to the wall.
If the medicine compels him to defecate, then let him hold it back as much as he can. And if he sleeps with it the whole night, it is more ultimately helpful.
But if you administer a clyster to him for diseases of looseness of the bowels, and his evacuation is hasty, then repeat the clyster until he is healed.
But if there are haemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very subtle, gentle, from among those clysters with which children are clysterized.
And this is the form of a subtle clyster over which the skin is tightly drawn.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have already spoken in the division concerning the treatment of wounds which was fitting for the book, and I will here describe their treatment with the hand and with medicines according to the supplement.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to that by which wounds are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
Therefore, the things by which wounds are made are many times such as the throwing of a stone, or the incision of a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance or stick or arrow, and similar things from many objects.
Moreover, wounds which are made according to the places of the body are such as a wound which falls upon the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or liver, and similar members.
And I am relating the treatments of certain wounds, so that I may place that reasoning and rule over the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, since the consideration of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound happens in the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it is from the throw of a stone, or similar things, and it has now broken only the skin: and it is a large wound, and you fear concerning the unhealthy outcome of a hot abscess: then hasten, and immediately phlebotomize the cephalic vein: and do not delay it: and draw from him blood according to the quantity of his strength: if nothing is prohibiting phlebotomy, especially if the blood which exits from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the patient beware of repletion, and wine, and coarse foods.
But if something prohibits bloodletting, then let him be lessened in food, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And if a hot abscess happens to it, place on the wound a cotum infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
But if you are secure from a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is fresh with its blood, and the air has not changed it, the powder which I shall relate shortly: and if the air has already changed it, then place on it certain ointments, until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is from the incision of a sword, and the like: and its lips do not come together by means of lint, then bring them together with a suture, according to what I shall relate in the wounds of the belly concerning their suture.
But if the wound is already laid bare from the bone, and hangs down, and there is no retention of it except in a small suspensory: then incise it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore in it hard flesh in place of skin, and instruct the patient to be nourished with foods in which there is thickness, such as are heads [of grain], and boiled wheat, until it is like pottage: and things like these.
But if you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone: then incise all the putrefied part, and throw it away: then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows: and it is not cut off by medicines, then investigate the artery. Therefore if you find it uncut, then cut it with a Spatula, or tie it. But if necessity demands when that which we have said does not help, then cauterize it, until the bleeding is stopped.
But if these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, light: so that you sprinkle this powder on them: because it consolidates them before the air changes them.
Method of incarnative powder. Take one part frankincense, and two parts dragonâ€™s blood, and three parts of unslaked lime: let the whole be ground finely and sifted: and let the wound be filled with it, and bound tightly, until it adheres well to it, and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it above with lint, and leave it, and do not unbind it while it remains, so that it may not fester. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes the lime alone does this, when you do not have frankincense and dragonâ€™s blood at hand; and the lime also performs the same operation in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to apply it and bind it, and they are fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
But if the air has already altered them in some way, especially in summer, then this powder is not suitable: therefore treat it with what we have said, so that you apply certain digestive ointments: or apply poultices of barley flour made with water and honey, until they discharge pus. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with a forceps: and I shall now describe the treatment of head fractures in what is to follow.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
If, however, a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in its treatment and that of a head wound when it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been cut, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to be done except to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as verdigris and vitriol, and similar things. Because they harm the nerves and contract them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is the most superior of the members; and let that which cures it be from mild medicines, such as lime washed with fresh water poured over it several times with oil, or with rose oil, and tutty, when it is also washed with fresh water; and the remaining mineral stones, in this way, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer a suture; or draw together its lips with powders. Then treat it until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus is already collected, then perforate it in the most depressed place in it.
But if an artery has been cut in the wound, and a flow of its blood has resulted, then separate or cut it and tie it; or cauterize it, if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the throat, or the whole of it, have been injured, and the al-haudegi have been saved, then bring together the edges of the skin with a suture over the tube of the throat, and do not touch the throat itself; rather, level it and restore it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for several days. Then treat it with ointments which possess astringent and drying properties, until it is healed.
But if the wound has made an impression on the bone, then investigate it: then if there are fragments of bone in it, extract all of them, or as many as you can: and what is not easily removed, leave it until the wound festers, and the fragment separates and its removal is facilitated.
But if the wound is fresh, then sprinkle on it the powder which I have described. But if bone is seen in the wound, and a fistula forms, then treat it with that which was mentioned in its chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a wound from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider. Therefore if air escapes from the wound when the sick man breathes, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it is not deep, and is fresh with its blood, then do not apply powder to it at first, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the sick man; but apply an attractive ointment, and dissolve it twice a day.
However, if you do not have ointment, then place old lint in the opening of the wound, so that it may absorb what comes from it in terms of moisture; and make the sick man sleep on the wound, so that what gathers in it may flow out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wounding, and the sick man has not experienced spasms, nor a bad tremor of the heart, nor shortness of breath, and you see his other conditions to be good, then know that the wound is safe. Therefore treat it with linen cloths and the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it always opens up again, then know that a fistula has now formed. Therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife cut, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what preceded concerning suture, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone, and fragments have fallen from it, then explore the wound and hasten to extract those fragments, if they are separate. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound putrefies, for their extraction is thereby eased.
The judgment in the treatment of the other wounds occurring in the other members is the same as that which we have described.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, and the protrusion of the intestines, and their exit.
The rupture which happens to the belly is great, moderate, and small.
Therefore, from a large one sometimes an intestine, or a number of intestines, comes forth.
Therefore their replacement and their suture is more forceful and more difficult.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. It follows therefore that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not hasten in their replacement to their place immediately: because it is inflated, opened, and thickened: wherefore its entry becomes difficult.
Therefore, on account of this, a middle rupture of ruptures is better: because with it the reduction of the intestine does not become difficult, as it becomes difficult in those two types.
Know that the suture of the abdomen is according to four methods.
Two common sutures are suitable for the suture of the abdomen, and for the suture of the other wounds of the body: and two other sutures are proper to the other wounds. Nevertheless, they are more proper with the suture of the abdomen: and I relate to you each one with explanation and clarification.
I say therefore, that when the wound is small, and something of the intestine protrudes from it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it: then that is because of one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we said: Or because there is inflation in the intestine because of the coldness of the air.
When therefore it is so, then it is necessary that you warm it, so that you submerge a sponge, or a cloth moistened in tepid water alone. Or boil in water squills, and cyperus, and spica, and embrocate the intestine with it, until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does this: and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow, or mallow has been boiled. For by it its entry is eased with a small thing.
But if its return is excused after this cure, then incise a little in the rupture, with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first cure is better when it is prepared, than incision: and coercion to incise is not done after necessity and failure of the instrument.
Let one part of it which is twisted be sharp, and the other part of it not sharp, and let the thin end not be with the thinness of a spatula, indeed let it be a little blunter: and it is that which is similar to a cautery, as you see.
Therefore when the wound is widened, and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its proper place to itself, if it is possible above it: because it is better.
When however the rupture is large in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back, and put his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the belly, then put your intention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which is the wound be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds and in medium wounds.
In small wounds however, put it according to what is prepared for you.
Then cause a devout assistant to be present between your hands, who holds the whole rupture with his hand: and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it to him who follows the suture something after something: and this is the method of the common suture of one.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the quantity of the size of the wound. Then leave from the end of the rupture the quantity of the ring finger, that is, of the physician: and fix one needle except that you insert into it thread in two extremities of the skin, with two extremities of the sifac which is under the skin from the insides, until you cause it to penetrate from the other side, and you have now brought together two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the sifac: and four rows are made.
Then tighten with doubled thread around the needle several times from the two parts together, until the two lips of the wound are joined with decent joining.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also, and fix in them another needle; afterwards tie it with thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease doing that with as many needles as you need, until you complete the constriction of the whole wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness: because thin needles very quickly cut the flesh: and thick ones are difficult to enter the skin: on account of that it is necessary that they be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in two extremities of the skin very close together: lest the flesh be quickly cut, and the wound be opened before its consolidation, and they not be elongated with the suture, lest the wound be prevented from consolidation.
Then cut off the point of the needles, so that they do not injure the patient in his sleep: and place on them little cushions of cloth on every side, which hold the points of the needles: and leave them until you know that the wound is now consolidated.
And indeed this kind of suture with needles is more suitable in small wounds. Because one needle, or two, or about that many, suffice in their suture.
The second suture however is common, so that you bring together with the suture four extremities: two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the fascia at one time, with a needle in which is wrapped thread equal in fineness and thickness.
Then when you have penetrated with the needle these four extremities, return the needle from the side from which you began the same, so that the drawn thread falls from the upper part of the wound: so that the suture is like the seam of a cloth by which cloths are sewn together, and place between each suture the amount of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture men know.
And with this suture I sewed a wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm: and there protruded from the intestines about two palms of the middle intestine, and the disruption was in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I returned it after its intestine had stood outside the wound twenty-four hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in the space of about fifteen days: and I treated him, and he was healed: and he lived after that many years, engaged in his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that he would not be healed.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor anything of medicines, was found. Therefore I placed old lint on the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
But I shall describe two kinds of suture proper to the subject.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture: and you insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin, and the muscle going according to the length and breadth of the whole body: leave the end of the sifac on the side in which you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate to the other end from the inside to the outside at the other end of the mirach.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate accordingly in the same end of the mirach from the outside to the inside: and leave the end of the sifac, which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate to the other end from the inside to the outside: and make it penetrate with your penetration with which you make it penetrate into the sifac at the end of the mirach, which is in its part, until it penetrates into the whole of it.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the end, which is of the sifac on the other side, and draw the needle out of the skin which it penetrated.
Then return it to that skin, and sew the end of the sifac, which is on the other side, with this end of the mirach: and draw it out of the skin which is on its side, and do that once, and again: and do it turn by turn, until you sew the whole outside according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that you sew the suture of the leatherworkers as they sew leathers: so that you sew the sifac once from one side with one end of the skin: and that you leave it another time from the other side, until you complete it.
The second kind of proper suture, which Galen mentioned.
This speech of his is narrated, so that you sew it according to the likeness in which some physicians sew, so that they bring together each part to its corresponding part naturally suitable: and they apply the end of the sifac to its other end, and the end of the mirach to its other end. And that is done as I tell you.
It is necessary that you fix the needle in the end of the mirach near to you from the outer part, and make it penetrate to the inner part in that alone: and leave the two ends of the sifac. Then turn the needle, and make it penetrate from the outer part to the inner part in the two ends of the sifac, namely on both sides.
Then turn both, also make them penetrate from the inner to the outer, into the two other ends of the mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is the suture whose mention preceded.
This is therefore also the word of Galen.
And some said. The suture of the abdomen is straightened with two needles. That is, you insert one thread into both, and begin with the insertion of the needle, from what is near you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew hides equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen.
The judgment, however, of the medication of the wound, is the judgment of other wounds.
That is, because when you find a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine, and its own, and fit it, then put on incarnative powder.
But if the air has already altered the wound, then put on it certain ointments which are present to you, until they produce pus, and the threads fall out, and the sifac and mirach consolidate. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it heals.
But if you do not have medicines present, then immediately put on it, from when it begins to emit pus, old lint; and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it heals.
For you do not need other medication in most instances, when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound communicates with the principal members in the injury, then it is necessary that you submerge soft wool in oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil, and put it around the areas which are between the groins and under the armpits.
But if he feels pain, or pricking, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens), then clysterize him with astringent, black, warm wine; especially if putrefaction has already reached the intestine and has become an exit penetrating to its concavity.
And know that what is from the large intestine is of easier healing, and what is from the small intestine is of more difficult healing.
