Then it should be bound above it with a ligature, with a moderate constriction. Then phlebotomize transversely little by little, neither laterally nor lengthwise: and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be such that you phlebotomize slightly above the joint of the hand.
If the flow of blood is prevented, then put the hand back in warm water, and let the blood flow into the water, until you obtain what you need.
But if it is in the summer months, then you are excused from putting the hand back into warm water. And indeed, the letting of blood into warm water is mostly done in wintertime.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve beneath it.
Phlebotomy of the Salvatella vein of the right hand, however, benefits liver diseases.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that you bind the little finger of the hand with a ligature, or with your hand, after you have put it in warm water, until the vein swells and is clearly seen. Then phlebotomize it transversely. And if you cut it completely, it will not harm it in any way.
And take care not to overextend your hand with the phlebotomy instrument. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers bared of flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein, and will be prevented from flowing.
Therefore, when you have drawn out the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein: so that it does not close quickly.
And similarly it is necessary that you do with every constricted branch.
Truly, the help of phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it contributes to the illnesses of the spleen; and similarly do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second equally.
Moreover, the help of phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is for illnesses which are in the lower part of the body, such as illnesses of the womb, and retention of menses, and weaknesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the infirm person puts his foot into warm water: and makes friction upon it, until the veins swell; and tie above the joint of the foot with a strap. And indeed the place of the vein is near the heel, appearing towards the great toe; and many branches ramify from it in the surface of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or near the heel in its collection; because it is better and safer.
But if you phlebotomize it in the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy, by which you phlebotomize it, transversely, as if you wish to cut it; and let the phlebotomist be skillful.
But if the exit of blood is prevented, then immerse his foot in warm water: and let the blood flow into it, until it is evacuated.
But if the phlebotomist misses the vein at the first attempt, then let him repeat with the phlebotomy a little higher, for the place is safe, and you do not fear harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the sciatic veins, as we said, is near the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy is for pain in the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the patient should enter a bath and hurry; and bind his leg from the side of the hip to above the heel with four fingers' breadth, with a long, thin band; for it does not appear otherwise.
When therefore it appears, then phlebotomize it according to whatever method is possible for you, or according to its course; and it is better, or to incise it with an incision, or to cut it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most men.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most men. But if you do not find it, and it does not appear to the sense at all: then phlebotomize one of its branches, and that is the one which appears on the dorsum of the foot, and towards the ear; and beware of the nerves, and let the quantity of blood you wish flow.
Then loosen the binding, and place a leather over the place of phlebotomy, and bind the place. For it heals quickly.
Concerning cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from air, and from glass.
And cupping is done in two ways. With scarification and extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And there are fourteen places on the body for cupping which is administered with scarification and bloodletting. Of which one is, the cupping of Alnocrati, and it is the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck: and the cuppings of Alhagdahim, and they are two plates of the neck on both sides together: and the cuppings of the chin, and it is under the jaw in the lower part of the mouth: and the cuppings of the two shoulder blades: and the cuppings of Alachosos above the end of the spine: and the cuppings of the two elbows, and they are the middle of the two arms: and the cuppings of the two thighs: and the cuppings of the two calves.
And cupping indeed does not draw blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that strength does not fall from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. Nor is it necessary that cupping be administered either with fire or without fire in any illnesses which occur from repletion, until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts cupping because of illness or habit, we administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
Which is because there are men in whom blood multiplies until it needs to be extracted by cupping; they find heaviness and headache in their head.
And some of them find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And some of them find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And some of them scratch the place of their cupping. And some of them laugh excessively. And some of them find the taste of blood in their mouth; or their tongue is ulcerated, and they spit blood. And some of them sleep excessively. And some of them see in their sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to them.
Therefore, when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order venesection to be performed, after two or three hours of the day have passed.
Now, the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that it helps with heaviness in the head and that which is poured out into the eyes. But truly it is necessary that this be after evacuation of the whole body.
And indeed this cupping is in place of cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted that one who is of cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh, use it. For it brings him great harm.
And because of that it is not necessary that the elderly use it, and those in whose head are cold illnesses.
But he who is assiduous over it, forgetfulness is generated upon him.
And because of that it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, that he put down his hand with the cupping glass a little to the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Cupping or Alkehel is in place of the median vein phlebotomy, and the basilic vein phlebotomy, and because of that it benefits asthma, and constriction of breath, and fissure of the instrument of breath, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel cupping glass be applied lightly, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping, however, alhagdahim, benefits pains occurring in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of the basilic vein phlebotomy.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, lest he press his hand with the scarification: lest he cut an artery, and a flow of blood happen.
Cupping, however, under the chin, benefits canker sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar illnesses which are in the mouth: and it stands in place of the phlebotomy alieherich, which are in the two lips.
Cupping, however, of the shoulders, benefits alchafacham, which is produced from repletion and heat.
Cupping, however, of the inner parts of the two ankles, provides aid to the phlebotomy of the three veins, the Basilic, and the Median, and the Cephalic: because it itself draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh: and those small veins draw from other veins thicker than them, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, lest he proceed too far in scarification: because the place is denuded of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
Cupping, however, which is cupped over the alchausos, benefits hemorrhoids of the anus, and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, that the cupping glass be large, and that it be of air: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps thin cupping glasses break: and let it be scarified with a large scarification.
Cuppings, however, of the legs diminish with manifest diminution: because they draw blood from the whole body, and benefit old pains in the kidneys, and the womb, and the bladder: they provoke menstruation, and benefit boils, and ademenul: and they stand in place of the phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and alhorcobeni.
Nevertheless, they weaken the body much, and cause syncope to occur in many men.
And the benefit of cupping of the alhorcobeni is close to the benefit of cupping of the two legs.
The quality of the placement of cuppings is that you place the cupping glass first empty, and suck with moderate suction: and do not prolong the placement of the cuppings, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors may come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to iterate this and continue, until you see the place now redden and inflate, and the redness of blood appears: then therefore scarify, and iterate the suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For whoever of men is of tender flesh, of sparse pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, not more: lest the place be ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to amplify the scarification, and deepen it a little: and moderate the suction in ease, and speed, and gentle movement.
But if there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify him twice. In the first instance indeed a way is opened for the thinning of the blood, and its watery part: and in the second indeed for the thorough investigation of the extraction of thick blood.
If however the blood is very foul, then let a scarification be made a third time: that it may achieve the ultimate.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or little by little, one scarification suffices for us. But if we wish the extraction of much blood, we scarify with many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification: and the equal limit in scarification, is the depth of the skin.
What is that which it is necessary to administer from oils at the placing of cupping glasses, and what the cupper should avoid.
Indeed in him whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are constricted, it is necessary that the places to be cupped be anointed with opening, softening, resolving oils: if it is in the time of summer, such as is oil of alkeiri, or oil of violets, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of pumpkin seed.
And if it is in the time of winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or sarbatine oil: and similar things.
If however the superfluity is thick, cold: then let the oil be marjoram oil, or costmary oil, or rue oil, or dill oil: and similar things.
If however the person to be cupped has large pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he be forbidden oil, and it is necessary that he wash the places of the cupping glasses after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with water of solanum, or with gourd water, or with purslane water: and things similar to them.
He however whose blood is of much humidity, let him wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumach: and similar things.
But he whose superfluities are thick, let him wash his cuppings with old wine, or with water of wormwood, or a decoction of dill, or chamomile: and similar things.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in the bath, or immediately after the bath; indeed it is necessary that it be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone sleep after cupping.
What is it by which it is necessary that the one to be cupped, or bled, be regulated before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary that you consider first: then if the cupped or bled one is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation prevail over his blood: then it is necessary that he take refrigerants, such as pomegranate: and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and succory, and julep: and similar things.
And put among his foods chickens, and the flesh of young sheep, and foods seasoned with verjuice: and similar things.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take moderately fragrant wine, which is between old and new: and let him be ordered to use little food. And put among his food chickens, and canebir; and sparrows, that is, small birds; and young pigeons, he should eat.
And it is necessary that drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than food: and it is necessary that in some men there be given in drink at certain hours theriac alfaroch, or a medicine of musk, that is, nutmeg, or alesselith before cupping: and before bloodletting, or after: so that the principal members may be strengthened, and the blood thinned. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning cupping without scarification. Cuppings, however, which are without scarification, are cuppings which are placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gout, and upon the belly, navel, and the regions of the kidneys, and the hip joint: because these members do not endure scarification upon them.
And nothing is sought by them, except either the drawing of blood from a member to a member: just as we place a cupping glass upon the breasts in the illness of nosebleed. And we use them to resolve from a member cold, trapped flatulence in a member: just as we place a cupping glass upon the belly and navel. For it itself rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes pain, therefore, because it resolves that flatulence.
Also it is placed upon the kidneys, when there happens in them obstruction, or a stone. For by the virtue of its attraction perhaps it opens the obstruction, or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed upon the liver and spleen with rubbing flatulence in both of them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in illnesses of pleurisy: and that is so that the cupping glass is filled, and is large, with warm water alone, or water in which herbs that are suitable have been decocted.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire.
Let the size of its opening be two open fingers, according to what we have formed: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be in its side, in the place which is about its middle, a small hole, according to the quantity that a needle enters it: let it be made of bronze, or brass, of thick edges, smooth, even, firm: so that it does not injure the limb at its placement upon it: and let there be in its middle a transverse rod of bronze, or iron, where a candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than that which we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
For the cupping glasses of children and thin people are different from the cupping glasses of men and those of thick bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire on a limb is to light a small linen wick with fire decently, or a small wax candle: and place it on the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass: so that the lit fire is around the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then let it be placed on the limb: and a finger should be placed over the hole which I mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glass; when we need it, we remove the finger, and the vapor will come out through that hole, and the cupping glass will be removed immediately.
Then relight the wick in its own way, and repeat it, if you need it.
In the cupping glass however which is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole: and it is not administered, except that it is filled with water, and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this cupping glass, the larger it is, so that it may hold much water, is better.
Concerning the application of leeches.
Leeches are not applied in many places, except in those members where the application of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their smallness, such as the lip and the gums, and similar things. Or in a member denuded of flesh, such as a finger, and nose, and similar things.
And the manner of using them is: that you take leeches which are in fresh waters, clean from putrefaction.
Then let them be left day and night in fresh water, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then let the body be evacuated first with phlebotomy, and with cupping glasses. Then wipe the member of the sick person until it is red. Afterwards, let them be placed upon it.
Therefore, when they are full and fall, and suction of the place with a cupping glass is possible, then it is most effective in helping; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and let it be rubbed, and squeezed out.
But if the flow of blood is prolonged after the fall of the leeches, and it is oozing: then dip a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top, until the oozing is stopped.
But if the blood increases, then powder on it crushed alum, or galls, and similar things from the styptics: until the blood is stopped.
Or let the halves of shelled beans be placed on the place: and let them remain, until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood is stopped.
And it is necessary, if a repetition of leeches is necessary, to change them when possible.
But if leeches are prevented from adhering, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood. Or prick the places with a needle, until something of the blood comes out. Then let them be applied, for when they sense something of the blood, they adhere immediately.
Therefore, when you wish them to fall off, sprinkle on them something of powdered aloe, or salt, or ashes: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O sons, that foolish doctors and laymen boast of this chapter, who have never inspected the books of the ancients, nor read a letter from them.
And for this reason it happens that this method is unknown in our land. And I never encountered anything good in it, and I did not obtain from it what I obtained except through long reading of the books of the ancients, and study to understand them, until I extracted knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter all that my knowledge contains: and what my experiment has shown, after I have shortened it for you, and freed it from the burden of prolixity, and abbreviated it with a fine abbreviation, and shown it with a fine exposition: and I have formed in it many forms of the instruments which are used in it, since it is an addition to the exposition, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we should say, in the prologue of this chapter, the summaries of the discourse, and the chapters, the understanding of which is necessary for the craftsman firstly, in order to stand upon their truth: namely, that you and those who are ready may know, or learn, this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or sprain, or fall happens to someone, then it is necessary that you hasten first to bleed him, and to purge him, or both together: unless something forbids it, such as weakness of strength; or if the one to whom one of these things has happened is a child, or a decrepit old man; or if it is a time of intense heat, or of intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, and the flesh of birds and kids; and forbid wine, and thick meats, and being filled with foods and every food which fills the veins with blood. Until, when you are secure from a hot abscess, and do not fear an effusion of matter to the limb: then let the infirm return to his first regime, with which his custom ran.
When therefore a broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the infirm be nourished with foods nourishing with much, thick, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from boiled wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish; and thick wine, and things similar to them. For with this regime the coagulation of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in early old age, and decrepit, it is not possible that they are joined and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the dryness of their limbs; and those which are in the end of softness are consolidated and joined, like the bones of small children.
Nevertheless nature attaches to a broken bone on every side something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together, and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its operations: and because of this cause it is necessary that you put in the nourishment of the infirm foods in which there are heaviness, and viscosity, and thickness, as we said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs: because a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head: and a fracture of the bone of the chest is different from a fracture of the bone of the back: and similarly all the other limbs are all different from one another: and I have come with a remembrance of every kind of fracture expounded in its Chapter, distinct from another. And the kinds of bone fracture are also diversified in themselves.
Because its fracture is curved, besides which splinters occur in it. And sometimes its fracture is according to the length of the bone: and its fractures are splinters, that is, splinters, and additions proceeding, such as are in a reed, when it is fitted with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And each kind has its own method, or instrument, in its restoration, according to what its remembrance will come, distinct in its places.
And from that by which a bone fracture is known, is its tortuosity, and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent tortuosity in the place, nor sound on your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the infirm find great pain: then there is no fracture there, indeed it is possible that there is tortuosity, or a small fissure: therefore it is not necessary that you move it with your hand and compression completely, indeed place on it of the medicines, of which remembrance comes, after that moment which is suitable for the place: then let it be bound with a thin bandage.
And know that when a place is arched, and split in two, besides what happens in it of injuries, nevertheless every part is removed from its equal: then it is necessary for you to begin from your hour to its rectification, equalizing it before a warm abscess happens to it.
But if a warm abscess happens to it, then leave it for some days, until the abscess rests. Then equalize it according to whichever way can be done with gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration, and its equalization, is easier than that of a bone in which injuries occur.
Then bind it, according to what will come to your mind concerning it.
But if there are injuries in the bone, then extension of this broken member from two parts is necessary, if it is a hand or foot; or with your hand, if the member is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position, in which you place the member, be on an even place, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try to make those additions return in every way, and according to whichever way you can, to their places; and beware, according to your strength, lest pain or injury happen to the patient through your operation; and try, according to your strength, to apply one of the two bones to its fellow, according to the best methods.
And it is necessary at that time that you try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it and equalize it according to the measure of your strength.
And beware of vehement extension and strong compression, as many foolish men do. For very often they cause by that operation of theirs a hot abscess, or a contraction in the member: as we have already seen that from their operations very often.
Then, after the equalization and proper operation and the binding of that member, and rest, and tranquility,  advise the patient about it. And make him beware lest he move it at the time of his waking, and his sleep, in its revolution and agitation, and in his excretion, and all his movements with his utmost strength. And that he beware lest the position of the member be a position which causes pain. That is, because when he feels pain or injury in the positioning of the member, then it is necessary that you change it to another position from that with which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with it, that its position be equal and straight, so that tortuosity does not occur in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the binding of the broken member is as I relate.
Know that broken members are diversified in their smallness and their size and their shape.
Therefore, those of them which are small, such as the arm and finger and the radius and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. But those of them which are thick, such as the hip and leg, it is necessary that there be in them a wide, hard wrapping.
Because a wide ligature connects a large member and binds it from every part with an equal binding, so that no blemish enters it.
Then begin after your evacuation to place a suitable liniment to it, on soft lint over the place of its fracture. Then begin to wrap the ligature over the place of its fracture with three or four wrappings, according to what the member deserves: and tighten your hand a little with the ligature.
Then go with it to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and tighten less than was your binding, with which you bound the broken place. Then lengthen yourself with the wrapping from the place of the fracture a little, and soften the binding gradually, until you take from the healthy place a large amount.
Then take another bandage, and wrap it again over the broken place with wrappings. Afterwards go with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your work be in the tightening of the wrapping, and the softening of it, according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings some soft tow, or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture is evened out, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another bandage over it. Afterwards evenly place over these bandages properly made splints immediately, if there is not swelling in the place, nor a hot abscess.
But if there is swelling or an abscess in it, then place on it that which sits upon that abscess, and removes the swelling; and leave it for a few days. Then bind the splints over it.
And let the splints be from the halves of broad reeds, shaved, skillfully shaped; or let them be from the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or gerard, that is, palm branches, or calingi; and let the size of each splint be according to this form, and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed over the injured part be somewhat thicker and broader than the other splints; and the length of the splint should be according to what is permitted by the limb in size and smallness.
Then bind over the splints with another bandage, according to the same first binding. Then bind over it neatly with threads, as we said about the binding: and it is so that your binding over the place of the fracture should be tighter; and the further it extends from the fracture, the looser the binding.
And it is necessary that the threads be medium in thickness and fineness, and be of soft linen.
Because if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the practice of foolish men placing their threads from twisted linen cords, it is a great mistake; because the binding falls with them outside the proper quality.
And thin threads again are not suitable; because you will not achieve with them through the binding that which you wish.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between splint and splint.
But if the patient is injured by the ends of the splints after binding in healthy places, then place under them some soft tow or carded wool, until nothing is injured thereby.
When, however, there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will deal with its treatment in a separate chapter.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with splints from the first day. That is because, when a limb is large, it is not necessary to put splints on it until after five days, or seven, or more, according to your assurance from the outcome of hot inflammation.
And this is the method of the plasters which the ancients used, which are applied to fractures and dislocations.
The method of a common abbreviated plaster, restoring fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children: because it is made of something which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, and it is the flour bran which hangs on the walls of the mill at the movement of the millstone: and make it as it is, except that you sift it with egg white: and make its consistency neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
The method of another plaster, restoring fracture, and dislocation, and torsion. Take alkanet, and laudanum, and acacia, and elecampane, and gum arabic: and the juice of all, of each ten drachms, of myrrh, and aloes of each five drachms, and litharge twenty drachms; let all be ground, and sifted, and mixed with litharge water: or with egg white, if the complexion of the patient is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many men.
The method of another plaster, which also benefits fracture and torsion. Take gum arabic, and alkanet, and marshmallow, of each ten drachms, of myrrh, and aloes of each five drachms, of acacia six drachms, of Armenian bole twenty drachms; let all be ground with much grinding, and sifted, and made up with water, or egg white: and let it be administered.
The method of a plaster for joints, and bones separated from their places: and it soothes the pain occurring to them, and the fissure of a bone, and fracture. Let greasy wool be taken, and submerged in vinegar, and boiled oil: and let it be placed upon the place.
And indeed in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good in the soothing of a hot abscess: and it expels pains properly.
The method of a plaster which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the wild fig, and leaves of wild poppy: and grind everything, and make a plaster from both.
The method of an abbreviated plaster, administered in the restoration of a bone fracture: and when you wish the resolution of the remaining abscess. Take of marshmallow root, and chamomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: let all be ground, and made into a paste with wine, if the limb is not heated.
But if it is heated, then prepare it with the water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
The method of another stronger plaster in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a limb. Take of marshmallow root, and linseed, and balaustium, and melilot, and bugloss, and violet flower, and chamomile, of each one part; let all be ground, and made into a paste with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. All this according to the warmth of the limb, and the abatement of its heat.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that anything should be put on a broken limb during its restoration except cerate made from wax and oil: nothing else: and he related that it should be medium between thickness and thinness.
To Galen however it seemed good that things which possess a drying warmth should be placed on a broken limb during its restoration, such as myrrh, and aloes and frankincense, and similar things to them from those which we have related.
Method of a plaster which helps with injury and pain. Take mugath, and chickpea, and cut human hair, or bird feather, and marshmallow, and salt in equal parts: let them be ground, and sifted, and mixed: and let a plaster be made from it.
The amount of time for which the bandage ought to remain, then be untied, is that you should consider then, if no pain happens to the patient, nor itching, nor is the broken bone moved from its place: then do not untie it for many days. But if intense itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling happens to him in the place: then hasten, and untie it at once, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides.
But if intense itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling happens to him in the place: then hasten, and untie it at once, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides. Then let the limb rest for an hour. Then place upon it carded wool, immersed in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it on for one night, until you are secure from a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then return it to a thin bandage, and a small plaster: and do not bind it with your first constriction, and do this carefully with it, until it is healed.
But if you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all the symptoms are now completely removed, and you need a plaster and bandage, then repeat it as you did at first equally.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the limb, then do not unbind it except after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when twenty days have passed, all of that, according to what appears to you from the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres, and the coagulation of flesh approximates it: then also add to the bandage more than your whole first bandage: and also add to the thickening of the patient's food, according to what the previous recollection of it has been.
But if you see the place of the fracture drying out and becoming thinner than it ought, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore bathe it with warm water at every time that you unbind it, and let this be for three days, and loosen the bandage a little.
Through this operation, therefore, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
But what foolish restorers of a limb fracture do in another way, when it is not restored at first as it ought, and is restored according to crookedness, is an error of their operation, and great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said it in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have nowhere seen any trace of them at all in it, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning an accidental injury to the head.
The kinds of fracture are diverse, and its forms are many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture which is made by a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone entirely until it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and on account of this, this kind of fracture is called Axial.
Or a sword cuts some of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its end: and this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Elevation absolutely.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a contusion, or attrition: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the membrane which is under the bone: or it is in the surface of the bone: and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone in the fineness of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this kind of fracture is called Capillary.
And among them is a fracture which is made by a fall, or a blow from a stone, and similar things, which cause a plate of bone to enter inwards, and a concavity is made in the place: just as happens to bronze cauldrons, when a blow strikes them, and part of them enters inwards.
And this happens most in the heads of moist bones, such as are the heads of children.
And for all these kinds of fractures separate treatments are made, and I will come to the recollection of all of that in its place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures by examining them, and investigating them with probes, and removing the corrupted flesh which is upon them.
The hair-line fracture however is recognized by this, that it is detected on the bone, and cleaned, and smeared with a hot iron: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the patient's symptoms: then if you see from the symptoms that it signifies manifestly danger, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of consciousness, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar symptoms in many conditions, undoubtedly it is not curable: and if you see symptoms not frightening you, and you hope for recovery: then begin its treatment.
That is, if a wounded man comes to you immediately after being wounded: and it is in the winter months, then it is necessary that you strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day according to every condition.
And if it is in the summer months, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the membrane is corrupted, and those symptoms which we have described occur.
But if the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and is with small pieces, and attraction: then it is necessary that you cut away the broken and abraded part, according to what I tell you.
And it is necessary to shave the head of the wounded man: and expose the bone in whatever way is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light upon the patient.
But if during the uncovering of the bone there occurs a flow of blood, or a hot abscess: then apply to it what is necessary. And it is necessary to fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and to make the wound rest, until the abscess subsides, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to incise the bone with a sharp-edged incisor: and this is its form.
Then apply another, slightly wider, incisor after the first: and this is its form.
Then apply yet another incisor, wider even than the second.
And the second method is that you have a number of different incisors: some of which are wider than others, and some shorter than others: and let them be very sharp at their ends, and let them be made of good iron or steel: and use care in striking the incisor, so that you do not shake the head and injure it.
But if the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary to perforate its circumference before you apply the incisors, with drills which are called non-penetrating drills: and they are so called because they do not pass through the bony limit to that which is beyond it: because the drill has a round end above that which is under its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take many of these drills, each suited to the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill for every skull: the length and shortness of whose sharp end is according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trepans.
The method of perforating the circumference of the fractured bone is: to place the drill on the bone, and to turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then change the drill to another place, and place what is between each hole at the thickness of a finger, or thereabouts.
Then incise with incisors what is between each hole in the bone: and make it as thin as you can from the end lightly, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with some other of certain instruments which you have prepared for it: such as forceps and fine scissors.
And it is necessary that you take every precaution lest the drill or incisor touch anything of the dura mater.
Therefore, when you have gone around the bone by perforating, and it has been separated from the dura mater, if it was continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary to shave and smooth the end of what remains in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor. However, it is necessary that it be thinner and finer than other incisors.
But if something remains of small bones and splinters, then take them away easily with what is at hand among your instruments. Then treat the wound afterwards with linen and ointments as we have said.
And another method of operation is easy, altogether free from fear: which Galen mentioned and praised greatly; and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first to begin to uncover the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and more evident, until when you have uncovered that part, and you have placed under it the end of this lenticular incisor, of which this is the form.
And the lenticular part, being smooth, does not cut anything; and its sharp part on its two sides going lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the soft tissue, its sharp side falling on the bone.
Then strike the incisor on one side with a small hammer, until you cut through the whole bone easily, as if it were being rolled; and you will be safe from the membrane, so that nothing happens to it which you fear at all.
And if the physician were not the most foolish of men, and most ignorant of this, nor were sleeping, then if something of the membrane remained attached to the bone in some places, he would cut it from it with the end of his lenticular incisor, and would free it from it easily. For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the membrane, and what is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughness and small splinters remain on it: then it is necessary that you scrape that roughness, and remove those splinters with fine rasps, of which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it is possible for you to use from them in every place the most suitable and most straight of them, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone, and its shape. And it is necessary that you use in your procedure, in which you first scrape the bone, the wider of those rasps. Then use after it a finer one.
And it is necessary that you use in your procedure, in which you first scrape the bone, the wider of those rasps. Then use after it a finer one. And do not cease to do that continuously, until you arrive at using the finest and most delicate of them all.
In each of the remaining small, hair-like fissures and delicate fractures, it is necessary that you administer care, according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a thing which is not hidden from anyone who has any study in this art, and has considered what we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the exposed membrane remains when you remove the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do this as easily as you can so that the intestine is not burdened.
Then apply a wide bandage over it; and do not bind it tightly except to the extent that it holds the wound only: and leave it with this dressing for one day or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then unbind it, and afterwards apply medicines with which there is superfluous dryness: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar things: and it is to make a powder from these medicines, which is to be sprinkled over the wound as they are dry.
And universally apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite. And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean: nor is there any dirt of ointment, nor filthiness in it.
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean: nor is there any dirt of ointment, nor filthiness in it. Nor allow pus to collect in it at all: because when pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and great harm happens to the patient afterwards: and it already happens in the inflammation of the head, when the bone is cleaned from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Consider therefore then, if the accidents which we have mentioned happen to the patient, know that it is undoubtedly very harmful.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine which is placed upon it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine to make that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and apply a cloth with them. Then place it over the inflammation; afterwards treat it with the kinds of treatment which are suitable, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that only the two upper parts of the nose are broken together, or one of them; because these are two bones: for its lower part is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and there only occurs to it attrition, and crookedness, and flatness.
If one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is necessary that you insert the little finger into the hole of the nose, and set that fracture with your index finger and thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural form; and let this be done skillfully and easily. And beware that no pain occurs to the patient through your operation.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary that it be set with the end of a radius, in which there is a small thickness.
If however the fracture is in two parts, then do similarly; and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at the rest of the warm abscess.
Then insert into the end of the nose a linen pledget, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two pledgets if the fracture is in two parts; and let the thickness of the pledget be of such a quantity that it fills the hole of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the pledgets should be soaked in butter, and changed every day. That however does not seem good to me; indeed it is necessary that the pledget be soaked in egg white, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the pledget until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of a pledget, after it has been wrapped in soft cloth; and it happens that this holds the fracture of the nose more firmly, and that the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not a necessary thing. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, do the pledgets.
But if a hot abscess should occur again in the nose while you are operating, plaster the nose with cerate, or with cotton infused in rose oil, or a little diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. Then place a soft pledget over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
But if the bones of the nose are broken by a small fracture, or are comminuted, then it is necessary that you incise over them and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them. Then sew the incision, and treat it with what produces granulation and consolidation from suitable plasters or ointments.
But if a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary that you treat it with linen strips and insert a lead cannula until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then observe if the fracture is only external, and it is not broken in two, and is bent inwards; then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth; similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and with it gently push the inward bulge of the fracture outwards with your other hand from the outside of the bone decently, and with it level it.
But if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to administer extension from two parts, according to straightness, until its equalization is possible.
But if shaking or disruption has already occurred in the teeth, then bind those which you hope will remain with a gold or silver wire, or bristle. Then place cerate over the broken jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and place a large, properly made splint over the cloth; or a piece of sole leather, equal in length to the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to the way its bandage is prepared and its application is suitable, until it is not destroyed; and instruct the patient to use tranquility and quiet; and give him his food in gentle sorbtions.
But if you estimate that something of the form is already altered in some way, then hasten and unbind it on the third day. Then rectify what is altered from it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the cerate; and place soft wool over the plaster.
While therefore that plaster remains attached to it, and the arrangement of the bone is not altered, leave it, lest you unbind it, until it is healed; or bind the fracture. For often this fracture is bound in three weeks.
But if a hot abscess should occur meanwhile, then administer what we have often said in its sedation, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a fragment is now separated from the bone, or from the splinter: then be skillful in this, so that you remove those splinters of the splinter with what suits you in their removal from among the instruments.
But if the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a fleam according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those splinters, and nothing of them remains, sew then the opening of the wound, if it is large; and if not, place upon it of the plasters which are suitable for it, and they will cause it to grow flesh, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the collarbone, when it is broken.
The collarbone is indeed very often broken on the front side, on the shoulder side, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two, except that splinters occur in it: and it is easier to restore it.
Or so that splinters occur in the bone: and it is more difficult to restore.
Or so that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation when the fracture is without a wound is that you cause two assistants to be present, so that one of them holds the aid that follows the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck toward the side.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers, until its form is made, according to what is necessary; and let there be neither prominence nor concavity in it.
But if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the patient's neck a sphere of cloth or wool: and let its size be according to the quantity of your need: and extend and raise the collarbone, and compress the sphere with your hand, until you equalize the fracture, according to what is necessary.
But if you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outwards because it has reached a depth, then it is necessary that the patient be laid on his back on his neck: and you place under his shoulder a pillow medium in size, and the assistant presses his shoulder downwards, until the bone of the collarbone which is deep is raised upwards: and then rectify the fracture, and equalize it with your fingers.
But if you feel a splinter from the collarbone, and it moves, then it is necessary that you incise over it, and extract the splinter easily.
But if it is retained in the bone, then make an incision in it with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the collarbone the instrument which preserves the pleura: and this instrument is of wood or of iron. And this is its form.
It is similar to a spoon which has no concavity.
And let its width be according to that which you need of it from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length however also according to what is possible for you for the operation.
And let it have two extremities, as you see: and let one extremity be wide, and the other narrow.
But if the disruption of the wound which you cut when you extracted the splinter of bone, is wide, and you are secure from a hot abscess: then join the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths, and powders according to the size of the wound's fissure.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then dip a cloth in rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and place it on top.
A constriction however of the bone when it is without a wound, and without a section, is that you place on the bone a plaster made from mill flour with egg white. And place a soft pledget on top of it. Then place a sphere under its tickling, if you need that. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long: and let its width be one palm, or about that. Then place a doubled pad over the pledget and plaster. Then take a small splint from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and in length similarly. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the splint in the place of the alhimemati which falls upon the fracture and the place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it is rolled, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy little finger, and under its unhealthy little finger: and bring it over every part, and as you see that the bandage holds the fracture with a decent bandage: and it is that which is not hidden from you, and the whole, so that the splint is not removed from above the broken bone. And because of this it is necessary that you look at the patient every day. Whenever therefore the bandage softens, and you see the splint being removed: then straighten it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's bed on his back, and place under his little finger at his bed a small cushion at night, so that with it his arm may be raised from his side. Therefore the fracture of the clavicle is elevated by the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck: and do not unbind the bandage, if no accident happens in the place from itching, or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see its surface sticking to it. And add to the constriction, and leave it until it is restored and coagulated.
And indeed the fracture of the clavicle is constricted and strengthened mostly within twenty-eight days: and sometimes it happens in some men in less time than that.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the scapula.
The scapula is rarely fractured, and in its broad part; and only its margins are fractured from it.
Therefore when a place is fractured from it, and it is fractured in its middle, and this is not known except by touch: then according to what the figure of the fracture is, attempt its preparation. And restore it to its natural form in every way possible to your skill.
Then place upon the place mill dust with egg white, and soft lint, and place on top a small cushion of doubled cloth. Then place upon it a broad splint of thin wood according to the quantity of the whole scapula, or a little larger than it.
But if under the splint there is a concavity in any place of the scapula, then equalize that concavity with soft lint, until the splint descends according to evenness.
Then bind it above with a long bandage, with fitting constriction, and firmness of the splint, until it is not removed from its place.
And examine the bandage every day. Whenever, therefore, the bandage is loosened, tighten it, and equalize the splint if it is removed from its place: and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And indeed, the shoulder blade is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then unbind the bandage: and you shall be in safety. For it is of bones whose consolidation is not feared, nor their separation.
But if a fragment of bone protrudes and pricks beneath the skin: then incise over it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the clavicle from the settling of a hot abscess, if anything should happen from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the breastbone.
The breastbone is sometimes fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens. Its extremities, however, are most often fractured and comminuted from accidents to it.
When its middle, which inclines towards the lower parts, is fractured, and there is intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing; and perhaps he vomits blood, and a concavity in the fractured bone is evident and not hidden from perception, its restoration is that the patient is laid supine on his back; and a cushion is placed between his shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and bring together the ribs from both sides or flanks with the hands; and carefully adjust its alignment, in every way that is possible and appropriate, until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be.
Then place a plaster and lint over it, and place on top a splint of thin willow or alder board, or similar light materials, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then carefully bind it over the fractured bone so that it does not move; and continue the bandage around the back several times, and tighten it with a suitable constriction.
Then examine the bandage every hour, and whenever it loosens, tighten it; and if necessity demands its loosening because of itching, or ulceration occurring at the site, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has occurred according to the method of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see it is needed; and apply a bandage to it until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts which follow the back, and at their extremities on the anterior side. And attrition does not happen to them except because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the sense upon examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that you equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until the shape is equalized, as is necessary. Then apply plasters, and bind the broken bone with a splint, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inwards: then intense pain and a puncture happen to the patient, like the puncture which happens to someone who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also difficulty of breathing, and coughing, and vomiting much blood happen to him: and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have devised many methods for it: and some of them say that you should give the patient foods which produce inflammation and flatulence, so that the abdomen is inflamed and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
We however avoid causing a rapid formation of a hot abscess, if it does not occur. But if it has already occurred, then add to it.
And some of them said: let a cupping glass be placed on the place, then let it be sucked with force, and this is more reasonable. Nevertheless, it is feared that the cupping glass will attract superfluities to the place, because of the weakened condition.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been submerged in hot oil: and let powders be placed between the ribs, until it is filled: so that the bandage is even when you wrap it according to its roundness.
Then let the sick person be treated with a treatment for pleurisy from food and medicine.
But if a severe matter constricts the sick person, which he cannot tolerate, and the bone pierces the covering with a wounding puncture, and we fear for the sick person: then it is necessary that we cut over the place, and uncover the fractured rib. Then let us place over it the instrument which preserves the sifac, the remembrance of which preceded: and let us cut the bone easily, and let us incise it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture: and let us treat it with plasters until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess should occur in the meantime, then hasten, and pour lint into rose oil, and place it over the place: and let the sick person be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile also, let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Fracture sometimes occurs to the bones of the neck, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly also to the vertebrae of the back. Therefore when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened and numb and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor clench them; and when you clench them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that he will not be healed, and in most instances it is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels constriction with the fingers and the prick: then know that the neck bone is safe, and that the sick man will be healed with treatment.
But if something similar to that happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then consider his feet; then if you see that they are softened, and that which happened in the hands happens in them: then when he lies on his back, flatulence and excretion exit involuntarily; and when he is laid on his belly, excretion exits involuntarily; and when he is laid on his back, and wishes to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore do not labor in his cure.
If, however, nothing of that happens to him, the matter is less serious.
And the treatment of that which happens from it is that you try to soothe the hot abscess: so that you put on the affected vertebra rose oil alone; or with roasted egg yolks.
Apply this several times a day until the hot abscess is soothed; then apply to the place one of the strengthening, drying plasters, and bind it tightly; and instruct the patient to use rest and quiet. Nor should he sleep on the side where he finds pain, until it is healed.
But if it happens at the abrasion of a bone that is decayed, or something that is already separated from it: then it is necessary that you incise the skin, and remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it heals.
If however the end of the alhausos bone is broken, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and set the broken bone with the other hand, as much as possible, and by a second setting.
Then place it on and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment which preceded, until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
Chapter XI.
Rarely are the bones of the hip broken. But if they are broken, then their fracture is only a comminution at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise, and deviate inwards: and there happens to the patient pain in the place, and a puncture: and the leg which is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration indeed is, that you extend your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture as is its form.
But if the fracture is only at its extremities, then set that fracture, according to what is prepared for you of a setting, until it is assimilated to its natural form.
If however the fracture is lengthwise, or it already deviates inwards: then make the patient lie on his belly, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When therefore you have set it, place a plaster over it: then place over it a splint of wood, or of leather: and bind it tightly, over which you do not fear a change of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of those with that which fills it, until the binding encompasses it according to equality: and instruct the patient to sleep on his back, or on his sound side.
But if a hot abscess should occur to him, then cease from its extension and its restoration, until the hot abscess subsides: and place upon it that which subsides it, according to what preceded, then return to its restoration: and bind it as is necessary.
If however it happens in a projecting bone, or is crushed, remove it.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone.
The collarbone is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder blade.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form which follows.
And two bandages will be tied at the two ends.
Then let it be hung from a high place: and let the patient sit on a seat. Then let him throw his broken arm onto the wood, until it reaches his armpit, in the middle of which the curve of the wood is attached.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that the assistant may extend it downwards. Then let the physician equalize the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns, as it ought.
And another method is, that the patient be laid supine upon his neck: and suspend his hand from his neck with a ligature. Then instruct two assistants, that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with his hands: and that the other hold that which is below it, and that each of them extend to his part.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a ligature, and above it with another ligature: and let each of the two assistants extend to his own two parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary that one ligature be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a ligature be placed above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then set the fracture easily without effort, until when the fracture is set as it should be, and a good application is applied: then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave off the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess is quiet: then bind it: and the method of binding it is to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster. Then let him support the arm on a support, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind the arm to the support, so that the arm stands in the place of the stars: if nothing prevents you from it, nor is anything changed in you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something will be changed in you from the broken bone, then use splints: and it is that you place over the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between each splint the width of a finger: and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind over the splints the bandage which I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage over the place of the fracture be tighter. And the more the fracture is lengthened, let the bandage be less tight.
If therefore you see the placement of the splints and the bandage, as we have said, at the hour in which you restore the limb immediately, then do so: and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave off the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the bandage in all three days, lest itching or swelling occur in the place, or food be prevented from reaching the member, because of the disposition of superfluous tightness of the bandage. Therefore rectify all that, according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bandage except after three days, or more.
And let the sleep of the patient be on his back, and his hands on his stomach: and place under the armpit a cushion filled with wool evenly.
And observe at every hour of the night or day, lest the shape of the broken member be loosened, or the bandage softened. Therefore rectify all that by your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have set forth, so that the food may be light at first, until the mouth begins to tighten: then it is necessary to increase his food.
For it is the custom of the arm and leg, that it be bound for forty days: then therefore it is necessary that it be unbound, and a bath be administered, and it be treated with ointments which restore it.
But if the fracture be foul, and comminuted, then do not unbind the bandage and splints until fifty days, and even up to two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearms.
Of these, one is small; and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed under the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forearm is broken, or the smaller; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the small upper forearm is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forearm is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone which is broken is the small upper forearm, then it is suitable for the physician in its restoration, that he place a small extension easily, until he sets it.
But if it is the large forearm which is broken: then it is necessary that he place a much stronger binding.
And it is necessary that he place the shape of the hand in its restoration, and its extension extended on a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers: and let the lowest joint be lower than all the fingers, or the rest: and let the patient sit squarely on himself, and let the cushion be before him, or directly in front of him, at such a height, that the patient does not incur exertion.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand, or with a bandage. And let another attendant extend it from above similarly.
Then let the physician set the bone, until he restores it according to the best shape that he can.
But if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then try to restore every fragment to its place by your skill.
But if a separated splinter appears in it, and pierces the skin, and you have no hope in restoring it: then incise over it, and remove it according to the method which we have described in those things which have been previously mentioned.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for that, therefore take its treatment from there.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then smear a cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a light bandage, until the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from mill dust with egg white. Then apply splints, and let the splint which is placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases: whether the fracture is in one bone, or in two bones at the same time.
Then apply your bandage over the place of fracture more firmly and tightly, and somewhat more with the bandage to the upper parts of the fracture, or to its lower parts: or apply a bandage somewhat softer than that, according to what preceded its mention at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths which are wrapped over the fracture be soft, moist cloths: and let them not be hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be of linen, properly, a medium between fine and thick, as we have narrated.
And consider the limb and the bandage after some days. But if something happens which requires correction, such as itching which happens to the limb: then it is necessary that you bathe the limb with warm water, until that itching ceases, and you leave the limb unbound, until it rests; then repeat the bandage.
But if the bandage is now softened, and the bone now removed, and similar things to those: then rectify all that with your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, because of the excess of the bandage, then it is necessary that you soften it a little, and leave it for a few days, until nourishment flows to it, then bind it.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the patient, then it is not necessary that it be loosened, except after twenty days, or about those.
Then let the patient's hand be suspended from his neck. And let his neck be straight, and let it be kept by its own strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and dispositions of strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that a fracture rarely happens to the back of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers. And it does not happen to it except by much rubbing.
Therefore when a fracture or rubbing happens to the palm, then it is necessary that the sick person sit squarely; and opposite him should be a seat of equal height. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the assistant extend the broken bones, and let the physician align them until when they are joined with a good joining.
Then it is necessary that he apply a plaster and lint, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a splint on top according to the size of the place; and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the lower parts around the sole of the foot, then make something similar to a sphere from cloth; and instruct the patient to bind it over it with his broken foot; then let it be bound with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied with the body of the foot; and bind it, according to what is necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary that you place one splint on top, and another splint below on the sole; and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a bandage, as the hand is rolled; and extend between the fingers with a ligature.
But if a fracture happens to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it is to be set according to what is necessary. Then it is to be bound with the sole; and if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it, so that the fracture may be raised, and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, or little, and ring finger, then it is set, and bound with the healthy finger which follows it; or all are bound together continuously; and that is better. Or place a small splint over it, as we said in the case of the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of a warm abscess, and oppose it with what is necessary, when something happens from it, according to what is repeated in its account.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the hip.
The hip bone is often fractured, and this is apparent to the senses: because it is turned towards the inner and posterior parts.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a ligament is tightened above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And let the patient be laid on his face.
Then let the attendant extend each ligament to its part equally; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
