But if the fracture is close to the root of the hip, then let the ligament be to the part of the pecten, so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then let the physician equalize the bone with both his hands, until he restores it according to the likeness of its natural form: and let the bone be joined with a good joining.
Then it is necessary that you place a plaster and a bandage, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then leave it for a few days, until the hot abscess is settled, then return to your treatment.
Moreover, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary that you wrap a hard bandage, that is, a linen cloth, over the fracture; and double it twice, or three times; and let a surplus remain from it. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee towards the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then roll over the leg and hip what is superfluous from the bandage.
Then place splints over the hip at the place of its fracture, and place one of them over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that the constriction is even.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four turns. And the more you extend with the bandage from the place of the fracture, let your constriction be less and softer, and gentler.
Then attend to the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the hip and leg. Therefore, tie with both three small bandages which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you reach the heel of the foot. And with both, tighten also the ends of the bandages from the other side, so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
Then loosen the constriction over it, while it remains, so that neither corrosion, nor abscess, nor swelling, nor things similar to them happen to the limb. If something of that happens. Therefore hasten with its loosening, and correct everything that happened from it, according to what we have said many times.
If, however, there is a piece of bone in the bone that is causing a puncture: then it is necessary to level it, if it is possible for you. And if not, make an incision over it and remove it: and treat it with that whose previous mention has been made, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is constricted, besides which splints are added to it, as we said in the case of the arm and shoulder.
Nevertheless, in the restoration of it thus fractured, lameness does not happen to the patient. And if it is restored alone, besides which the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he whose it is will always limp.
And know that the hip is constricted in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is subtracted: all that is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap rarely happens, but attrition happens to it very often.
If, however, a fracture happens to it, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And this is with a wound, and without a wound. And the whole thing is treated with sense.
And its restoration is such that what is disrupted of its parts is equated with the fingers, until it is aggregated and joined together as much as is possible by means of equation and ease, and fabrication.
Then apply a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white: and place a round compress over it, if you need it: and bind it above with a bandage suitable for it.
Then consider all the dispositions which we have narrated in the other fractures: such as a hot abscess, and similar to it: so that you oppose every accident with that which is suitable to it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, of which one is thick, and is named by the name of the leg: and the other is thin, and is named the fibula.
And the same kinds of fractures happen to both as happen to the two bones of the arm: and because of that, its restoration is done, like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
If both bones are broken, the leg is turned in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is turned to the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it lower down: and it is among those which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and equalization, and the tying of splints according to that equally.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a bad fracture, having many splinters, the extension should be less, and your alleviation gentler.
And in the leg indeed there is something in the operation beyond the arm: and it is that when you equalize the splints, and have completed all your operation, then take two splints of pine wood, which is used in the making of sunshades which is put under boards: and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from them those which are of small thickness, and neither let them be from the small ones: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap over each one a cloth with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee to the lower part of the foot.
Then bind the two splints in three places, at the two ends and the middle. For when constriction is prevented, the leg inclines neither to the right nor to the left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a cradle be made of wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it may preserve it from movement.
And it is especially necessary that you do this in a fracture when there is a proper wound with it.
Then examine the leg every day, and treat it diligently against an abscess, or inflammation, or other such things. If therefore anything of these things happens, then oppose it with what is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Concerning the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
Indeed, a complete fracture does not happen to the heel bone.
However, fracture sometimes happens to the bones of the foot, and fracture also happens little to the toes: and attrition happens to them in most instances, not fracture.
If fracture happens to the bones of the foot, and you see those bones raised up on top of each other, then let the patient put his foot on the prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand you, and put your foot on that which is raised up from those bones; afterwards press, or tread on it; and equalize it, until it returns to its places.
Then apply a plaster, and lint on top; and place under the sole of the foot a small board, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with a suitable bandage around the foot, after you have wrapped it with cloths, and other things which it needs.
When therefore three or four days pass, then untie the bandage. For you will find the bones even, whether they are fractured, or dislocated. Therefore the operation in them is as you see.
But if some fingers are broken, then restore them and align them according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint with your finger along the length of the finger, and let it be a little wider than it: then place this board which I have told you about under the foot, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place on each finger a splint of its size, wrapped in linen cloth: and bind the board to the lower part of the foot: and let it stand on one end of the board outside the belly of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is fitting for you, that you do not forget, but consider all that I have told you in the other fractures from the accidents which we have spoken of: and apply to each accident what is necessary.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and of the pubic bone, and of a man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is broken, then make her sit squarely. Then incline her slightly toward her back, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with cotton until she fills it; and let it be made into a sphere in the vulva from it.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. Then that cotton comes out, until it becomes like a sphere before the opening of the vulva, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when she wants to urinate, let her remove the cotton easily, then let her urinate: and let her replace it in the way in which you inserted it at first: then let her return to her first cushion.
Let her do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the bone itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and tie a reed tube over its opening, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with cotton, and let it remain for a few days, as we have described, until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of a man or a woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and its alignment what we have described for the hip bone.
And the rectum is not hidden in these nearby fractures which occur slightly, especially to someone who has some skill or experience.
And understand this book of mine well, because the fracture itself indicates to you in many situations the proper method for its restoration and its straightening; therefore understand.
But when the penis is broken, take a goose's neck and insert the penis into it. Then place over it a bandage of cloth, and it should be bandaged, and left for three days or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the bones when it is with a wound.
It is fitting for one to whom a fracture with a wound has happened, especially if it is a large one, such as of the hip bone, or of the thigh bone, or similar to it, that you should hasten and immediately bleed him: if the conditions for bleeding attest to you, as we have explained beforehand.
But if the wound flows blood, then it is necessary that you hasten to its excision, so that you may sprinkle upon it crushed alum: if nothing else is present to you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, if an abscess does not occur.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then postpone its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess is settled. And do not approach it on the third day, and on the fourth absolutely, for you would cause bad things to happen to it.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered: then it is necessary that you try to reduce it, and to equalize it with your hands with ease and slight extension.
But if the extension of it, and its equalization with your hands, is not prepared: then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument which is made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven or eight fingers: and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And because of that it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, according to the quantity which he needs for it in the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be in it a small thickness, so that it is not doubled up during compression upon it at the time of operation: and let it have a sharp end, which has a margin at its extremities. And let its upper part be thicker than its lower part.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that its sharp curved end be placed upon the end of the protruding bone; and push it with it at once, until the bone returns and is made even by a certain alignment.
Therefore try to align the ends of the fracture to each other.
But if the ends of the broken bone are small, and the instrument does not grasp it well, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument can hold it.
If however you cannot reduce them completely with what we have described, then incise it with whatever is suitable from the incisors which we have mentioned: or saw it with one of the saws in whichever way is possible for you.
Then shave off what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
Therefore when the bone is reduced, and the patient finds intense, hurting pain after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Then do this. For you provide the patient with great help when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then submerge the cloth in astringent, black wine. And especially if it is summer. And do not place cerate over the wound, nor anything containing oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption therein.
Then apply small splints at the completion of your bone restoration. And leave the wound uncovered, so that you cut a hole in the bandages with scissors according to the size of the wound.
And take every precaution lest you constrict the wound with a fracture. For many times foolish physicians have done this, and have caused death, or corrosion, or zuchem, that is, pus, to befall their patients. And let your binding be soft, gentle, different from the binding of other fractures.
But if the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some bad occurrences which we have mentioned, and you find pain suspended in the place: then it is not necessary that you place splints upon it. And make for them wrappings of hard cloths in place of splints, and bind it with them.
But when it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove from upon it the cloth which you placed upon it with wine. Then administer lint and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar things.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the binding. And examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus flows from it downwards with ease.
But if many days pass for the wound and it does not consolidate, nor is pus discharged from it: then know that there are small splinters of bone there. Therefore it is necessary that you explore the wound with a probe. However many of those splinters are separated, remove and extract them. And those of them which are not separated, and prick the limb, and cause pain to occur, try to incise them, and remove them in every way that is possible for you.
But if a canker, or ekileten, that is, an esthiomenus, or another type of corruption and putrefaction happens to the wound: then it is necessary that you oppose each of them with that which is suitable for it from the treatment, the account of which preceded in its chapter.
And that concerning which my speech must stand, and to which you should direct your attention when a bone is broken and protrudes above the limb, such as the hip bone, and the collar bone, and similar large bones, is that you should not attempt its extraction, for very often death results from this, but rather leave it until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then therefore treat the wound, if it seems good to you; and if not, leave it.
Concerning the treatment of a node in the track of a fracture.
This node often occurs in the track of the healing of a fracture. Especially that which is near the joints, and the shape of the limb is disfigured by it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore consider then, if the node is recent: administer to it medicines which have astringency, such as aloe, and olibanum, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar things.
So that you may take some of these, or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and put them on the node in a pledget, and bind them tightly upon it with a good bandage.
And leave the bandage, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the node is removed.
Or bind a lead plate neatly upon it. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
But if the node has already turned into stone, and is constricted, and urgent necessity compels its removal: then incise it from its upper part, and cut away the protruding excess: or shave it with some razors, until it is removed: and treat it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains afterwards thin, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and the limb afterwards remains thin, weak: then this does not happen except for many reasons. One of which is, the multitude of the loosening of the ligament and its binding not as it ought: or because of the excess of constriction of the ligaments, until nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb: or because of the multitude of superfluous embrocation at an unsuitable time: or because of the small amount of blood in the body of the patient, and his weakness.
And its treatment is, to nourish the patient, and make his body strong, until the blood is multiplied in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness to him.
Then place pitch on the limb: so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. And frequently embrocate it with warm water, until nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly.
When crookedness and a prominence of the broken bone, or a knot, occurs in a limb already set after its healing, and the form of the limb is disfigured thereby, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural function: then it is not necessary to heed the opinion of him who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed highly reprehensible, leading to death, or great fear, so that it is certainly deadly.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent, then it is necessary that it be bathed with warm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as mallow leaves and root, and melilot, and the like. And let a plaster be made with a softening plaster, such as properly made diachylon.
Or let the mucilage of mallow root be taken, and shaken with hen's fat, or sesame oil: and let it be plastered with it.
Or let fat figs be taken, and ground with pigeon dung, and similar things which are named among medicines that lessen consolidation.
And sometimes the knotting is resolved with frequent, gentle, and long rubbing, which is done with wet hands. And let movement of the limb be administered to every part at all hours.
But if tortuosity has preceded, and become strong, and has been converted into stone, and necessity has been provoked to its cure with iron: then it is necessary that its upper part be cut, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that which superabounds from the knotting or bone be incised with fine scalpels: and let ease be administered in it with care and diligence.
Concerning the speech on dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of any of the joints from its place. Therefore it is prevented from its motion, and the shape of the member is deformed. And it causes the infirm to suffer pains and severe injuries.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration; and not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, on account of that, it ought not to be moved, nor extended at the time of its inflammation. Because it very often causes spasms and painful injuries.
But when that happens, then it is necessary that you hasten to the bloodletting of the patient. Then let it be left alone until the inflammation subsides.
Then let the limb be rubbed with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced easily.
And let every limb be treated with that whose remembrance will come in its place.
Indeed, I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even as preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated, except rarely.
And the dislocation of both happens according to one of two methods. For either they are removed from their places by a small removal, and are softened a little. Or they are dislocated by a whole, complete dislocation, until they are softened to the breast, until the saliva of the sick person flows, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
When, however, its dislocation is small, then it returns in most instances by itself with little [effort].
If, however, the dislocation is complete, whole: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not be delayed at all: and it is so that a helper holds the head of the sick person: and the physician inserts the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the jaw of the dislocation between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is on two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he may pull.
And instruct the patient to clean his jaws, and to loosen them for eating on every side. And the physician pulls the jaw, and pushes the jaw, until it returns to its place.
But if its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are affected at the same time: then administer a vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: nor should you delay the reduction of both completely, as we have said.
Therefore, when they return and are aligned, and the mouth of the patient is closed, and they are not softened: then place on them small pads of cloth, with cerate that is made from wax and rose oil.
Then it should be tied easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back: and let his head be supported between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right and left: nor let him try, or be forced to chew anything: rather, let his food be a gentle sipping, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is set: then let him eat what he wants, and let that be administered with ease.
Let him not strain his mouth during eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is set, and healed.
But if the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at some time, and are not returned to their places: then fevers often arise from this, and constant thirst: and perhaps the patient's bowels are loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore when you see that, then know that it is a loss, and very often he to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle, and the end of the shoulder.
Indeed, the clavicle is not dislocated from the inner side, because of its connection with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated outwards; and this is apparent to the senses.
And its restoration is that the patient should lie on his back, and extend his arms. Then firmly compress the place with your palm, for it will return itself.
Then place a plaster and compresses on it, and bind it.
However, the extremities which follow the elbow and are continuous with it, are not dislocated except rarely. But if it is dislocated on some day: then it is necessary that it be reduced and set according to what we have said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster, compresses, and a bandage on it. And instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the humerus is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of a dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated towards the armpit inferiorly. The second is that it is dislocated towards the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated posteriorly, because of the scapula. And it is not dislocated anteriorly, because of the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and exits inferiorly towards the armpit: especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it exits quickly in them, and enters quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely because it exits with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men through a blow, or a fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought because of that, that it is already dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you investigate it, until you stand upon its certainty, and then you proceed in its cure.
And a dislocation is known when it is towards the lower parts in relation to the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you find a manifest difference between them, and you find in the head of the humerus a concavity, and under the armpit on palpation the head of the humerus as if it were an egg: and the sick man cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all motions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then you find a manifest touch, nor is it hidden.
And indeed, the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is that a helper raises his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under his armpit, and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place: and the helper raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then let him place it lower, for indeed it returns quickly.
But if it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and a softening and soothing embrocation be applied, such as if the roots of mallow, fenugreek, and melilot are decocted in water, and applied.
Then the patient is laid on his back; and a ball and wool are placed under his armpit, which is equal between softness and firmness.
Then let the physician place his heel on the ball, and raise the head of the humerus forcefully, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is that you cause a man to be present who is taller than the patient, and stands on the side, and inserts his shoulder under the patient's armpit; and raises his armpit upwards, until the patient is suspended in the air. And another assistant pulls the patient's hand towards his lower abdomen. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long piece of wood is fixed in the ground, the head of which is round in shape, like the handle of a mortar: which is neither thick nor thin. Then let it be placed under the patient after soft cloths are placed on the head of the wood; and let the patient stand along the length of the wood.
Then his hand is extended downwards on the other side, and his body is extended on the other side with force; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But if its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you take a piece of wood whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the rectum is eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are tied over the round head, so that the wood does not injure the patient.
Then let it be placed under the patient's rectum, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wood downwards; and let the wood be tied over the support and arm and the end of the hand.
Then let the arm be placed on the step of a ladder across its width, and let the hand be extended downwards, and let the rest of the body be left hanging on the other side. For the joint enters immediately.
Therefore, when its entry is completed in whatever way is possible, then it is necessary that you place under the rectum a sphere of equal size made of wool. Then place the plaster prepared from mill dust with olibanum and egg white over the whole shoulder, as it is rolled over.
Then bind below the rectum with a suitable bandage; and let the bandage be rolled over the plaster from above; and let his hand be bound to his neck, and let it be left, nor let his hand be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the food of the patient be meager, until the limb is strengthened; for this is quicker for its healing.
Then let more be given after seven days, or five, and let it be exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and not softened, then it is now healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture which happens to it, or because of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three holes be applied to it, according to what was stated before in the chapter on cautery.
But if all this is done, and the bandage is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and constriction are applied over it several times, and it is not strengthened, and it falls, and he cannot raise it to the upper parts: then know that its nerves which are at the head of the shoulder are now cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The reduction, however, of a dislocation which occurs towards the breast and nipple, or towards the back, is done with pushing and extension with the hands: until it returns, and the remaining constriction and care are applied to it, until it is healed.
But if after healing heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then let the infirm person use a bath many times, until that heaviness is lessened, and it returns to its first nature.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty also: and it is indeed dislocated to all parts, and especially to the anterior, or to the posterior.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: because it is falling under sight, and under touch, according to whichever form it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is clearly shown to you. For the joint is concave, and he cannot bend the arm, nor touch his shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hasten, and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess happens to it.
For if a hot abscess happens to it, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it is not healed completely, especially when it is at the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocation, and of more vehement pain than them: and very often death comes with it.
And its restoration, when it is of those which it is possible to reduce, is that his hand be extended with two hands: and his arm be extended, and the physician's hands be above the elbow and under it, and he himself push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he doubles the hand with his hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
But if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer a vehement extension, very strong: and it is that two assistants extend the arm: and two assistants also hold him, so that he is not moved during the extension.
Then let the arm be revolved to every part after a doubled long cloth, or a broad ligament, is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil: so that it may be of those things which aid in the lubricity of the joint with ease. Then let him impel the joint with a vehement impulse, until it returns.
And after its reduction it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it, in which there is stypticity, and desiccation with egg white: and let it be bound with a decent bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the neck of the infirm one: and leave it for a few days.
Then let it be unbound. If therefore the joint is strengthened in its place, then unbind the ligature from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not well bound, then repeat the plaster and the bandage, and leave it for several days, until it is bound. Then unbind it.
But if heaviness occurs to it after reduction of the joint, and the disposition is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place on the joint the fat of a ram, that is, the rump of a ram. Then let it be bound, and leave it on it day and night. Then remove it, and put it into the bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat on it the application of ram's fat once, and a second, and a third time with the bath's entry, until it is softened.
And if you wish, place on it cow dung, moist, warmed with butter, and bind it on.
Do this several times. For it is thus softened, and returns to its proper condition.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation, Almahassen.
The hand is very often dislocated by Almahassen, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Contrary to the other joints. However, it is necessary that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation at the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place is inflamed: or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place the weak hand of Almahassen on a board, and the attendant extends his hand: and the physician places his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns.
However, it is necessary that it be considered whether the palm has already receded to the belly of the hand: then let the infirm person place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be intrinsically on the board, that is, the inner part of his hand be placed on the board, so that the physician's hand falls upon the prominence of the joint itself.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a plaster soothing the abscess: and leave it, nor repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you achieve its reduction after days have passed, but nevertheless the joint remains according to its crookedness: and it does not harm the infirm person in any way, except that the hand is softened, and cannot grasp anything: then know that the nerves are severed, or abraded.
Therefore there is no remedy for it, except that it be bound with a cautery near its case. And perhaps that does not contribute anything.
Therefore, when Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster which we have described. Then let it be bound, and left for five days; afterwards let it be unbound and left, and the hand exercised.
But if its movement is deprived, and something serious happens to it: then soften it with warm water, and friction several times, until it softens.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or a finger to the back of the palm, or its belly: then extend the finger, and push back the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straight on that day.
When it is night, bind it according to the same account: and do not cease to unbind it during the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days, until it is tightened.
And similarly do with it, if it is dislocated towards the belly of the hand: and bind it to that part, and perform your first operation on it, until it is healed.
And similarly do with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck happens to someone, or many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure for it, because death quickly approaches the sick person.
And a sign of that is that the excrement of the sick person goes out without will, nor can he hold it back: and very often some of his limbs are weakened from it, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them: or one vertebra is removed from its place.
For very often it is removed, which very often makes the removal of them to four parts.
That which is removed to the rear is called a hump.
The cure however of it is that you consider then, if the hump happened from childhood: then there is no cure for it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
But in that which happens from a fall, or blow, or similar things, the ancients have already spoken of methods of cure at great length. Of which very little returns with profit.
I however have now shortened it from that, of which little excuses much of it from my proximity to the intention: and my exposition by which I have expounded it, and formed its instrument in a different way from that by which they showed it, and expounded it.
I say therefore that in a hump which happens from the front part, there is no skill, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which is on both sides also.
And it is not cured from them, except that which happens in the back properly, with that which I relate: and it is, that the infirm person be extended upon his face upon a level surface near a wall: and a wet mat be spread under him, so that his chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be placed fixed in a hole in the ground, at the head of the surface: and another piece of wood near his feet at the other end of the surface: and let an assistant hold the wood, and let it not be fixed in a hole. And let another assistant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the sick person, and under his armpits, a soft, firm ligature: and extend the end of the ligature to the wood which is at his head: and tie it to it.
Then tighten another ligature above his hips, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the ligatures, and tie them to the other wood which is at his feet.
Then let each assistant extend a part of the ligature to the wood, and let the two woods not be removed from their places in which they are fixed; but nevertheless let them not be fixed, as we have said.
And the physician shall place his palms upon the vertebrae with strength, until it returns; or let him place a board upon it. Then let him attach his feet to the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits. And hollow out in the wall, of which we have spoken, which is near the patient, a place into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board upon the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet upon the other end. And let him pull strongly until the vertebra is constricted, and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, work with a lathe which is turned with your hand: and it is, that you fix in the ground near the head of the patient at the end of the bed two pieces of wood, whose length is that of one arm: and near his head at the end of the bed two pieces of wood.
And let the length which is between each piece of wood be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place, in which the lathe runs: and let every piece of wood be firmly fixed in the ground, lest it be moved at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted: and it is a lathe in which the rope revolves in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity is a hole, in which is fixed a piece of wood, whose length is a palm together with that which revolves: and in two other pieces of wood there is similarly that.
Then tighten the bandages which you have tightened on the chest of the patient with the lathe which is near his head: and the bandages which you have tightened on his legs with the lathe which is near his feet.
Then let a servant stand by each lathe, who turns his hand with the rope, with which the lathe revolves: and let the physician adjust the vertebra, as we have said.
This is the form of the lathe, which follows.
Then after the vertebra returns and the place is even, then it is necessary that you place a drying plaster with egg white. Then lint. Afterwards, place on top of the plaster a small plate of wood, whose width is three fingers, or thereabouts. And its length a quantity which takes in the place of the hump, and above the part of the healthy vertebra.
Then let it be bound with the bandage which we have described. And let the patient use a light diet until he is healed.
But if something of a prominence remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the application of the plate which we have described for a long time. And now a lead plate is applied to it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the last vertebra of the back; and a dislocation is thought to be caused by it, and it is that bone which now protrudes. Therefore, a presumption should not be made about it with this medicine; for perhaps it will cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that in the hip joint and the shoulder joint, only dislocation occurs; nor does what happens to the other joints from a small displacement and concavity happen to them.
And the hip joint indeed is dislocated in four ways. That is, because it is dislocated anteriorly and posteriorly.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards; and it is slightly dislocated anteriorly and posteriorly.
And the treatment of its inward dislocation is: because when you join the injured leg with the healthy one, it is longer; and its knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the injured person cannot bend his foot at the groin; and the place which follows the groin is inflamed with a hot inflammation, because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of him to whom outward dislocation happens is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of him to whom anterior dislocation happens is that he extends the healthy leg completely; however, he does not bend it without pain in the knee; and if he tries to walk, he cannot step forward on it; and his urine is retained, and his groin is inflamed, and when walking he treads on his heel.
And the sign of him to whom posterior dislocation happens is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction however of this kind of dislocation is such that you consider then, if the dislocation is old, which is already long-standing in the one having it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its disposition: then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, a complete presumption should not be made for it.
But in him whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four kinds of dislocation: then hasten to this, that you move the joint, and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left: for perhaps it returns, and does not need other care.
But if it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and he extend his leg from below: either with his hands, or with a ligature, which is tied above his leg above the knee: and another assistant extend his arm from above, so that he puts his hands under his armpits. Then let him bind with a soft ligature above the root of the hip, and hold the end of the other ligature.
And let its extension be either from the anterior part of the groin to the part of the clavicle, or from the posterior to the part of the back.
And let the extension of them all be at once, until the sick man is raised with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common kind of the four kinds.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for every kind that which I shall say of proper cure.
In its proper reduction, when the dislocation is towards the inner parts, it is necessary that the sick man be laid on his sound side. Then let him place a bandage above the root of the hip, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the clavicle.
Then let another strong assistant take his arms, and lessen the thickened place of the diseased hip, and extend it outwards with a strong extension. Now indeed it returns to its place.
And this method is easier than the other methods of cure, by which this limb is reduced.
But if he rebels against you, and does not obey you in entering upon this method of cure at all: then it is necessary that the two feet of the invalid be bound together with a strong, soft bandage, above the two heels, and above the two knees: and let the length of each from its fellow be the quantity of four fingers: and let the diseased leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the patient be suspended from the head of a beam which is in the house: and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then order a strong youth to support the head of the hip; and let another youth be suspended with the invalid: and let another youth push, supporting the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in its proper reduction, when the dislocation is outwards, it is necessary that the patient lie upon a bench, according to what we have narrated in the case of him who has a hump. And let a bandage be drawn tight above his leg, the diseased one properly, and above his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let a piece of wood be added in the middle of the bench, firmly fixed: upon which let wet cloths be wrapped, so that it may not injure the patient: and let the wood be between his hips, so that he may not be drawn downwards during extension.
Then let each assistant extend to his part, and let the physician equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to repositioning, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the board, and press it, as we said in the case of a hump, evenly. However, it is necessary that the patient lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the leg of the injured person be extended quickly: and it is according to this same account on the board: and let the physician place the palm of his right hand upon the groin of the injured person. Then he constricts it with the other hand, and with that constriction he puts it extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the rear: then it is not necessary that the injured person be extended downwards, and be raised above the ground: rather it is necessary that he be placed upon a hard thing, just as it is necessary that it be done even if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his lying upon two hips: and it is upon his face, and the ligaments extended: according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also upon the place to which the joint goes, when the reduction is completed as is necessary.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the injured person: and when you see them equal, and the injured person draws his leg, and extends it without difficulty: then you know that the limb is now reduced, as is necessary: then join the two hips, and apply a plaster, and bind it with a bandage, that is, a roller: so that the hip does not move to any side: and let the injured person adhere to rest for three days, or four.
Then loosen the bandage and plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in position, then know that the dislocation is now fixed: therefore allow the injured person to walk.
And if you see any softening: then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make him delay in walking on both for some days, until it is well strengthened.
Concerning the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is to say backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply his leg to his hip. If, however, it does not adhere to the hip, then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit upright, so that he extends his leg if he can upon it, and you place your back against his face; and put his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers above his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another assistant should extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres with the hip gently, not laboriously.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then unbind them.
And let the patient not use walking for some days, until it is healed.
But if the reduction is refused by you, then administer strong extension with the bandages, the description of which preceded in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocation of the heel bone.
The heel bone is sometimes removed by a small removal, and sometimes dislocated according to the whole. And its dislocation happens, either inwards, or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel bone swollen, protruding towards the part to which it is dislocated.
The treatment, however, of its removal, is easy restoration: and it is that it is extended easily with the hands, and is set, until it returns.
In the treatment, however, when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary that you make the sick person sit upright; and let a strong assistant hold him firmly from the posterior part of his back, in the middle of it.
Then hold you with your right hand his foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the place of the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left [hand] twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have related.
Then push the front of the foot to the leg a third time, and you pull the splint.
If therefore it returns in one or two times according to this method, and you see the foot set, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it; because it returns.
But if the reduction of it is prevented by what we have related, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and fix a peg firmly in the ground so that it falls between his hips; and you have already wrapped a cloth around it, so that it does not injure the patient.
Then let an assistant take his hip; afterwards let another assistant extend the foot, either with his hands or with a ligature which he ties above the foot. Then let each assistant extend against the extension of his fellow; and let the peg stand between the patient's two hips, retaining him so that his body is not drawn downwards during the extension.
Then let the physician set the dislocation with his hands, and let another assistant hold the sound leg downwards. For the dislocation returns quickly.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and the soundness of its return is declared to you, then apply a plaster and lint, and bind it firmly with ligatures, and bind the foot with a ligature to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or be afraid of the nerves which are above the heel posteriorly, lest the ligature be too tight upon them and injure them.
Then leave it for two or three days. If the ligature loosens, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he attempts to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and corrupted again, and that it will not then receive a cure.
But if a hot abscess occurs to him, then it is necessary that you administer to its alleviation that which we have previously described elsewhere in the treatment, and an embrocation until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes, and its cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated from them be set with slight extension without effort; and this indeed is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the dorsum of the foot, then it is necessary that the infirm person sit with his foot on a level place on the ground, or on a board; and he is standing as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then tread on it with your foot with force, until it returns, and you see it now set, and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place under the belly of his foot a board which holds the whole foot, to which there are two heads. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then unbind it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is tightened and secured by reduction.
Concerning the types of dislocation with a wound or fracture.
When something of that sort happens, and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often follows: and therefore one ought not to presume to treat such a thing unless one is skilled in the art through long experience, merciful, pious, cautious, not rash, nor presumptuous; and to use, in the beginning, only medicines that soothe hot abscesses; and to leave the outcome to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for, salvation from destruction with a mildness of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning, before a hot abscess occurs.
If then the member returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that soothes the abscess: and treat the wound with what is suitable from drying ointments.
But if the dislocation is with a fracture, and splinters occur in the bone, then attempt their removal, and do in this what we have said in simple illnesses from what preceded in their locations.
And be cautious according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear: according to what my previous instruction to you was.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and safer for your body with those in your time.
